THE RED PAGE.
CONSIDERATIONS
Struck by the fact that there is no good edition
of “Cook’s Voyages” sold currently, Lond. Outlook
enquires ‘ ‘ Is there any special demand from Australia
and New Zealand for Captain Cook’s records ? ” There is
not. A small number of cheap abridgments sell annually
for school prizes, and if the three voyages could be printed
for the price of the first voyage in Wharton’s edition, to
retail at about 10s., there would be a limited demand—say
500 copies to begin with and 100 copies a year thereafter, if
the book got reasonable push and advertisement. But,
generally speaking, Australians have no taste for ancient
history : they want the novel note, the momentary im-
pression. Much can be said for the attitude. “ The
savage turning in his tomb ” of each of us demands to be
considered critically. It was reasonable that in his day
he should praise past time, seeing that he had no com-
prehension of his place in time coming. But we
can follow his bent of ancestor-worship too far. After
all, what have deeds that won the empire to do with
us? What fun or satisfaction, what fuller, keener
life, are we going to get out of them ? They have been
written off to last century’s profit and loss account, and
concern to-day’s business no more. “ But if they had not
been, you would not be.” Fallacious “if” ! They were:
we are. A living dog is better than a dead lion,” says
Hebrew wisdom. “ Carpe diem ! ” says Latin wisdom.
“ Eyes front [--forward, march!” says Australian wisdom.
This is not a diatribe against knowledge.
Knowledge is an excellent thing, to be held in reserve in
the libraries. But most of the knowledge that has been
accumulated is useless (o you personally. Too many
facts—or lies—have been accumulated about too many
people to be overtaken by any one man’s acquirement.
Since the damnable (?) invention of printing “ the mass
grows more and more of volumes yet to read, of secrets
still to explore.” Why not admit frankly that most are
not worth reading or exploring,?- for us. “II faut
cultiver son jardin,” says last week’s bridegroom. But
accumulation is not cultivation. “ Faut une bonne a
tout faire,” says last year’s. You want a just
impression of past history and present relations,
and details don’t matter if your mind is a fit
engine of acquisition. Then, if you need knowledge,
you can absorb it at any time as a barrister absorbs his
brief. Learn, as he does, general principles ; and let the
particular application depend upon the minute’s fee.
What modernity implies is not a brain full of knowledge,
but one full of indices to knowledge. A dull mind and a
memory laden with accurate information ahout Captain
Cook are not to be preferred to a fresh mind that knows
merely where to find accurate information about Captain
Cook—when wanted. Most of us will never want it. It
takes us all our time to build a house and plant a tree
and father a son : the three things that ancestral wisdom
recommended in posterity-worship.
&
These are the things, it appears, which
Spencer regretted he had no share in. Mommsen
managed to combine literary creation with a baker’s
dozen of a family ; but Spencer, farther removed from
the primitive friends of Tacitus, deliberately rejected
marriage in favour of fame. He has been represented as
doubting the wisdom of the choice.. .when he reflected
how slowly his books sold. Those books illus-
trate the argument: they were useful in their
day, but now that we have absorbed the cen-
tral idea their laborious proof is negligible. Upon the
stream of science Spencer found a rock and built a light-
house for mariners of his generation. He took the dream
that “ all things the world which fill, Of but one stuff are
spun,” and made it real. He gave the argument of evolu-
tion a universal scope. But the tide of dream and argu-
ment has swept past him. The rays of his lamp still light
the course, but we no longer need to know how he built
the lighthouse. It is sufficient to accept his illumination:
the argument remains for those who have time to study
it. Study will not repay many of us; we must live by
ideas, not by volumes. And Spencer’s idea is unshake-
able, for the light shines.
A By route Australian.
ARTHUR LYNCH, released from prison,
might think it well to return to his native
land. If the stipulation that he is not to
enter Parliament refers to Britain only,
he could find a useful career in Commonwealth
polities. When he left Australia in pursuit of
his ideals national sentiment had not'crystallised
in enduring form. It would be odd, yet natural,
if the Grail sought for in many lands had been
waiting him at home. “ Dreamer,” “ tete
mtintee,” “champion of lost causes” he is de-
scribed ; and his chivalrous sincerity would make
him a valuable force in Federal polities—
not, so far, notorious for sincerity or chivalry.
His talents do not seem remarkable ; but none
can deny him what is rarer and loftier than talent
—the heroic soul. While the rest of us sat smug
and cowardly, however protesting, to watch the
outrage against human right and human liberty
committed by Britain in Africa, he risked Iris life
and the sacrifice of all save honour in defence of
the idea of justice. “Mon idee, ma vie,” is the
motto of his earlier books. Whatever his dis-
illusionment, his manly acts have realised his
boyish dreams.
Why, then, some of his friends have asked, did
Australians make no endeavour to secure his
freedom ? Because he was beaten by greater
force, and there remained only the appeal for
Caesar’s clemency which (one imagines) was the
worst humiliation he would have suffered. He was
defeated ; he paid the penalty of defeat; and it
was reasonable, however wrong, that he should
pay. No State can permit its citizens to take up
hostile arms, whatever the ethical or personal
justification. And the plea that Lynch was not a
citizen of Britain would not bear argument.
Besides, it was known or guessed that his
imprisonment would be comparatively short. The
Government which imprisoned him cannot but
respect him. An apostle of freedom, he can
count, like Paul, a merit in his chains.
&
Born at Srnythesdale, Victoria, in 1861, Arthur
Lynch is in Ins forty-third. year. He was
educated at Ballarat and Melb. University,
whence he took the degrees of M.A. and C.E.
(his father also a civil engineer). A good student
in many ways, he seems to have been by nature
and inclination too versatile to be exceptionally
distinguished. His aim was complete culture of
body and mind. He wrote verses, shone in
mathematics, cultivated modern literature and
ancient philosophy, and was especially proud of
his feats on the running-track. Leaving the Uni-
versity, he set upas civil engineer, grew impatient
of results and took to lecturing ; then felt the
wander-lust (stimulated possibly by the exploits
of his fellow-student, George Morrison) and, after
a tour of Maori land, went as student to Berlin.
His Irish affinities led him thence to an unsuc-
cessful candidature for the Galway seat which he
gained in 1901. He seems to have got in touch
with Irish Nationalist agents in Paris; but he
could not come to terms with Parnell, and after a
fierce contest he was beaten by some fifty votes.
Followed ten years of journalism, authorship,
and political agitation in London and Paris. Lynch
spent some time on the staff of T. P. O’Connor’s
Sun, which sent him as war correspondent with
the Ashanti expedition of 1895. But he had for-
gotten to get a War-Office permit, and was not
allowed to land—his ■ first piece of ill-fortune
from Africa. Yet as a journalist he was
uncommonly successful. His equipment of
brains, languages, and energy was excep-
tional ; and by 1898 he was representative of
The Daily Mail in Paris and special correspon-
dent for Le Journal. But more and more he
tended to action. A trip to America had asso-
ciated him with the “physical force” Irish
liberationists, and it is said that he was docketed
“dangerous” at Scotland Yard. The African
war gave him the chance for which he had been
pining ; and, since his enthusiasms marked him as
easy prey for baser men, he was lucky to make his
coup in a cause so honourable.
Lynch’s literary capacity is that of a clever
journalist. His two books of prose criticism are
interesting keen, pregnant, yet not highly
illumined or finely expressed, and leaving a
memory of occasional acumen rather than of full
judgment. His verse model is usually Bvron,
though his work contains very literal echoes from
Shakespear, Keats, and many another. Indeed, in
his joy in physical exercise, in his poetic ebul-
lition, and in his “ championship of lost causes,”
Lynch may be fairly compared with Byron.
There is no comparison of talents. Lynch’s
verses are essentially commonplace, though un-
usual ; fluent, energetic, very personal, and not
seldom bad in form. Some of the stanzas of A
Koran of Love have singular applicability to
Lynch’s career—
I’d envy then a death for some ideal—
-1 ’ll take no type but keep my proper thought —
And after that a great hope large and leal,
To sene a land oppressed and over-wrought
In griefs, unconquered yet, where yet shall peal
The hymns of freedom, with our life blood fraught :
And then ? Why simply then, whate’er my fate,
With stoic pride, serene and calm to wait. ..
For what is life? Life *s but a worthless toy,
If not the soul within its truest bent
Strain towards some glorious accomplishment...
I turned to look upon my scanty life,
A soldier pressed by numbers did 1 teem,
And brought to bay and wounded in the strife.
Yet buoyed in hope, and dauntless still, I deem,
While high o’er all shrieked thinly Fame’s wild fife ,—
Strange when the substance fades, we hold the dream !
No, no ! the voice that hies us on to glory
Is riot a dream, but life’s own inmost story. ..
A nd at the roll of Alexander’s name
My very flesh did pant and burn for fame...
Once more I look, and Cortes’ noble form
Stands in the lustrous centre of my eye,
My darling hero, who doth best inform
My soul with flashes of nobility...
This, though Spain in Mexico did as Britain in
Africa!
Then who am I ? Even from my inmost heart
Some dim-known Faith hath ever led me on,
Besought me by the fame of great ones gone
To strike a signal fire ere 1 depart. . .
Misfortune, ever better nearer known,
O, stoic teacher t Thou art true to me ;
Ambitions, hopes, and joys have lightly flown,
M comrades noiv are only Pride and thee ...
J*
One cannot find anywhere a reference to
Australia. A later volume, Religio Athletoc, is
devoted to the praise of athletics as the foundation
of manly rength and beauty. Some dogmas and
opinions
I love that fine brave atmosphere of the athlete, the
fresh and lusty strength, the deep repose...l want serenity;
and with it strength always...
With i man, handsome, strong and graceful, and
touchedwith the spirit of prestige; and with a woman,
beautiful, polished, and accomplished in all the arts of
beauty whose glance and whose touch are ever like a
gage to chivalrous deeds, the laws of morality are bounded
by mutual pleasure 0n1y...
I submit that the truest poetry has a certain tone of
simplicity and gains its witchery, one scarcely knows
whence, not from artifice, but from the fineness and
depth of the soul from whence it issues...
Not many beautiful women are seen to the greatest
advantage in the nude...
We must look into a man’s eye as well as upon his con-
formation before we can gauge his value as an athlete
I hardly believe that life has sensations more great
than this—on a genial summer day to come to the scratch
stripped for the contest, and determined that win or
lose your best shall be done, thence to fling yourself in
the race with your rival young ephebes, and having felt
the severity of the struggle in every nerve and limb to
dash first past the tape. What joy when, in the fast
returning crises that bring you hack to strength and
perfect health again, you realize that you have set your
fame once and for all on the records, and that the sweet
and supple limbs that you rejoice in, shining in their
bloom in the sunlight, may be looked upon by all as the
flesh of a hero...
Truly, the succession of Beauty is not limited, and we
in our generation are privileged to look upon more
glorious types of womanhood than the world has seen
before. So I now believe [at thirty-three]; at seventeen
I was sure of it. My blood was hot, and boundless
desires of every kind were fretting at my heart. The
world was represented to my mind as an arena in which
the picture of the youth, straining every nerve to reach
the goal and win the crown of laurel, was no unfitting
image of the main purpose of life. A life of incessant
effort appealed to my fibres, and pain and torment were
but the testing of the Athlete’s mettle. And the deep
emotions, the passions that seemed to gnaw and burn
upon the very vitals of the body—it was delicious then
to feel their spur, perpetually...
Virtue is not summed up by mere abstentions and
circumspect meanness. There is no virtue that is not to
be measured by accomplishment, by vigour, energy,
conquest, by magnificence, gloire...
In an earlier volume, Our Poets, Lynch avenged
himself for the reception that his literary essays
met in London. All the log-rolling cliques would
have none of him ; The Saturday Review, in parti-
cular, made him a laughing-stock ; and, generally
speaking, he found no one to take him seriously.
His books, apparently published at his own ex-
pense, were left on his hands ; and some of them
had to be disposed of as “ remainders” at a- loss.
So, with a memory of English Bards, he penned a
seriea of assaults on the gods of the moment M 291)
—Wilde, Le Gallienne, Swinburne, Lang, Killing
and others. The jibes are too unmeasured to be
really severe, but some of them are written
shrewdly. Of Andrew Lang—
His mind diffuses some illumination
To every theme he merely glances on,
And yet with all this fine appreciation
The primal impulse to create is gone.
Austin Dobson gets this —
’T is sweet to sit beneath the leafy shade
When still not dark—just after dinner hour,
And feel, while yet the sky’s with crimson ray’d
The soft-bloomed quietude’s digestive power.
And then, ere yet a single throb of blood
Should rebel prove to that fine influence,
Rise, dust your knees, smile amiably, be good,
In dear, wise, gentle, meek, mild innocence.
To Oscar Wilde, Lynch was by temperament and
training instinctively opposed, and there is
curious prescience in his summary. “Fewthings
in literature are so saddening as the spectacle of
Oscar Wilde’s career,” lie writes. Wilde’s little
finger, of course, is artistically stronger than
Lynch’s loins; but the younger man, sane and
hale, breathed with disgust the atmosphere
of decadence. “In the exquisite little study,
entitled, ‘ Pen, Pencil, and Poison,’ the word Sin
occurs incessantly. It is tlie key-note, always
struck in with such fine intensity that its delicate
tintinnabulation is heard at last ringing through
the entire theme. And soon.
Lynch is always full of himself; and it is as
the self-study of a cbnrageous and'exuheiant per-
sonality, rather than as literature, that his books
attract a reader.

In the admirable Winter Number of The Studio
R. de la Sizerannr nomraents upon Turner’s pine-trees—
-11 repre’sented as much slimmer than they are. in reality,
much more geometrical, devoid of branches, and spread-
ing far too high for human heads the ironical protection

Why? Because of his intuition of beauty. Turner was

One likes the excellent British person who talks
to The Nineteenth Century about European spas. “ Some
of the springs are stronger than other.-, but. having drunk
most of them, I must record uiy conviction that there is
no difference among them, whatever the doctors may say,
and that, if one took IS grains of hie rbonate of soda and
25 grains of sulphate of soda and di-solved them in a pint
of lukewarm water, they would be just as good as any of
the waters of Carlsbad, while, if one added 10 grains of
citrate of lithia to the mixture, it would be better in the
case of gout than any water in Europe.”
G. H. Lorimer’s new “Graham letters’' run
along briskly. At latest he was talking of letters of intro-
duction. “ Right here I want to caution you against
giving away your signature to every Clarence and Willie
that happens along. When your name is on a note it
stands only for money, but when it’s on a letter of intro-
duction or recommendation it stands for your judgment
of ability and character, and you can’t call it in at the
end of thirty days, either. Giving a letter of introduc-
tion is simply lending yopr name with a man as collateral,
and if he’s no good you can’t have the satisfaction of
redeeming your indorsement even ; and you’re both dis-
credited. The first thing that a young merchant
must learn is that his brand must never appear
on a note, or a ham, or a man that isn’t good ..
It’s a mighty curious thing, belt a lot of men who
have no claim on you, and who would n’t think of asking
for money, will pan-handle both sides of a street for
favours that mean more thanmoney. O' course, it’s the
easy thing and the pleasant thing not to refuse, and after
all, most men think, it does n’t cost any thing hut a few
strokes of the pen, and so they will give a fellow that
they would n’t ordinarily play, on their-friends as a prac-
tical joke a nice sloppy letter qf ijvtrqctuetion to them ;
or hand out to a man that they wduld n’t give away as a
booby prize a letter of recommendation in which they
crack him up as having all the qualities necessary for an
A 1 Sunday-school superintendent and bank president.”

berlain is a confirmed smoker .yes,; “he does not take

endure longer evei) than Brass.
Max Beerbohm on a question debated ... by
people who don’t know that the criterion of a piece of
art is simply whether it is well or badly done; “We
demand of the playwright who deals with ugly things,
just as we demand of the playwright who deals with
pretty things, something more -than the sight of his sub-
ject matter. Mere gall is no better than mere sugar. It
is worse. Mere sugar is not disgusting. Nor is gall dis-
gusting if it be rightly prepared: In other words, horrible
subject-matter ceases to be horrible when it is treated by
a fine artist. The subject-matter of a tragedy is, in itself,
necessarily horrible. Some tragedies are horrible.
These are the bad tragedies, ungraced by any beauty
or nobility of treatment, or ungraced by an idea, and so
meaning nothing, leading nowhither, merely affronting
us with their own horrors. pain or pleasure
depends not at all on the artist’s material: it does
depend, entirely, on the artist.” Max contradicts him-
self, of course ; but the last sentence is doctrine.
“Evil to him who evil drinks,” quotes Life
TVie reproductions of Mrs. Allingham’s pictures in Happy
England, published by A. & C. Black, should be seen by-
people interested in colour printing. They represent
about the best three-cdldur process work yet done in
England. If they fail artistically ,it is because Mrs. A. is
too literal a copy st Hodder <& Stoughton, London's
demi-Scotch, semi-religious publishing firm, issue Max
Pemberton’s last story, Dr. Xavier. That is one bad
mark for Max, and the story is another.
We shall have to modify that curse against the
current American novel. It really applies only to vapid
stuff like D. G. Phillips’s Golden Fleece— froth that is n’t
even iridescent. But Chambers’s Maids of Paradise
(praised already), John Fox’s The Little Shepherd of King-
dom Come, and Frederick Palmer’s The Vagabond are
in a good second-rate class. The first few chap-
ters of The Little Shepherd are enthralling: fresh,
sincere, strong, with the Lorna Doone feeling of
Man and Nature in them. The whole book is
readable. The Vagabond has less original quality, but it
is a romance that is a rpmance. When American senti-
ment drifts in, toward the end of these books-, the
authors descend from their plane. American sentiment
excludes passion or the representation of passion ; and
the make-believe is n’t convincing or satisfactory. Still,
as make-believe, Fox and Palmer handle their matter
more featly than most.
“He was a Scotchman, and Scotchmen have
always seemed to feel a pained solicitude about the
English speech,” writes Prof. Lounsbury of James Beattie
in Dec. Hamer's. Lounsbury is discussing “ corrupt
English,” and the zealots who would purify it. But
Beattie’s zeal is easily understood. Foreigners who learn
a language painfully seem frequently to resent the fluent
usage of the native-born. Possibly it appears to them
unfair that, after they have devoted time and pains to
mastering the letter, the spirit should be preferred, by
wilful people who “ ought to know better.” There is a
well-meaning German musician down, in Maoriland who
edits a little monthly called The Triad. In every issue
he devotes space to an elaborate criticism of the lin-
guistic blunders made by local singers and writers. Often
he does useful service in his drill-sergeant’s way ; often
his commentary is more faulty than the other fellow's
text. But his “ pained solicitude,” like Beattie's, is the
thing that strikes the philosophic eye.
Some local music-lovers may like-to buy the new.
Lond. edition of Gilbert and Sullivan’s operas in 12
volumes at 3 1. 15s. for subscribers.
The union between W. T. Stead, as proprietor,
and W. H. Fitehett, as editor, of The Review of Reviews
for Australasia, was possibly due to the sympathy
between two Nonconformist consciences. The division
is due to the fact that Stead remains among the company
of anti-Jingoes, while Fitehett insists that Rhodes, Jews,,
and Chinese labour in Africa are all A.M.D.G. and the
Empire. One of the young Steiids came out from Eng-
land recently to find that Fitehett,. with the Review staff,,
was about to publish a new inaga. from the Review
' offices ; and that it was rumoured the Revied > was to
cease Australian publication. This was business ; and
the new niaga. is now to hand — Life. It isn’t attrac-
tive. Why? Well, it lacks individuality; the matter
is cheap and looks cheap; and the type is too small
(Et tu, doesn’t apply, for cases alter circumstances).
Fitehett used to have an uncommon knack of neat para-
graph work : he could say something new, even at
the month-end, and he could say it tersely, with exactly
the right balance of thought and phrase. But for the
last year or more he has been writing smooth common-
place : apparently he has been writing,too much: any-
way, there has been no value in him. For the rest, Life
has too many 1 ill-compounded scraps : a lesser quantity
better written would have left a greater impression.
T. A. Browne’s account of how he wrote Robbery Wilder
Arms is familiar : he should have told us how lie wrote
A Modern Buccaneer. George Reid, informing about his
speeches, is interesting ; and Lorimer’s mew matter is as
good as the old. Generally, there is too much economy
and too much Fitehett—which amount to the same
thing. However, the way to improvement is wide open.

“ Reader ” : I£ a bookseller asks you 7s. for Our
New Selection —published at 6s.—write for it direct to this
office. By ordering 12 copies, a bookseller gets the
6s. book tor 4s. s d. If he orders 50 copies, he gets it
for 4s. net. That is to say, he gets one-third of the price ;
and author'and publisher, who divide the other two-
thirds, have to pay for printing and illustrating and
binding the book. Two shillings profit (less freight) on a
6s. book is enough for any reasonable trader. Three
shillings is monstrous “ Myrtle ’’ asks why old Dad never
planted a fruit-tree on “ our selection ” 't. J7J ” com-
plains of the illustrations to Our New Selection. “ "Who-
ever saw an Australian cowyard like that shown with
Dave and Joe milking? No bails ; no leg ropes ; no yam,
in fact,—for they might as well be sitting down in the
middle of the ‘ Old Man Plain,’ for all the difference it
makes to the cows.” Caber writes : The Australian
Star (Sydney), in criticising Our New Selection, wants to
know the date upon which a frost destroyed the wheat
crop, and the latitude and longitude of the trouble. It is
evident that the man who knows anything about farming
has not started writing for the Star yet. At only one
stage of its growth will frost destroy wheat —w'hen in the
“ milk.” This occurred (to. give one instance) at the
latter end of October, 1900, on the plateau of the Central
(Darling) Downs, when thousands of bushels of wheat
were lost—GreenpioUnt (“ Rudd’s ” district) being one of
the stricken localities L.F.: Amy Levy's A Minor Poet

don Plane-Tree, out of print, would cost you possibly 10s.
Any intelligent bookseller will procure. YV.A.B.’s “The
Dead March of the Waters ” was republished from The
Bulletin by Melville, Mullen, and Slade, Melbourne, in

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The River Bells.
So loud in the morning light they ring
They set the stars a-quiver.
Whore the rapids clatter they sway and swing,
And I trow each bell has a golden string
Which the fairies tug for ever.
In the flush,
In the hush
Of the dawn they ring—
“ Dong ! Dong ! Ding !”
Down in the laughing river.
And they chime so slow in the drowsy noon
When the river grasses quiver,
When the lilies droop in the glare, and swoon,
And the willows sway to an ocean tune,
That the soft sea-winds deliver.
They swing,
And they ring
In the stifling noon—
“ Dong ! Dong ! Ding ! ”
Deep in the lazy river.
And they toll so softly at eventide,
When the nodding rushes shiver,
And the ripples gleam in the golden pride
Of a sun that marches through star-lands wide,
To the Land of the Great Light-Giver.
They roll,
And they toll
In the eventide—
“ Dong ! Dong ! Ding !"
Sweet in the rushing river.
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
Yester-Even.
If I told you, maiden fair,
When I met you yester-night—
Flowers and berries in your hair
Rippling in a wondrous snare,
Clinging to your forehead white—
If I told you I had altered
Would my lying lips have faltered ?
Would you think me over-bold —
Standing close beside you there —
If I shore one curl of gold
As they did in days of old
For a keepsake I might wear ?
Would you think me wild and reckless
If I made my arms your necklace ?
If I wooed you, little maid,
With the grace of old-time wooers—
When we stood within the shade—
Would you shrink from me afraid
If my lips were pressed to yours
And my passioned kisses burned them ?
Tell me, would you have returned them ?
When you laid your dainty hand
On my lips to keep them mute.
Did you, could you understand
How I plotted, schemed and planned
That your lips might follow suit ?
Had I won them with my scheming,
They were mine for fevered dreaming.
If I whispered in your ear,
When your tresses swept my cheek
In the moonlight, soft and clear,
Whispered words you should not hear,
Words that I should never speak—
If I told you that I loved you,
Would my words have thrilled or moved you?
If they had, oh, little girl,
And your face grew wondrous wise
’Neath its brow of gleaming pearl ;
If I, fondling back each curl,
Read the love-light in your eyes,
I would barter hopes of heaven
For another yester-even.
vio.
C. CRAWFORD.
If I Have Been a Failure.
If I have thrown the sword down,
If I have quit the fray,
Remember, ah, remember,
Man will not fight alway.
And if I’ve left the roadway
That leads aloft to fame—
If I have been a failure,
At least I feel no shame.
If I have chosen by-ways,
The little paths of green,
Remember, ah, I pray you,
What men you might have been
If you had left the high-road,
The hard road winding on—
Remember, ere you blame me,
What joys you might have won.
If you had followed my way,
The little tracks of Love,
The cold winds had not numbed you
On lofty plains above.
The pleasures of the by-paths
Are sweeter far than fame —
When hearts are dead and bloodless
There’s naught in gauds and name.
The stars have shone on my paths,
The sun hath cherished.me,
Soft arms have stretched in welcome
Beside the shining sea.
Red lips have been my mile-posts,
Sweet eyes have been my stars,
While ye have climbed, a-weary,
To Fame’s gray prison-bars!
The winds have beaten on you
While I have lain at rest,
While I have laid my forehead
On Love’s head golden-tressed.
The stony ways have hurt you,
Your hearts have yearned fcr fame;
If I have been a failuie,
At least ’tis but in name.
For you have tasted sadness,
The failure of success,
The barrenness of title,
Fame’s bitter emptiness;
While I have supped and revelled,
While I have played Love’s game—
If I have been a failure,
At least I feel no shame.
VIC.

Long and Weary Suffering.
Rheumatic Fever and a Torpid Liver.
An Eight Years Permanent Cure.
The CftB6 of Mre. H. R. LAWLER.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER).
Every scientific discovery means the
introduction of some commodity for the
purpose of rendering » service to the
multitude of its users, and as the public’s
ready appreciation of everything possessing
indisputable merit is observable on all sides,
as will be noticed by the remarks of Mrs.
Hester Rose Lawler, of No. 4 Broadstairs-
street, Balmain, it is considered necessary
to place the following narrative before the
general reader.
Mrs. Lawler’s introductory statement
was:—“Several years before I married I
was taken ill with rheumatic fever. All my
joints swelled up and were most painful, my
knees being particularly bad. My head was
dso terribly swollen, besides being so
afflicted with severe pains that I scarcely
knew what I was doing half my time. The
loctor gave me some medicine which caused
i burning sensation in my chest, and I felt
o distressed after taking each dose that I
sked him to change it, but he said it was
he best medicine he could give me.”
Reporter : “ Was there no ease whatever
lerivable from the treatment he gave you?”
Mrs.<«j Lawler: “Not the slightest.
Previous to my attack I was an unusually
strong woman, capable of working hard all
day without showing any signs of fatigue,
but for four years afterwards I was subject
to such sudden seizures of intense paina and
faintness that in the middle of any light
task I was often compelled to leave the
work undone and lie down for the rest of
the day. With my rheumatism came a
complete collapse of the system, everything
seeming to go wrong at once. The terrific
agonies running through my body kept me
awake at nights, so that when I ought to
have been getting up I felt so dead beat and
depressed that I had no ability or desire to
do anything. Thus the days and months
dragged wearily on, and my condition was
getting worse all the time. My appetite
was in a very bad way too, for I seldom wanted
any food, and if I took a little it did not do
me any good because it would not digest.”
Reporter : “ How could you tell that ? ”
Mrs. Lawler: “Because I felt almost
stifled through having a heavy pain in the
elicsc for one thing, and my stomach felt as
unsettle 1 as if I had drunk a cupful of
yeast. Sometimes the food turned positively
sour and came up again, but before it was
vomited I always suffered acutely from
internal pains. Biliousness gave my mouth
such a nasty taste that retching used to
come on, and what an awful time I had with
sick headaches ! They made my eyes feel
swollen, and my sight was bad. The
swollen state of my ankles and feet often
prevented me from putting my shoes on,
yet, in a way, this did not matter much, as
I was generally too weak to walk about.
There was also something radically wrong
with my shoulders, as I could feel a sharp
pain between them which gave me more
annoyance than enough, and the aches in my
loins were even worse. Suffering so long
and Beeing myself going down hill year after
year at last made me utterably miserable,
and fears about the future were constantly
thrusting themselves upon my mind.”
Reporter: “ Are you naturally of a
nervous temperament ? ”
Mrs. Lawler : “ Not by any means ; but
when I was ill my nervous system was
greatly weakened, and if anything startled
me I could feel them jumping as though
they had been cut loose. People say that
life is sweet under any circumstances, but I
used to think I would rather die than live
this unbearably painful existence any
longer. Up to this time I could never
understand why people should look at the
black side of everything, and allow them-
selves to think of ail sorts of things that are
going to happen ; but that is exactly the
frame of mind I was in, so it must be ill-
health which accounts for such unhappiness,
Anyway, I was too weak and weary to
trouble much about anything, but, thank
God ! all that has passed, and for eight years
I have been free from all the tormenting
symptoms which were bearing me down t
the grave.”
Reporter: “ You had a change in your
treatment, I presume ? ”
Mrs. Lawler: “ You may depend I had,
or else I would not be able to speak to you
as I have done, and it was a friend who
gave the advice which resulted in the
obliteration of my maladies. He was a
friend of my husband’s, and he said he could
recommend something that would do me
good. *lt is Clements Tonic,’ he said, * and
you cannot go wrong if you take it, because
it cured me when I was in the same state
as yeti are in now.* Dubious as I was
through losing confidence in previous medi-
cines, I purchased some Clements Tonic,
and before going any further I would like
to state that it was the only medicine which
did me any good at all. The revivifying
effects of Clements Tonic were nothing short
of miraculous, as two or three bottles set
new vigour coursing through my weakened
system, and I passed from a long period of
melancholy hopelessness to a life of comfort
and cheerfulness. It took a little time, of
course, but the fact remains that Clements
Tonic gradually braced me up and started
to ease my pains very quickly. My people
got a surprise when I started to eat, because
it was so long since I had experienced the
sensation of being hungry, and my appetite
became more vigorous as the days went by.
I am positive that nothing in the world
can equal Clements Tonic for producing
refreshing and peaceful sleep, as it made
me sleep shortly after retiring to bed,
dreams never troubling me, and when I
awoke there was no bitter taste to sicken
me, and the aching sensations had left my
head and limbs.”
Reporter. “Your rheumatic pains—did
Clements Tonic affect them ? ”
Mrs. Lawler : “ Affect them ! Why, when
I could take plenty of food without vomit-
ing or retching, or feeling any pains in the
chest or stomach after meals, I was nearly
as well as ever, because by that time the
swellings in my limbs had gone down and
the rheumatic pains were hardly noticeable.
All I wanted was another bottle or two of
Clements Tonic, and you may be assured
that I got them. The end to my sufferings
was assured in the tone and steadiness which
Clements Tonic imparted to my nervous
system, and in due course ihere was
nothing in my appearance to indicate that
I had gone through the serious illness which
had set at defiance all other medicines
excepting Clements Tonic.”
Reporter: “ may I bring your statements
before the public ? ”
Mrs. Lawler: “You are at liberty to
publish them in any way you think best,
as I cannot speak too freely or too gratefully
about Clements Tonic for giving me the
splendid health I now enjoy.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Hester Rose Lawler, of 4 Broads'airs-street,
Balmain, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting of five folios, and
consecutively numbered from one to five, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account of
my illness and cure by Clements Tonic; and also
contains my full permission to publish in any way
my statements which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment; and I make this
solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of
the Oaths Act, 1900.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 4, 1904.
The Deadly Spirit of Compromise.
When the call was made a month ago for a
Whole Hog Party—one with a platform of
honesty so bold and decisive that it would make
everybody either enthusiastically praise or
enthusiastically curse—there did not at the time
seem any party likely to meet the call. But The
Bulletin devoutly hoped that its old friend, the
Labor party, would come forward to take up the
work—necessarily undergoing considerable con-
version first. Last week the Labor Leagues met
in conference to talk about that and other
matters. Practically every district was repre-
sented. From wayback, delegates came full
of fine enthusiasm. If it had been left
to them—the bush-worker and the miner—
there is no doubt what the answer would have
been. Whole Hog themselves downright,
thoroufc.i, unoom promising—they would have
jumped at the chance to give to the political
movement their own spirit. But they had to
reckon with another force, which was merely the
old enemy, Vested Interests, in the new disguise
of the sitting Labor member; and Vested Interests
temporised and compromised, and the final de-
cision of the Conference was Yes-No. An honest
Yes to the demand for a Whole Hog party was
advocated by the Federal Labor members and by
Mr. Dacey among the State members, but, as a
party, the State members were against the policy
of Thorough ; and were joined by those timorous
souls who were led by practised tongues to
look at things in the light of compromise.
Many of these —perhaps most of them—liked
Honesty. To them personally it seemed the
proper course. But what of the “general public”?
What of the man who runs the grocery at the
corner, and the alderman who is a butcher down
the street ? The Whole Hog might alarm these.
Vested Interests said: “Why not leave well
enough alone? Why alarm anybody? Why
make anybody enthusiastically angry ? Not the
Whole Hog, then; and especially not an un-
dressed hog. Dress him; bring him in in
sections; put pleasant sprigs of parsley round
him, and then the . .eye of the fastidious grocery-
man would not be shocked, and his vote would
still be safe.” Thus, in the end, the vote was for
not a Whole Hog party, but a Frilled Ham party.
r* • » •
When .the Labor party first entered the State
Parliament most of the members had the repu-
tation of being firebrands and revolutionaries.
Their arrival followed a wave of social discontent
which travelled almost round the world. Men
had never been stirred so deeply before, in this
generation at any rate; and in three States at
least, N.S.W. among them, something very much
like Whole Hog parties were called into life. But
somehow the Labor member, who formed part of
the Whole Hog party or thereabouts of 12 or 14
years ago, soon began to undergo a change.
Taken out of his old environment of “.holy
discontent,” and no longer feeling the per-
sonal pinch which he ha.d known in the past,
the call for reforms seemed to him to become less
urgent. When he did strive—as sometimes he
did —the cumbersome forms of Parliament first
worried and then wearied him. He began to think
that it was better to coax than to push. This, of
course, led to bargaining, especially in New South
Wales; and bargaining led to demoralisation.
Ministers who owed their life to the Labor mem-
ber. naturally went up and down the country
saying what a good fellow he was, and how grossly
some people had been maligning him. All this
had its effect. The party strove to live up to its
reputation for mildness and meekness. The
grocer at the corner-store and the fat alderman
who runs the butcher’s shop already alluded to,
became its supporters. Even the bank manager
. tolerated it. And now when it is called
upon to be again the Whole Hog party that
it was in the beginning, the grocer and alderman
have to be considered. They have to go into the
scale on the other side. It is the Grocer’s vote
against the Whole Hog policy ; and the answer is
Yes-No. So the bush-worker and miner from
way-out, thorough, earnest, and uncompromising,
and the city worker who also hasn’t lost his inde-
pendence, go back to their fellows, weary and dis-
pirited. They hate snuffling and “ smooging ”;
and yet they must know that the policy framed
for them is a policy of snuffle and “ smooge.”
* * * »
“ No borrowing ” was the Whole Hog policy.
It was simple, direct, and incapable of two mean-
ings. No politician could twist it and Yes-no
upon it. It meant no borrowing, and nothing
else. Only loans that fell due might be renewed,
or existing loans might be converted ; but neither
would be borrowing, since neither would add to
the debt. Already this State owes per head of
population more than almost any other country in
the world ; it has borrowed so much, and its credit
is consequently so low, that when the other day it
asked in London for half-a-million in order to
pay bills that were due, it could only get the money
at a cost of about £4 13s. per cent. That is to say,
this State is now paying more for money than the
banks will pay for fixed deposits. Yet we may be
sure of this : Bull-Cohen will lend —lend to any-
body—so long as he believes he can get his interest
and principal. It is only when he believes the
borrower to be carrying such a load of interest
that he cannot possibly struggle under much
more, that he puts up the rate in order to cover
himself for the extra risk he takes. It is said
that there is no risk of repudiation, and that there
is no such word in our language. Bull-
Cohen knows better. If he didn’t, he wouldn’t
charge us £4 13s. per cent. When, therefore,
Cohen charges an abnormal rate, it is the notice
to stop. If, however, the State does not stop,
then Cohen will only continue his support on
conditions. Already he has said that he does not
like the security ; and if he is to go on advancing
upon it he will certainly ask for a say in the
management of the concern. Already he does
exert a passive influence. What, we are all the
time asking, will they think in England? Victoria
had to borrow money to renew a loan for which no
provision had been made. A Treasury official
was sent to London, and he reported that
prospects were black ; Cohen didn’t like the
■ecurity. Thereupon, Bent made his great “eco-
nomies” in the railways, and roared about them
Irvine and Shiels cut down the old-age pen-
sions; almost everything that the Labor party
is supposed to think kindly of was jumped upon
with all the violence that Bent and Irvine
owned. Cohen was immediately mollified. As
the Agent-General reported, “the Government’s
policy in the matter of the railway trouble and
with regard to economy generally made an ex-
cellent impression in the City ’’ —and Cohen, who
before had hesitated, now lent the money. It is
not too much to say that the price of that loan
was the cruel reduction of old-age pensions and
the equally cruel treatment of the railway service.
It is equally certain that the same sort of price
will have to be paid by every State in the future
unless the Whole Hog policy intervenes; the
price will in every case be something that
the Labor party treasures, or ought to trea-
sure. And yet it will not say “No borrow-
ing.” The corner grocer’s vote must be kept
even though it means that Cohen will
presently rule this country, and that the country
will no longer rule itself. How Cohen rules a
country which is nothing more to him than a place
for making money in, South Africa shows. But
the Labor member will risk all that to keep the
vote of the Corner Grocery.
• • * •
“ No borrowing ” does not involve stoppage of
works for the development of the country. Even
without further taxation it should be quite possible
tojpay £2,000,000 into a publio works fund and
£250,000 into a sinking fund ; for the State’s net
revenue is now pretty nearly that total sum more
than it was three years ago. By true economy—
by getting rid of useless officials ; by abolishing the
“ Government stroke ” in Government offices ; by
employing in the State’s service the best men,
even though they have to be paid high prices ; by
economising office space ; by abolishing political
bribes—these funds could be fed to the extent men-
tioned. On the works provided from such a works
fund there would be no interest burden, and conse-
quently far better prospects of success. Lower
freights and fares would thus eventually be pos-
sible. The growth of the sinking fund, if it were
accompanied by a fixed determination to borrow
no more, would so enhance the State’s credit that
the renewal loans would be available at a great
reduction, and an enormous saving in taxation
would be possible—or, better still, an enormous
increase in the fund available for public works.
What is happening in Canada is that Cohen is
actually providing the sinking fund himself ; in
other words, as the sinking fund grew, he accepted
a lower and still lower rate of interest, and thus
a saving was made which constituted a sinking fund
in itself. The Labor party could accomplish exactly
the same result for Australia, and thus do for it a
greater service than any other party ever did for
it in the past. Instead, however (with its eye on
the Corner Grocery), it decides on borrowing to
do things that will pay their way and 1 per cent,
over. That won’t alarm anybody. Also, meaning
practically nothing, it will arouse no enthusiasm.
Nobody will feel sufficiently excited by it to throw
at it either a brick or a cheer.
* * * *
Practically every work that has been carried out
for Heaven knows how long has been “ permanent
and reproductive.” The Owe’Sullivans have
E roved over and over again that things that have
een thrown on the scrap-heap years ago are still
earning interest. No Ministry ever has owned up
to carrying out with loan money unproductive
works. And still Coghlan’s figures show that
between 1898 and 1902 only 40 per cent, of the
loan money was spent on railways and tramways
&c., the other 60 per cent, going in trifles, by far
the most of which can never pay anything at all.
Still they are all “reproductive undertakings,”
and they have been carried out, too, by a Govern-
ment which has done pretty well what the Labor
party has required. All, then, that the new
determination means is that the works are going
to be 1 percent, better than they have been in the
past. The State Parliamentary Labor party need
nave no fears; that poor little 1 per cent, will not
cause any alarm in the Corner Grocery, and the
vote of the alderman who keeps the butcher s
shop is still safe. >
• • • *
So the Labor party misses its chance. Fearful
of giving offence to its “ middle-class ” patrons, it
steers a middle course. It aims at creeping along
unnoticed-v.too inconspicuous to have a bnck
thrown at it. Such tactics must fail, Ihe
people appreciate boldness and brightness, dash
and daring. Mark how the “ Thoroughs of
Queensland have won to power against enormous
odds. Mark how, on the other hand, when the
N.S.W. Labor party, the other day, proposed a
policy of compromise with regard to reduction of
members, the people despised and rejected the
miserable proposal that was neither one thing
nor the other. The “neuter” has been held
in contempt for all time, and he is held in con-
tempt now. Yet that is more or less the role
which the N.S.W. Labor party has deliberately
chosen. The position of W hole Hog party is still
waiting to be filled.
Federal Politics.
There is a national interest in the state of Mr.
Kingston’s health just now. A hale Kingston
would do much to clear a doubtful political
situation, and to clear it in the most satisfactory
way by a step forward in Federalism and Radical-
ism. But, so far, there is no definite promise of
that. Speaking after he resigned from the Barton
Cabinet, Mr. Kingston expressed a doubt as to
whether, if the call were made to him, he could
summon up enough health to take the lead in a
great fight for the Progressive cause in politics.
Since then he has had leisure to re-
cuperate. But to a man of his ardent,
nay volcanic, temperament, leisure does not neces-
sarily mean rest; during the Federal elections,
though he had no personal opponent in his con-
stituency, Kingston took up a great burden of
work on behalf of the Radical cause. Now, in
Maoriland, he is making some effort to restore his
shattered health. If that effort succeeds the
political future is fairly clear; if it does not, the
loss to Australia will be hard to calculate.
• • • •
The strategic point around which a great poli-
tical battle is now progressing—a battle of which
there are practically no outward signs, but which
is none the less stern and real —is the Industrial
Arbitration Bill. The Deakin Cabinet, as an
entity, maintains the iRViNE-dictated attitude into
which Premier Barton was entrapped last session,
refusing to apply the benefits of the proposed Act
to any employees of a State Government, or a
public body, or to seamen of deep-water ships.
That would confine the benefits of the Act to a
very small class indeed. Constitutionally it can
only have jurisdiction over disputes affecting
more than one State; limit its scope further to
exclude seamen, publio servants, railway, muni-
cipal and public trust employees, and only a shred
of benefit would remain. This limitation was
suggested originally by Messrs. Irvine and
Bent ; it was accepted by the then Federal
Government in a moment of folly, persuaded
that Victoria was solidly with Irvine in a Tory
re-action. When Mr. Deakin was called upon to
re-construct the Cabinet it was open to him to
retract from a position which was politically mis-
taken, and of which, if all accounts be true, he
was not conscientiously enamored. But the
shadow of Irvine frightened him and, to a greater
extent still, his colleague, Mr. Turner. The
result was the Ballarat manifesto against a
complete Arbitration Bill. It would not
do any serious violence to political traditions
as they are in Australia if Mr. Deakin
were now to repudiate that declaration.
Mr. Reid, for instance, oould repudiate a
dozen such statements before breakfast any
morning. Mr. Deakin, who is of finer grain,
refuses to take that course, and, Quixotically faith-
ful to his folly, is resolved to risk the existence of
his Cabinet oil that issue.
• • * *
The incident can, in all human probability,
have but one end—the defeat of Mr. Deakin.
Possibly, with Mr. Reid’s help, he may carry his
mangled Arbitration Bill through the House of
Reps, by a narrow majority ; even that -much is,
to the utmost degree, improbable. Certainly
disaster would await the IRViNE-dictated Bill in
the Senate. There Federalism and Radicalism
hold undisputed sway. The Bill returned
to the House of Reps, in a Liberal form
would, presumably, be dropped; and then Mr.
Deakin Would drop too. There are two reasons
why the Labor party, and its “flying buttresses ”
—the men who support the party without being
inside it—are so keenly anxious for a radical
Arbitration Act. One is the desire for industrial
peace, the hope of averting in the future such
unhappy incidents as the recent Victorian railway
strike ; the other is an ambition for an advance-
ment of the Federal spirit. The passing of a
thorough Federal Arbitration Act would range
the Cabbage Tr.ee mob and the Johnaynes coterie
and the Tory anti-Federalists all over the conti-
nent into one camp—such a camp as the Scots
held at Dunbar when Oliver Cromwell moved
on to their destruction, sword in hand and
Psalm on lips. It would provoke an outbreak
of anti-Federalism, but an outbreak under such
conditions that the forces of peace and order
and reason and democracy would be on the side
of the Federation. A thorough Arbitration Act
would commit the Federation to a contest with
the provincialists; but it would at the same time
commit the democracy to the Federal principle,
and the issue of the struggle would be for no
moment uncertain. From the contest the Federa-
tion would come stronger and dearer in the
hearts of the masses of the people, who have the
power to give the ultimate verdict in Australian
politics.
• • • •
Mr. Deakin professes to believe that he has a
chance of holding office whilst maintaining
the Irvine attitude on the Arbitration Bill, and
he argues almost angrily with himself on the
point, but still in his heart he cannot but be con-
vinced of the almost certain ruin to which his
Cabinet moves. Others have no doubt about the
matter at all. Mr. Lyne, if all accounts are true,
takes the Labor party attitude on the Bill, and
will retire from the Ministry rather than follow
Mr. Deakin in the matter ; and he would be fol-
lowed possibly by one, or even two, other mem-
bers of the Cabinet. This is so generally reported
that it is said that Mr. Bruce Smith is already
suggesting that now has the time come for Mr.
Deakin to call two or three genuine statesmen from
the Opposition side—say himself and Mr. Reid—
into the Cabinet and save the country from
Labor domination. Mr. Reid is in the painful
position of needing an even more intricate atti-
tude than that of Yes-No to make him safe.
He wants office. He recognises that there must
be a re-distribution of the prizes, and yet is
absolutely puzzled to know on what side he
should range himself to make a call for him
certain. On the whole, so far as the country is
concerned, if Mr. Deakin is determined to side
with the Tories, and if Mr. Turner has come
to the same bad conclusion, it would be well
for Mr. Reid to go into the same boat,
and go down with it. The worst that could
happen would be that the Labor party, for want of a
better combination, should decide to side with
Reid for, apart from the fiscal issue—which is for
this Parliament a dead one—Mr. Reid would
command no following. He bring to a
coalition very little more than his Yes no. Pre-
suming Mr. Kingston comes back to Parliament
with renewed health, the situation would soon
simplify itself. The Progressives would naturally
range by his side, the Conservatives on the other
side ; and the Progressives would have a working
majority, for, the tariff issue abandoned for the
present, several of the so-called Freetraders would
range themselves on the Radical side. If Mr.
Kingston is not able to take the lead the_ position
is more complicated. What is needed is a man
with whom the Labor party will be willing to
share power, but who will be able to attract in
addition a party of Progressives, independent of
that party. Further, if the Labor party takes
a share of office, it will only do so on the condi-
tion that a forward policy is embodied in the
Government programme: it will not be content
to merely hold office and mark time. The
position is one full of difficulty if Mr. Kingston
is not available as a solution. One way out, it
has been suggested by some of Mr. Deakin’s
friends, is that he should, if he finds it impossible
to hold office himself, advise that the Governor
send to Mr. Watson direct, and thus
throw the whole responsibility of the situation
on the Labor party : and there could be worse
developments than that.
A State Newspaper.
A State neiwspaper—with a firm accent on the
“news ” —is coming within the range of practical
politics. The Bulletin would not be surprised
to find it figuring very shortly in the programme
of the progressive party. For the development
of political and industrial life is bringing about
an issue between democracy and the newspapers
—a position in which democracy can only survive
by a radical change in regard to the system of
news distribution. This is stated as a cold
fact, not as a variation of the average poli-
tician’s cant about the “tyranny” of the
newspapers which do not agree with him (and
which are “blessed instruments of progress and
enlightenment” when they are with him): nor as
an angry protest against some conspicuous bit of
newspaper misrepresentation. Given (1) present
newspaper organisation and (2) the present neces-
sity of news to the citizen, and the State must take
upon itself the. responsibility of supplying news
without taint, or else surrender the government
of the country into the hands of mercenary news-
paper proprietaries. That is the thesis—startling
enough: but strictly provable.
• * * *
The genesis of the newspaper and the present
organisation of the newspaper must be taken into
account to get a correct view of the position.
Originally there were two forms of journals—the
news letter which was a record of gossip collected
in the town and distributed in the country,
and the pamphlet, which was an argument 0&
some political subject. About the right to pub-
lish and circulate pamphlets arose the great
struggle for the “liberty of the press” (and the
fruits of that historic fight for human liberty no
one would lightly propose to sacrifice). With the
growth of civilisation the two forms of journal
began to merge into one and to become the
modern newspaper, partly made up of news,
partly of comment. At first the honorable tradi-
tion was, that the news should be, as far as
was humanly possible, correct, whatever the
bias of the comment. The next stage of
development was the advertisement. Newspaper
change marched then with rapid feet towards
the modern daily paper, purporting to be mainly
a newspaper, but giving with the news much
comment, and depending absolutely for its exist-
ence on the advertising support it gets The final
step is the newspaper trust —the combination of
the great daily newspapers in a particular capital
or country, at first for the purposes of economy in
co-operating to secure certain classes of news,
later so as to “stand together ” for mutual pro-
tection or mutual aggression.
* » * *
The position in Australia now is that the
daily newspapers are all combined, more or
less closely, for certain trading purposes, and
are always ready to combine for political
purposes. In N.S.W., for example, the two
morning journals are in sympathy on most
points, and, combining their forces, they are able
to almost absolutely dominate politics. If they
did this by the logic of their arguments the
position would be such that no exception could
reasonably be taken. But that is not so. They
work to a common end by suppressing some facts,
by putting other facts in a misleading perspective,
by coloring other statements of supposed fact with
bias, and sometimes by absolute mis-statement.
They combine, too, against any opposition so
effectively that it is almost impossible for any
private person to challenge their monopoly.
When another morning paper was mooted a little
while ago, the existing dailies agreed to boycott
all news-agents who dared to handle the new
publication.
* * * *
Buttressed as it is, the existing monopoly is
practically unchallengeable by private effort, and
as news is just as much a necessity to the normal
civilised man as bread and meat, there is no
possible remedy in refraining from buying the
daily newspaper. Buy it people must; and the
public mind is thus just as surely contaminated by
whatever poison the newspaper proprietaries care
to instil into their wares as if the public water-
supply was polluted. A weekly newspaper or a
magazine is in a different position. It is not so
much a necessity as a luxury. People can buy it
or do without it, as they choose, and, whatever
lies or truths it cares to mix up with its comments,
it never becomes such an evil as to call for drastic
remedy. But the daily news the citizens must
have, and, as existing suppliers refuse to give it,
except in such a garbled and colored form as suits
their ends, the State must one day step in and
fill the want.
• * • •
A State newspaper at first thought seems im-
possible. But it is quite feasible. It should be
put at the outset under the charge of an experi-
enced journalist, with the power and surety of
position of a Supreme Court Judge. He should
be given a commission to produce a bright and
literary newspaper absolutely without political
comment. He would print Parliamentary and
law reports—in full or abridged according to their
interest—cabled and telegraphic news, police
items, stories, sketches, verses, literary reviews
and all the other features of a daily news-
paper served up according to his discretion,


, leaders ”- or comments on politics
• ' i- con fiuing all Government adver-
an ■ Rnnouncements to the State organ
eie would be secured for it at once a
rge revenue and a certain forced circula-
mn. Additional subscribers it would have to win
on its merits. “ Political influence ”in regard to
s conduct would be strictly tabooed, and would
De guarded against by giving the editor the status
o a puisne judge. There would be no “ letters
tiom members of Parliament,” and no arguments
tor or against any political proposal. The paper
would deal in facts, and facts alone—except in
purely literary matters, when opinions and views
would be in order. Those who wanted political
fancied* 3 su °h other newspapers as they
A State newspaper would be nob at all
an impossible nor even a very difficult under-
taking. As a speculation it would, if properly
conducted, at least pay expenses. As an aid to
the upgrowth of an educated and unfettered
citizenship, it would be worth to the State very
many thousands of pounds a year. The nataral
intelligence of the people would gradually bring
them to seek in a publication, the truth of which
was guaranteed, the news of the day. What
comment they cared for they could seek in private
organs. The power of those private newspapers to
mislead the people would then be gone, for there
would always be a standard by which to gauge
their statements and a means to expose their sup-
pressions. There would be no interference with
the liberty of the press; private persons could
still print what they liked within the four corners
of the law ; the State would simply take the
liberty of printing the cold truth once a day, just
as it would, conceivably, undertake to provide
pure milk twice a day, if it found that all dairymen
were banded together to foist typhoid bacilli on
their customers with the milk.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Perplexed Briton.
** Mr. Theodore V. S. Augier, steamship owner,
of London,” is another traveller who has been
telling Australia what a stupid little person it is:
Your “White Australia” policy is a perplexity to
Britons. It seems so petty, so unnecessary, and so selfish
that they cannot understand it. Australia has room for
all classes of people, and Nature intended her to sustain
all races. The man has got hold of the wrong idea who
thinks that there is a certain amount of work to be done,
and that if black and yellow races come here they will be
employed at the expense of the white man.
How Mr. Augier came to be such a cocksure
authority on the secrets and purposes of Nature
he doesn’t explain. He tells that he “ has just
completed a tour of the United States,” and
possibly he picked up the information there —
say, at a negro lynching in the South. What
is more to the point, though, is that the
Briton should be helped out of his perplexity.
The trouble really is that he is looking up the
wrong tree. He thinks (if he is as foolish as Mr.
Augier says he is) that the only question at issue
is one of work and. wages—that the white man
fears for his job. Perhaps he does; he knows that
the colored workman is ignorant and un-organised,
and he knows, too, that there are white em-
ployers who will be prepared to fill their own
p ockets by trading on that ignorance and weakness.
But that is not the large matter. Not the
loss of a job, but loss of racial purity, is at the
bottom of the demand for a White Australia.
So far, the Australian is clean white; and he is
proud and jealous of it, and of his European
ancestry. Cannot the perplexed Briton under-
stand that—cannot he understand pride of race ?
“So petty, so unnecessary,” the perplexed Briton
says. “So petty” to struggle against degenera-
tion into a decadent race of half-breeds ! “So
unnecessary ” to strive to cultivate a race which
shall have pride of birth ! If this is “ petty ” and
“ unnecessary,” will the perplexed Briton tell us
what is patriotism ?
- The-Hard Work of Reform.
The difficulties which would face a Whole Hog
Cabinet in the work of reform are faintly indi-
cated by the howl of indignation which has
greeted the sensible action of the See Govern-
ment in abolishing the South Head Road Trust
(Sydney). This Trust had control of various
roads which run from Sydney to the Eastern
suburbs ; it was formed originally when the popu-
lation of those suburbs was thin and scattered,
and its work was to keep the main roads
in order, the State footing the bill. This
arrangement has since continued, whilst at Pad-
dington, Bondi, Waverley, Woollahra, Rose Bay
and Watson’s Bay, flourishing and thickly popu-
lated suburbs have grown up, and the area served
by the Trust has come to shelter the greater por-
tion of the rich folk of the metropolis. So John
See says that the district can now keep its own
roads in order and refuses to subsidise it with
£4OOO or £5OOO a year of State money. The
suburbs affected—which are all strongholds of the
Deform Party and are frantically anxious for the
Treasurer to be economical at the expense of the
civil servant and the State road laborer and the
old age pensioner—are, in consequence of this
action, purple with indignation, and that enthusias-
tic Deform paper, the Daily Telegraph, howls in
the van of their protest against a stoppage of
theirplunder. The incident, weliavesaid, indicates
the difficulties which stand in the way of Whole
Hog Reform. It also, however, indicates the
opportunities lying at the hand of a party which
went all the way in the path of honesty; for then
all over the country would arise the squeals of
people robbed of their chance to spoil the Trea-
sury, and all over the country would be advertised
the fact that a new era had dawned and that there
was a somewhat different Government in power.
Such an advertisement would rouse the great
body of the people to come forward in support of
true economy and reform. The proportion of the
voters who benefit by the system of jobs, swindles,
steals and concessions is proportionately small.
All that is needed is to drive them into one camp,
and encourage them and their newspapers to
make as much noise then as possible. Then their
destruction would be easy.

Great Sydney and the Small Alderman.
The Greater Sydney movement, it was made plain
last week, can expect no real help from Sydney
City Council. Alderman Fitzgerald —who is
one of the few aldermen with notions above the
kerbing and guttering level—moved to memorial-
ise the Premier to create, by Act of Parliament,
a citizens’ convention to draft a constitution for
a Greater Sydney. That is the simple and direct
way to the object in view—that or an Act of
Parliament embodying a constitution without the
preliminary of acitizens’convention. Theone thing
necessary is to get the matter out of the hands of
the aldermen—the city aldermen who are in only a
few cases threatened with civic intelligence and the
suburban aldermen who are, as a rule, absolutely
hopeless Bumbles. But Mr. Fitzgerald’s motion
was met by an amendment from Alderman
Meagher— appropriately seconded by Alderman
Griefin —“to open up communications” with all
councils in the metropolitan area on the subject.
That means to invite the aldermen, the wild herds
of suburban aldermen, to talk on the subject.
And that means no hope of progress. What is
wanted—to put the case in alderman language—is
“yacker, not yabber.” The Sydney Council by
its resolution opens the way to endless discussion
but to no decision.

How the Mining- Industry is Hampered.
The Sydney newspapers are suffering from one of
their periodical spasms of interest in the mining
industry. A dozen or more correspondents have
written to say, in the usual vague fashion, that
mining is a large part of the backbone of the
State, and that something more ought to be done
to “encourage it.” They make also the usual
vague remarks concerning unsatisfactory legisla-
tion, and draw dazzling pictures of the prosperity
that would sweep over the State if something or
other were done—they don’t say exactly what.
The nearest they ever get is a suggestion that
mining rents should be reduced and the labor
conditions abolished. Several of them draw
attention to the difference with which the
State treats the pastoral and the mining
industries. Pastoral country, they remark, can
be had for Id. per acre per annum; but if the
same land is required for mining, a man has to pay
from ss. to £1 an acre for it. This is plainly a
monstrous anomaly, they say. But it is nothing
of the sort. Given fair treatment, the land is in
no way depreciated in value by pastoral occupa-
tion; in fact, oftener it is improved. The miner,
on the other hand, makes his money by robbing
the ground. A man might rent a house for .£lOO
a year; but if he had the right to carry off the
place, the landlord would probably want some-
thing more, and that is the position in which the
mining industry stands. The State is the land-
lord ; and in asking the man who has the right to
carry away its property more than it asks him
who merely has the right to use the pro-
perty, it is doing nothing unreasonable what-
ever. In fact, the State, as landlord, behaves
with the utmost generosity, not to say prodi-
gality, ,to the mining man. Except in rare
cases it asks nothing in the way of tribute or
percentage ; and what private landlord would
let his Broken Hill Proprietaries on such
terms as that? The State is suffering to a great
extent because it handles its affairs so differently
from the way in which a private concern would
handle them; and the people who say that the
State can’t be trusted with business concerns are
also the very people who are loudest in demanding
that the State shall act in this grossly unbusiness-
like way with regard to its landed estate. The
other point upon which a change is asked for
is with regard to labor conditions and tenure.
“The mining industry is hampered above all
things by the labor conditions,” the S.M. Herald
echoes. “The law requires so many men to be
employed that people cannot afford to engage in
mining. It kills the goose which lays,"’ etc.
Well, there is plenty of mining land in N.S.W.
upon which no such conditions press—upon which,
in fact, there are no conditions at all. These are
private lands, acquired by ordinary grant or
m.c.p., and the goose isn’t interfered with in the
slightest. Yet the wretched bird might as well
be dead for all the laying she does. On the very
day when the Herald was describing how the
mining industry would flourish if there were no
labor conditions, and how many thousands of men
would be employed by mine-owners if only mine-
owners didn’t have to employ anybody, a deputa-
tion waited upon the Minister for Mines (one,
merely, of scores of similar deputations which
have filed in at the same office) with a local peti- :
tion with regard to some lands naar Orange:
The lands are owned by the Scottish Australian Gold-
mining Company of Sydney, Limited, and are held by
virtue of a Crown grant-without reservation of minerals.
The petitioners state that there are deposits of gold and
copper ore and other minerals on this land, and that the
lands were at one time profitably mined by the company,
but for the past 20 years have been lying idle. - Several
applications have been made for mining licenses on the
lands, but these were refused on the ground that-the
lands were held by the company. If these lands were
available for mining, leases would be eagerly sought for,
and employment provided for upwards of 500 persons.
What the people who want to abolish labor con-
ditions really seek is, in effect, to put all such
lands in the position of these near Orange ; and
they say they want it “for the encouragement of
mining!” The only sort of “mining” that it
would encourage is the sort that is carried on in
Pitt and Collins Streets. How much the Pitt
and Collins Streets miners add to the wealth of
the country Coghlan, curiously enough, omits to
say.

Japan’s Industrial Position
The war-cloud in the Far North darkens again.
The fact that Japan, after trying every foreign
market, has raised an internal loan is the strongest
of all hints that she contemplates fight. The
“ Eldei-s ” of the nation have, so far, had a re-
straining influence on the people’s Jingoism, but
their power of restraint seems to be now ex-
hausted, and Japan prepares to rush headlong into
war with a nation which, alone among European
Powers, has so far never lost a serious campaign,
and never had to yield an acre of territory.
There can be but one conclusion to such.
a struggle if it is confined to the ori-
ginal combatants. No matter how high the
spirit of the Japanese; no matter how fine their
organisation, they must succumb in the end from
sheer lack of material resources. There is no
money behind Japan, and there are no acres
out of which money can be taxed. The Russian
Government may be poor, even bankrupt, but the
country has always the staff of an immense
stretch of fertile land pouring out its riches with
the recurring seasons. Japan’s small territory is
already taxed to the utmost to give its population
a bare subsistence. With a chance to get to close
quarters with its foe, Japan might have a chance,
presuming military success, to keep a war going
with- loot, adopting the Napoleonic method of
making a campaign pay for itself. But with the
seat of war in Manchuria that would not be
Sossible. Supposing the most favorable result to
apan’s miltary operations, it does not seem
possible for it to maintain a campaign for any
length of time. Stephen England, in the Daily
Mail, gave recently a picture (the fruit of careful
study) of the social life of Japan, which is a
startling variant from the superficial impressions
of the tourist, and shows how hopeless would be
that country’s position in a long-drawn-out war :
In Tokio not fewer than 200,000 people seldom, if ever,
know of a certainty where the necessities of the next day
will come from, and throughout the land the great
majority are too poor to eat rice. The high-grade rice
grown in the islands is exported almost to the last sack,
and inferior rice imported for those who can afford it. I
have spent days and nights in company with a brilliant
Japanese sociologist, who, like scores of his fellow-
students of men and things, believes that Japan has left
its good days of general happiness and general comfort
for ever behind, and is entering upon a sordid and merci-
less age of industrialism, in which its people are not fitted
by temperament to compete. He is crying out a warning
to Japan that her seat at the council-table of the Powers
-is being paid for in the blood of her citizens, not ex-
pended as they would pour it forth cheerfully in war, but
in factory and on farm, in shop and in office. “Think
for a moment,” he cried last week as we looked at a
Japanese battleship in the offing, “ what a multitude of
our tiny rice fields it takes to support such a monster,
and then remember that our people can’t afford to eat
rice ! ”.
Suppose a Japanese worker of the casual class has had.
a good day and returns to his home with, say, sixpence.
He will expend this in farthing purchases of miso, a kind
of soup stock, oil, fuel, tobacco, and perhaps a little fish,
which, if he feels reckless, he will eat raw with horse-
radish. He buys in driblets, and like the poor in every
city of the world, pays enormous prices. Had he re-
turned empty-handed he would have hurried to the
pawnbroker, always near at hand, and raised a few
farthings on his precious brass pipe, his hibachi, or his
few poor garments not in actual use. With the money
he would have purchased fish entrails or the offal from
horses used for food, and perhaps a handful of scraps
from a garbage barrel. With these he would have feasted
with his family, and with them prayed that Providence
would give him a better day to-morrow, so that he might
reclaim his goods. With the fearful piercing frost of a
Japanese winter, some covering must be had. Now
appears another plunderer of the poor in the guise of the
capitalist who rents quilts by the night. He charges
from one farthing for a shred of dirty, patched old rag, to
a penny or even twopence for a foul but heavy covering.
Rent must be paid in advance, and before the family go
to sleep the collector comes and gets either the money or
the quilt. Few of the inhabitants of Shitaya ever get
enough money ahead to buy bed-clothing, and the
ghastly tragedy of renting is re-enacted again and again
for winter after winter. Nothing that was ever edible
can become too bad for the very poor to use. From this
and similar quarters the scavengers go forth daily
searching for food, and they rake the city as with a
comb. Back they come at night laden with bad rice, de-
cayed fish and meat, scraps from slop barrels, broken food
from restaurants, and all manner of queer odds and ends.
Poverty has its ultimate expression here—its last word.
That is poverty such as no European “sub-
merged” can even imagine; and it is poverty
from which practically nothing can be drawn in
taxes. Where the casual laborer thinks himself
lucky to get sixpence a day, and a shilling a day
is a good wage, there is no reserve for the nation
to draw upon. And the poverty of the proletariat
in Japan is not due to the existence of a great class
of wealthy rich-; the rich in that country are very
few in number and very moderate in wealth. The
position is that the country is an intrinsically poor
one. In its ancient state, when it did not have to
pour out-millions to maintain a bloated, militarism,
the population was well enough off; a high standard
of aestheticism and a gentle social nature made
poverty endurable. But ambition has changed
poverty to destitution. The alternatives are to go
back on the path of militarism or to replenish the
national Treasury by foreign conquest. It is
because the second alternative seems to be the
better to the Japanese Jingoes that the nation is
such a menace to the future of Australia.
