The existence of great dissatisfaction among
Sydney tram employees, owing to the suspension
of the annual increments, is being utilised by the
Sydney daily press for several bad purposes. That
the tram men have grievances is true ; but the fact
that they have stated a case for the Arbitration
Court is a sign that they have no idea of breaking
the law to seek redress. But the papers have set
to work to bring about a mild panic in Suburbia
by conveying the impression that a tram strike
was more than likely. That served to alarm
Suburbia as to the evil of labor organisations, and
also,- incidentally, to impress a moral against the
Arbitration Act. As a matter of fact, no strike
was ever contemplated by the Union. The tram
men say that, if anyone talked strike, it was
probably some -non-unionist who would have
talked the blowing up of Government House and
the kidnapping of the Commissioners if desired.
But the daily newspapers desired to discredit
Unionism generally. A strike would make the
Tram Employees’ Union unpopular among the
people who depend upon the system to get to and
from the city daily, and the next best thing to an
actual strike would be the dread of one ; therefore
the creation of a scare. The papers also wished
to bring home to the people of Sydney the fabt
that the existence of the Industrial Arbitration
Court could not necessarily prevent (though
it could soon . stop) a strike ; of the Tram
men, and thereby heap more contempt on
the Court. (In this connection it mignt be
pointed out that the existence of the police-court,
the Criminal Court, and Robert Howard do not,
in N.S.W., prevent always the perpetration of
murder.) There is some reason for a suspicion
that the D.T., at least, wanted the men to strike.
.It held out the inducement to them that by
holding up the trams they could force the Court to
consider their grievances quickly instead of wait-
ing ten or twelve months. It dragged this bait
before . the exasperated employees while skilfully
avoiding any reassurance to the public that
the prompt interference of the Court would
quickly end the strike and public inconvenience.
It further complained bitterly about the men
appealing to the Government for redress after
invoking the Arbitration Court. Which was
most ridiculous. The Government is their em-
ployer, and the fact that a man has sued his boss’s
manager for ss. due is no reason why he should
not try to effect a friendly settlement out of court,
especially when the Court won’t hear the case, for
12 months. The Bulletin is confident that the
tramway employees will avoid .the fatal mistake
o,f being tempted by the daily papers into a strike.
That would help the enemies of labor, but it
would not help the men.

“W.T.G.” takes one view of a matter which is
open to much argument: —
In view of the remarkable capacity often manifested
by the Sydney daily newspapers for trimming, clipping,
distorting, and otherwise manipulating the reports of
proceedings at political conferences, it is by no means
surprising that at the annual conference of the Political
Labor League it was decided to exclude the press, an
exception being made in favor of the paper which is under
the direct control and censorship of the Labor party. It
is quite easy to understand the objection to the presence
of reporters from the daily papers. There are two
methods of misleading the general public which may be
adopted by the press. One method is to manufacture
plain, unvarnished lies and publish sensational reports of
occurrences which haven’t happened ; the other is the
more simple and much safer plan of suppressing material
facts. In courts of law, the suppression of the truth by
witnesses is held to be equally villainous with the
utterance of false evidence, and it has even been
held by some jurists that the suppression of material
truth is a more rascally form of lying than plain
invention. That is why the oath in law courts is made so
comprehensive, and a witness is sworn to “ speak the
truth, the whole truth, and nothing but the truth.” The
newspapers find the suppression of the truth much safer
than the manufacture of lies, and just as effective in
achieving misrepresentation. The careful and judicious
elimination of material matter from a report is just as
useful for party purposes as the manufacture of a
straight-out lie. The Political Labor League knows
the capacity of Sydney journals in both the posi-
tive and negative methods of misrepresentation, and
therefore it is not difficult to understand the
exclusion of all press representatives except those of the
League’s own organ. Still it was a mistake. There are
four daily papers in Sydney, and, supposing none of them
wished to tell the truth, the chances are they would not
all hit upon the same kind of lie. Twenty men may
follow one another into a witness box, and each tell the
same tale and stick to it if they are telling the truth;
but if they are not telling the truth, no matter how they
may agree as to the kind of lie to be told, it will almost
certainly happen that incompatible allegations will be
made. The witness of truth has but one story to'tell;
the witness who desires to deceive has an unlimited
number of lies to choose from, and the consequence is
that one liar will very often tell a tale that is utterly at
variance with that told by the other liar. It would have
shown more wisdom if the Political Labor League had
admitted as many press representatives as were pleased
to attend its conference, the larger the number the better;
even if they were all determined to suppress material
truths, the probability is they would not all suppress the
same truths, and would thus convict each other of mis-
representation. The exclusion of the press was bad
tactics.
There was an obscure item in the country news
in some of the papers the other day that was
worth noting down: Tarella, a station in the
droughtiest part of the Far West (N.S. W.),
had men out cutting “meadow hay.” Last year
and the year before, this, station, like its neigh-
bors, struggled without much success to keep its
flocks alive by cutting scrub. Then bleaching
bones were the most conspicuous feature of, the
landscape; this season the bones ar-e hidden by
waving seas of grass, 50 times more than enough
for current requirements of the stock. Such
seasons there have been before; yet practically
no attempt has been made to conserve even a little
of the wasting fodder, and when the attempt is
made even now, after the hard experiences of the
past years which ought to have taught wisdopi, it
is so much of a curiosity that it is worth talking
about. Acres have been written about irrigation
as an insurance against dry seasons; but in by far
the most of these inlands irrigation is utterly out
of the question, while the conservation of surplus
fodder is not out of the question anywhere.

About a Land Boilers’ Inspection Act an expert
writes: — - -
I knew a foundry boiler (ordinary'upright, supposed to
supply 7h.p. engine) working an emery wheel for dry-
grinding ironwork, a 32in. lathe, a drilling machine, a
facing sand mill, a planing machine, and a fan for forge
blast, all at the one time, with the steam indicator fluc-
tuating between 100 and llOlbs. The feed wat6n was sup-
plied by a plunger pump actuated by eccentric gearing
on the fly wheel shaft, and a high speed of engine conse-
quently gave a very poor delivery. Being engaged on
other work two hours before the usual starting time, I had
to exercise my talents in getting steam up, and some days
frequently found water gauge dead.,empty, even after
removing bottom plug and thrusting wire through to
remove any possible obstacle. What a mercy that
boiler had no damper plate to keep steam up. all
night! Have known boilers working every day left
uncleaned for two years: hence great danger of burnt
plates through sediment supplanting water spaee. . Did
you ever see a safety-valve weighted to blow off at 60 not
passing any steam when indicator stood at 95 ? Trouble ?
Nothing much—only the connection damaged. Did you
ever see dirty, scaly water priming in an uncertain man-
ner in the gauge-glass, rendering ft impossible to deter-
mine the water level? The boiler should have been blown
off last Christmas, only there was a special job on. A
good Boiler Act would stipulate for a regular blowing
off and cleaning, say every three months. Fusible plugs
should be cleaned on fire and water side regularly, and
fusible metal renewed every year. Would like to see all
cocks asbestos' packed or oiled regularly, and steam,
feeder, and safety valves compulsorily overhauled once a
year, and let the person who makes or supervises such
examination be held directly responsible.

Age, “ to imagine a more lame and impotent con-
clusion to the Boer war than the substitution of
Chinese for European labor on the Rand . . .
merely in order that a ring of wealthy mine-
owners should be enabled to amass further wealth.”
It is good to see the Age connecting the war and
the mineowners’ plot like this. But, then, having
done that much, the Age ought to go into sack-
cloth and ashes. Having shared in the conduct of
the war, it must also share in the responsibility
for the “conclusion to the war.” It may plead
that it “ didn’t know it was loaded ” —that it
hadn’t any idea that the thing would end like
this; Perhaps not; but the responsibility is still
there all the same. And, anyhow, if the plea of
ignorance may be more or less accepted once,
it can’t be accepted twice. A man who shoots
somebody with a didn’t-know-it-was-loaded gun
may be forgiven, even though he be despised as a

Guarantee.
She gives a hand, our Motherland, to guard the
ways of Greece,
O’er half the earth she’s sworn to fight to keep the
gates of Peace;

kens with glee
He rests beneath the shadow of the Britislr
Guarantee.

guard;

yard;

J apanee—
O! happy universe that sleeps beneath the
Guarantee.
The Kaiser hath her written bond about his
Saxonee,

spread her gamp for me ! ”

Dish up the juicy mission-man beneath the

Nore.

“ Brown’s in Town” is the same sort of
bright, frivolous, inconsequential, irresponsible
person as “Mama’s New. Husband,” whom
Stephenson’s American Musical Comedy Co. in-
troduced to Australia at Sydney Palace over a
month ago. At least, if there were any Brown to
be in town, that is the sort of Brown he would
be; but, as a matter of fact, there isn’t any
Brown. A youngster of 24 or so, principal come-
dian Stine’s only boy, has got married against
Stine’s wish, and under the name of Brown has
taken the girl to a Long Island farm-house. Here,
of course, everybody turns up, just a 3 though it
were the hub of the world ; and Stine’s son, to hide
his matrimony, starts lying; and, never having
been in politics, he makes a mess of it. Some-
times he is Brown and sometimes he isn’t; some-
times he isn’t married, and sometimes he is ; some-
times (as there has to be a Brown, because it is
Brown’s house) one obliging friend is Brawn, and
sometimes another; and so the same people get
married to two or three different people, and once
or twice to two or three at the same time, which
leads to scandal. If you think about it, it is shock-
ing nonsense; if you don’t, it is light and bright
and amusing, brisk and bustling all the time, and,
like most American stage-work, clean and whole-
some. As in the first piece the co. played,
the dancing is the best feature of all;
besides the Bicknells, who this time take
a more serious part in the comedy, Mr. Douglas
and Miss Ford show strikingly artistic work in this
line, and for a father of his tonnage Mr. Stine
ends also with a remarkably nimble foot. But
Mr. Stine’s most successful work is, in conjunction
with Miss Evans, a caricature of melodrama—
a travesty packed tight with humor. Others
worth a line for the entertainment they give include
Mr. Bates, who is Brown so far as there is any ;
Miss Marshall, the Mary Ann of the household, of
whose respectability she has grave doubts; and
Mr. Hunt, who sings a sentimental song or two —
almost the only serious things in the whole merry
performance.
** * *
That “ The Belle of New York ” has won
the popularity which its bright original humor
and music deserve was shown on Saturday night
at the Royal, Sydney, when, on revival after a
very short lapse of time, the opera attracted a
crowded audience. Every feature of the piece
was greeted . cordially—the lady with whom
marrying was a hobby, and who at the cost of 10
trips to the altar, had got a knowledge of the
etiquette of the business ; the other lady who
kicked so high that she nearly dislocated her'
digestion; Fifi Fricot, so apt to learn the
lessons of love, so anxious to be taught
how to kiss, squeeze, fondle, and sit
on the “ sympathetic knees ” of the other sex.
Familiarity has taught not contempt but liking
for these bright, phrase-making characters, which
is matched by the appreciation of Ichabod Bron-
son, Blinky Bill (with his faithful Mamie),
Snifkins, Mug, the Polite Lunatic, and other
humorous males. So crowded is “The Belle of
New York ” with bright humor and American
wit, so well treated in the matter of music, that,
with a decent orchestra, it pretty well plays itself.
At the Royal on this occasion it has the advantage
over last reyival of “slicker ” stage management)
but there are in other respects more points for criti-
cism. It is not an improvement to give the color-
less Harry Bronson an interlopated song ; as Billy
Breeze Mr. J. Hughes gives an unpleasant idea
that he is going to burst his boilers, such terribly
hard work does lie make of his song ; and if it is
desired to give Miss Minnie Hooper a better show
as Kissy Fitzgarter she should have an additional
dance, not a song, for she has extra-nimble feet,
but an undistinguished voice. Miss Vinia de
Loitte made a happy first appearance in Sydney
as Fifi Fricot ; she has a sweet, sympathetic
voice, reminiscent of that of Violet Var-
ley, and acts with dainty grace; but she
doesn’t yet know how to dance; with that
knowledge added to her other accomplishments
she should do well in comic opera. The only other
notable change in the cast was that Miss Elsie
Moore took the part of Cora Angelique and, of
course, showed as a graceful figure, but she might
have acted with a little more animation. For the
rest, Graham was a most humorous Bronson, and
Fred Leslie and Olive Lenton made, perhaps,
the brightest feature of the evening with their
street-arab antics.
#* # *
Two hundred pounds was realised at the
Wallaroo Disaster Concert at Sydney Town Hall
last Saturday night. That portion of the hall
where they seat Vice-regal persons, society and
press dead-heads was nearly uninhabited. It trans-
pired, though, that a lot of the naval and military
paid for seats but preferred “The Belle of New
York “as a spectacle. The “handy man” and
his girl made up the bulk of the audience that
crowded galleries and lower half of hall. The
civilian section of that same audience received the
“Cutlass Drill” display by bluejackets from
H.M.S. Mildura with great enthusiasm. Cut and
thrust and guard and the gleam of naked steel (on
a stage) always thrills the civilian youth. For the
rest the handy man’s girl lost her collective heart
to the wondrously physically-cultured giant of a
petty-officer who directed the drill.
*# * *
With entrepreneur Rickards’ return, stars
are falling upon the Tivoli almost as thick as
autumn leaves in Hyde Park. The Dumonds
arrived on Saturday, three of them, eccentric
musicians. The musical critic praises them for
their sweet timbre, their tender ensemble, their
intricate double-stopping, their accuracy of pitch,
and the neatness of a long descending chromatic
passage, and of an extended arpeggio in harmonios.
The gallery probably didn’t notice the arpeggio,
but it relished the rare fooling of the musical
clown of the trio, and the tenor serenade of the
singer who plays the guitar. The Cirnocs have
moved on, but Vulcana still bares her beautiful
back, and moves her wonderful muscles in the
limelight; Fanny Powers is repeating her popular
imitations of Peggy Pryde and other celebrities ;
Dewar’s dogs still play their melodrama; and
J. 0. Leetc stands at the door and surveys a full
house every night.
*# * *
Audiences of the “Eternal City” miss
one of the great charms of the piece by turning
their attention to self or neighbors when the
curtain drops on the various scenes. Attention
should merely be transferred from stage to
orchestra and Mascagni’s music. He that hath
ears to hear let him near what Mascagni sayeth
to the listeners. Let him ignore the fact
that he hath a mouth. The man and the
dog who wait without will wait till
eleven o’clock. The feminines would do well
to subordinate their tongues to their ears. As it
is, the charming preludes, interludes and serenades
don’t get a tenth of the attention they deserve,
and poor Alfred Hill, who conducts, casts re-
proachful glances at the chattering, shifting,
chocolate-eating dress-circlers. The piece is
drawing good houses. Julius Knight continues
to be intense and as like a Yarra bank orator as
any gifted mummer out of Haw-land may be.
Miss Jeffries wears regal clothes regally, and sobs
as convulsively as if it wasn’t all Hall Caine
and gammon. Arthur Wontner, as Baron Bonel-
lin, inhabits a mighty fine palace, judging by its
loggia. One wonders if ever he lets it furnished
(at a nominal rent to a good tenant without
children). When he strikes matches on the mar-
ble bust that Donna Roma has just completed one
wonders why she doesn’t use the mallet (that lies
handy) on his head. There is a quarter of a mile
of cast, and the units thereof are all good—in spite
of Hall Caine.
** * *
Miss Yvonne Leverrier, a sister of
Sydney’s scientist-violinist and University lec-
turer, gave a piano recital at Paling’s last Thurs-
day. She is an Australian who studied for five or
six years with Leschchetzsky in Vienna, and then
set up. in Paris, whether she intends returning
in a few- months after giving her countrymen a
further taste of her melody and technique.
#* * *
Miss Clare Scarr, whose concert, under
the management of Mr. Sykes, occurs on Feb.
10th in Centenary Hall, Sydney, is said by those
who have, heard her privately to own a sweet and
mounting soprano. She left Sydney some years
ago and studied under Madam Renee Richard in
Paris.' This lady, who was once mezzo-soprano
star of the Paris Opera and Covent Garden, has a
little theatre of her own for her pupils, and in it
Miss Scarr, after hard study, appeared in the
principal rdles in “ Samson et Delila,” “Carmen,”
“ Mignon,” and “Orpheus.”

“In London Town ” will hold the floor at
Melbourne Royal for a few nights only, the revival
of a horsey drama being now in earnest prepara-
tion. The present drama is remarkably free from
horse, also it is lacking in orthodox scenes of
heroism. Walter Baker plays a noble character
whose advanced age and enormous wealth unfit
him for reckless deeds. Baker is philanthropic,
picturesque and credulous, and his rich, musical
voice harmonises with his alleged banking account,
but it isn’t he who saves Miss Ross from a watery
frave, or manslaughters the principal villain ; Bland
[olt, as a comic-relief sailor with a long lost mother
in the workhouse, is the real hero of the piece,
although his rescue of the drowning female seems
to be rather a soft snap. A body comes floating
along on top of a painted river as buoyantly as
the corpse m a boot-trunk tragedy, whereupon
Bland makes himself scarce for a moment before
returning to observation with Miss Ross in his
arms. Last Saturday he was a bit late at the
rescue, and the floating figure appeared to crook
its forefinger in a significant manner, as though
reminding Bland of his job. Punctuality is the
soul of sensation-drama business.
#* * *
At the Opera House improved patronage
waits upon an improved programme, and the star
of Rickards seems to be redeeming its former
position in the heavens. Melbourne feels not
unkindly towards the Empire Girls, who nod
their head and legs in unison, and otherwise
behave as one girl multiplied by six. The internal
government of this Girl Commonwealth is good.
Their unity is strength—about 95 per cent, of their
total strength. And their air of universal friend-
ship may be commended to the imitation of many
different half-dozens of girls who assemble and
meet together for purposes of work and wages.
Clarke and Hamilton are still giving the capital
“ turn ” that begins with broad musical comedy
and ends in a limelight dance. The colored
biograph is satisfactory, and a new contralto sings
on a go-as-you-please system with her hands
behind her back, which attitude of vocalism is
probably studied from illustrations of somebody’s
patent corset.

The Bijou panto, lost the run of Harry
Shine once or twice last week, but recovered that
best bf she-male comedians on Saturday, in con-
sideration of which joyful circumstance Shine
will take a benefit the last night of the season.
Also the Australian author and the bona fide native
producer are conspiring to take a benefit in the
interests of Literature and Art, or out of respect
for their own commercial principles. Anyway,
the joint parents of the panto, promise a special
interlude for the occasion.

The Wild Cat Column.
Australian Treasurer George Turner is having
his “quiet talk” this week with the State Treasurers.
There are two things in particular to talk about —pay-
ment for the public buildings, &e., taken over by the
Federal Government, and the taking over of the debts.
As to the public buildings the State Premiers, when they
met in Sydney, asked for cash. That, of course, is out of
the question, unless the States use the money’ to pay off
the loans with which the taken-over works were built.
In that case the Federal Government would have to
borrow at once, and this isn’t a good time to borrow.
Moreover, it would be an unnecessarily round-about pro-
cedure. The only plan suitable for the times is to take
over as much of the debt as the works represent.
But when it takes over that much of the debt it might
just as well take over the whole lot. For three years
more, and so long as may be decided, separate accounts
have to be kept for each State: and if the whole debt
were taken over, the simple plan would have to be, and
easily could be, followed of charging each State with its
own’interest. If the present amount of “Federal re-
funds” w’ould not cover this charge, then that amount
would have to be made larger by increased taxation. If
that cannot be accomplished, then the railway revenues
might be tapped for whatever shortage there may be.
As loans fall due they would be renewed by the Federal
Government at a uniform rate of interest. That rate
might not, for a start, be as low as it ought to be, but it
will certainly be. lower than any single State could get
the money for. And it would go lower with the growth
of the sinking fund, upon which the Australian
Parliament would certainly insist. A large con-
version scheme would follow when the market was
ripe for it. There is no need of an elaborate
scheme of “ taking over the debts in fact, any attempt
at fixing up an elaborate scheme will most likely end in
nothing at all for a good many more years. The “ blot ”
by which separate accounts may be kept for as long as
the Parliament pleases provides the easiest, simplest,
and most certain way’ out of the difficulty.
One obstacle has been the refusal of the States
to agree to borrow in the future only with the Federal
authority’s approval. Prime Minister Deakin put it,
even before he was Prime Minister, that the Federal
Government could not be expected to take over debts if
it did not have some control over the mortgaged assets ;
in other words, it could not allow the bill of sale to be
double-banked. But, even if the State Governments
will not give up this power to borrow “ for the
development of their own States,” as they put it, there
is a way out. The Federal Government has practically
unlimited powers of direct taxation ; and while it has
those powers, it has command of the largest and best
asset. If, then, the State Governments persist in borrow-
ing, the Federal Government can persist in paying back.
It can do that by direct taxation, applying the results to
purchasing the new bonds. It can’t take over the new
debts, but it can buy them up in this way ; and when the
people find that, after all, they are paying certain taxes
for no other reason than that their demoralised State
Governments are floating loans, they will not be long
in deciding that it would be better business to do with-
out the loans, and to apply the taxes direct to the
carrying out of necessary works. The Federal Govern-
ment has, in short, the whip-hand. It can put Australia
on the track of sound finance and solvency if it wants to.
And sooner or later it must do it. That the present
system, or want of system, should continue indefinitely
is unthinkable. The Government has the power, and as
the guardian of all-Australian interests it has the respon-
sibility. The only question is whether it will act now or
wait till things get worse, and it becomes more difficult
to act.

The accounts are still presented in their old form, but
another cleaning-up is In sight, and when that operation
is through the figures will look very different. It has
been obvious for a long while that a good many of the
items in the balance sheet were something between a
tragedy and a joke. Capital (paid) stood ana still stands
at £1,168,041. On that amount and reserves as at July
Ist the business profit was equal to 3s. 2d. per cent, per
annum. The whole year’s profit was equal to about 7s. 9d.
per cent. For the past four years (including the term when
a lucky hit was made in Queensland) the average has been
11s. sd. If 4 per cent, is a fair return (it is, as a matter of
fact, much under the recent average of Australian
banks), then the whole of the capital would have to he
written off as lost, and a full third of the reserves into
the bargain. Even on a 3 per cent, basis, last year’s
business profits indicated a total capital of only £178,000.
If last year’s profits were a fair test it would
therefore be necessary for the A.J.S., in order to put
itself on a sound basis, not only to write off the whole of
its paid capital, but to make a call as well. (The
authorised capital is £1,566,020, so that there is £397,979
to call.) But, probably, though most of the other Aus-
tralian banks made much larger profits than usual last
year, the A.J.S., being mainly interested in way-out
pastoral properties, which felt the drought the worst,
could not reasonably be expected to do so. Upon this
probability a hope may be built that, though an amount
equal to the total of the paid capital will have to dis-
appear, nothing further will have to be called. Presum-
ably the reserves will disappear first; then the capital
may be written down to £250,000. This would mean a
writing-off of £1,244,153. It is heroic, but it is no use
delaying longer.
During the A.J.S. Bank’s past half-year the
bank and the Western Lafcids Commissioners have been
going through the Far West (N.S.W.) pastoral securities
held by the bank. Under the Western Lands Act the
lessees out in that dry country may have certain rent
and term-of-tenure concessions, provided that they have
a reasonable chance of eventually getting their heads
above water. In other words, provided that, among
other things, the mortgagee writes off enough of the
debt to give the mortgagor a chance, the State will
make certain concessions. The A. J.S.’s writing-off under
this arrangement will amount to £344,112. _ It _ would Da.
interesting to know what proportion this is of the
original debt. But there are a lot of interesting things
that you can’t find out from balance-sheets. Pending,
however, the general clean-up, this £344,112 is not yet
formally written off.
The whole position compares as follows with
that of 12 months back :

New deposits, notes, bills, and balances due other banks,

About £260,000 of “A” deposits were due on Jan. Ist;
even with that paid out, there would still be almost 100
per cent, of liquid assets against prior-charge liabilities.
This, no doubt, means comparatively low earning capa-
city ; but, then, it is the business of the A.J.S. at present
to inspire confidence rather than to make immediate
profits.

In the last eighteen months £166,589 of depreciated

(and disregarding any expenses) it has bought back its
own 1.0. U. at a discount of between 34 and 35 per cent.
Still, if it is true that one can get comfort out of the mis-
fortunes of others, the depositors who lose 35 per cent,
may do well to remember that the shareholders are in a
very good way of formally losing just about 100 per cent.
They have already lost it in every other way than
formally.

The Australian Insurance and 'Banking
Record, Melbourne, 20th June, 1903 : The statement
of the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society s new
business shows a substantial increase in both the total
and the average amount per policy, and the accounts
show a steady increase in premium income, a higner rate
of interest,’a lower expense ratio, and an almost
stationary position in payments to policyholders, a
very substantial sum has in consequence been added to
the accumulated funds. The accounts betoken careful
and assiduous administration with an improvement in
material features.”*
* •
The Cathcart Proprietary G.M. Co. (Victoria)
has lately placed an order with the Austral Otis Company
of South Melbourne, for a complete electric lighting and
power plant, including also boilers, feed pump, surface
condenser and air pump. The new plant will furnish
sufficient electricity for motors to drive puddlers, the
winding engine and a three throw pump, the latter
being capable of lifting 25,000 gallons of water per hour
against a total head of 215 feet. The generating set will
consist of a vertical enclosed compound engine of lbU
8.H.P., coupled direct to a Ganz three phase generator
of 110 killowatts output. The electrical portion of the
plant will be carried out in the latest three phase system
of Ganz and Co., with 500 volts working pressure and a
frequency of 60 periods. We believe this is the first
instance in Victoria of an individual mine adopting
electricity as a motive power for pumping, hoisting ana
puddling machinery, and no doubt when the plant is in
operation, which it will be in about six months’ time a
number of other companies will follow the example of
the Oathcart Company.

British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with*about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

But the whole of this reduction has sprung from a rather
serious falling off in new business. Only 2198 policies,
assuring £425,254, were issued as against 2736 policies
•SJNjrjPfl £514,423 in 1902, and 3438 policies assuring
£703,759. in_ 1901. Expenses of management alone,
as distinguished from commissions, medical fees,
travelling, &c., were equal to £9 12s. 6d per
cent, of income from premiums and interest last year
as against £9 3s. 6d. in 1902 and £9 9s. lOd. in 1901. The
movement shown above is therefore not all that it looks
on the surface For part of the increase, the Vic. income-
tax is responsible. Insurance co.’s have met the alleged
Australian depression in two ways ; some of them, fear-
fully nervous about loss of prestige, and therefore dread-
ing a downward movement in the amount of new business
written, have made a special effort, at a very special
expense in some cases, and consequently show a substan-
tial increase in expense rate ; others have decided not to
force business at bad times, and have taken things even
more quietly than usual. The Widows’ Fund is one of
the latter. Yet, though it has evidently made no special
effort, and though the total therefore shows a large falling
away, what it did get cost rather more than usual. Com-
missions, medical fees, and travelling expenses absorbed
136 per cent, of the year’s premiums on the new policies
written, compared with 133 per cent, in 1902 and 117 per
cent, in 1901. This cannot be regarded as a very satis-
factory experience, though to largely increase the expense
rate in the struggle to get as much new business as was
got formerly, might very easily be even less satisfactory.
It is simply a question of choosing between two evils.
The next calculation gives a more cheerful re-
sult :

The reserves consist of reserve fund £110,450 and undi-
vided profit £8456. In addition to this there is a reserve
for the replacement of meters, £7194, not included in the
above. A weakness of the situation is the absence of
any investment apart from the business. Not only the
whole of the capital and reserves, but £500,111 of “ tem-
porary loans, debentures, etc.,” is sunk in mains,
buildings, coal, lamp-posts, etc.; and as something may
conceivably happen some day to very greatly reduce the
value of most of these things, the present system seems
to be a good deal open to objection. Certainly it would
be difficult to get hold of investments that would yield
as high a rate of interest. In its entire enterprise the co.
.has £1,554,637 invested ; and last year’s return on this
amount was equal to £7 4s. per cent. A co. which, by
extraordinary good fortune, such as.is not likely to come
the way of any other co., can show a return of that size
ought to be doing something considerable in the way of a
sinking fund, and it is doing nothing. It has got the
borrowing disease almost as badly as the State has had,
and, possessing about the most gorgeous monopoly in this
continent, it has had less excuse for it than even the
Owe’Sullivan could urge. On the paid capital (£750,000)
and reserves as at the commencement of the half-year
the profits were at the rate of just under 14 per cent, per
annum.

The reserves consist of reserve fund £19,500, dividend
equalisation fund (a new and not commendable feature)
£LOOO, commission reserved account £13,228, and un-
divided profit £948. The commission reserved account
has received the large addition of £4200 during the year;
this amount is not shown by the co. as profit, but is
necessarily included as such in the above table. This
latter large and unexplained accession of profit, accom-
panied by the fact that there has been no writing off
during the year, establishes for the co. an easy record.
On the paid capital of £25,000 the return is equal to a
dazzling £42 15s. per cent. ; on paid capital and reserves
combined, it is equal to £2O 6s. per cent., as against
£l5 8s 6d. in 1902. Last year the Ballarat Trustees Co.
showed £l5 15s. lOd.; but the Sydney Perpetual easily
beats that figure again, and so continues at the head of
the list. There is nothing, however, to indicate
that this may be reckoned as normal; no doubt
the profit shown by the profit and loss account
(£6319) gets nearer that mark. Yet even that is equal
to about 12J per cent on total shareholders’ funds. The
amount of business in the co.’s hands is growing rapidly.
In 1895 it was £2,424,413 : in 1902, £4,653,450 ; last year,
£5,453,002. From an investing point of view the liability
of £9 10s. per share is uncomfortable, though with the
rapid increase of reserves the uncomfortable feeling grows
less ; from the client’s viewpoint, shareholders’ liability
is, of course, grateful and comforting.

The paid capital is £200,000 ; on .that and reserves a total

co. now ranks with the greatest of British fire insurance

income. It has now, for instance, passed the Guardian
(excluding accident and life business), and is almost up
to the Atlas.

The co.’s paid capital is £lOO,OOO ; and the half-year’s
return has been at the rate of only £2 15s. 9d. percent, per
annum on this. The co., unlike the State railway system,
is dependent upon one industry and one district; and
the past few years have been particularly bad for
that industry and that district. Nevertheless, in the
past three years the co., with only this one bad dis-
trict to draw upon, has made its 4 per cent., and £99
over to go to reserves. This isn’t an argument in favor
of private railways ; but it is an argument in favor of im-
porting into the management of public railways some of
those things which go to make the private lines a finan-
cial success.

During the year the co. has plunged in station properties;

at the end of 1903 it stood tor £146,511. Practically the
whole of these transactions have been on credit, with the
result that, apart from drafts on consignments, the co.

heavy responsibility to take, especially upon horrowed
money, secured apparently on the co.’s assets ; though as
the season has turned out so well and as the meat
market is very firm, the speculation will most likely
prove good.

is descended from the royal Stewarts. Which, if
true, shows the ascent of man. From a king to a
Radical Senator ! It would prove evolution.

THE COST OF UNDRESS.
HE: “ What! Twenty pounds for that! Seems
to me the less you women put on the more
you spend.”
Victorian clerics are holding a sort of
informal referendum on the question'of allowing
Scripture teaching in the State schools. Voters
before saying “yes” to the proposed innovation
should study the results of Scripture teaching in
the N.S.W. schools. The N.S.W. teacher used
to give a Scripture lesson adapted from the Bible,
but it was objected that that gave an opportunity
for his personal sectarian prejudices to be intruded.
So teachers were ordered to give out to the pupils
the Bible, impure and unadulterated, and the
consequences in mixed classes are embarrassing.
The safest course is that now ruling in Victoria—
to leave religion absolutely out of the school
routine. Religion, after all, is the churches’ busi-
ness and not the State’s.
“F.": Wondered why so many ancient
fentlemen, apparently' members of the N.S.W,
Fpper House, held frequent informal caucuses in
the Sydney Palace Gardens, in the summer time.
Found the explanation in a long flight of stone
steps, passing down which weregirlsinsummer.cos-
tumes with thedeclining sun blazing behind them.
* * » «
Theiiew Gov.-General, via his A.D.C., on
arrival spread the usual dose of jam over Australia.
To the reporters hanging around Government
House front door, agog for his first impressions
about the place, he said :—•
Their Excellencies were delighted with the way the
people of Melbourne received them. They thought
that the landing at St. Kilda was an exceedingly
picturesque ceremony, and the drive along St. Kilda road
had pleased them extremely. As a city Melbourne im-
presses them with its importance, being far larger than
they had anticipated.
And so on. It will be an awful shock to Melbourne
someday, when a Governor with a liver that’s been
churning for six weeks on the ocean, and has got too
stiff to be polite, uses some plain language about
keeping a respectable elderly man dodging about
on a gunboat, and landing him in an out-of-the-
way little watering place, giving him a long dusty
drive, so as to allow various fat little mayors to
air themselves in his presence at various stages,
and their wives to throw cabbages at his wife in
the vice-regal carriage. That Governor won’t be
popular.
• • * *
W. T. Stead’s latest London venture is
The Daily Paper. It’s to be run largely on the
given-away-with-a-pound-of-tea principle. Those
who pay a guinea a year (or two shillings a month)
will get the paper delivered at home free by one
of a brigade of young girl messengers (who are to
deliver the paper and to receive any suggestions
for its improvement from subscribers), a portfolio
of colored piotures, the Review of Reviews and
some other monthly, and the use of a local contre
as a club for various purposes. When the
project was mooted there was a very heated
discussion in various religious and semi-religious
quarters as to the moral danger incurred by the
young girl messengers who were to call at houses
to deliver the paper: unscrupulous men, it was
pointed out, might subscribe for the worst of
motives, and offer the worst of suggestions. A
very cute advertiser is-Mr. Stead !

Once in the Dim Remote the Hebrew
community in an Australian capital was much
scandalised by the unseemly doings of a Gentile
girl who beoame an alleged convert to the
faith and married a wealthy gentleman.
She was larky and yellow-haired from
the first, and from being only a
tall person with a suspicion of
hair-dye she developed in-
to a first-class scoffer.
The climax came when
she tore from the door
the bit of parchment
with the sacred texft
upon it and threat-
ened, if it were re-
placed, to nail a pork
chop on top of it.
She gathered in most
of her husband’s
money before he died
and made Judaism a
thing of mirth in the
household. When
she was left a widow
she became plain
Gentile again, and
ate the ham of back-
sliding in public.
That was a Ilong
time ago, and
Judaism lost sight
of her for years.
It has now learned
that she married a
Gentile, who
whacked her, and
lost the old Jewish
husband’s money to
other Jews on the
racecourse, and then
left her a widow the
second time—a very hard-up widow whose account
with the Badly Chosen People is about square now.
Everything comes to him who waits, and Moses
and Aaron can afford to wait. Theirs is a tough
race, and Time blunts its teeth on the three
golden balls and the bookmaker’s bag.

Not long ago a prominent Bendigo J.P.
was about to sentence two accused persons, a man
and a woman. Occurred to him to ask 'the prose-
cuting sergeant if anything was known of the
prisoners. “ Yes, y’r wushup,” said Mr. Ser-
geant, whipping over the record book; “one
of them has been three times charged with being a
common street-walker.” “Eli ! —what’s that?
Which one?” Sergeant (solemnly): “The
woman, y’r wushup.”

Sydney Homoeopathic Hospital struggles
along on the basis of voluntary subscriptions. It
put through 574 patients last year, and will con-
tinue the voluntary basis, though the loaves and
fishes of Government subsidy tempt longing eyes.
IN THE UNDRESSING ROOM.
1ST. HUSSAR: “ What did you do before you took
to the stage?”
2ND. HUSSAR : “Minded my own business.”
“Pucha”: The Maoriland love of holi-
days is often a subject of attack by the virtuous,
seldom or never of defence by the wicked.
Holidays and not work are life; holidays are
sacred things. In the darker ages (the Puri-
tanical ones), holiday-making was a thing
accursed. Men worked blindly. By cramping
the healthy enjoyment of life, Puritanism leads
directly to greed. R. L. Stevenson observed it
of Scotland, but the remark is true the world
over. Combine Puritanism and modern com-
mercialism, and you promote the least happiness
of the greatest number. When men are sensible
enough to demand high wages, they soon learn to
depjand short hours and frequent holidays. All
this talk about the dignity of labor is unavailing.
Let a man try walking, cycling, fishing, or, say,
sitting under a tree and smoking. Let him try it
for a week, and, if possible, for a fortnight, and
then let him candidly answer if holiday-making is
not the nobler gospel.
Some poor creature writes in an English
weekly—a popular working-man’s penny weekly,
too :
Some little time ago, it was my great privilege to be
included in a group photograph in which His Majesty
the King figured. I was one of a house party* which the
King honored by joining for a brief space. It may be
taken for granted that when His Gracious Majesty con-
sents to be one of a group photograph, he does so only
on the absolute understanding that the personages with
w'hom he is taken are above reproach from a social and
from various other standpoints.
There’s not much hope for a country when that
can be written —and published in a people’s paper.
Now that smallpox has broken-out
over the water, even the devoutest Van -
demonian feels rest fully glad that the
medical officer has been given such full
Eowers in the matter of Tasmania’s public
ealth. The Launceston outbreak, with
its tangle of hateful blunders made by
ignorant or prejudiced officialdom disgusted
everyone with the old trumpery order.
The powers conferred by the new Health
Act are delight-fully complete. The thing
it cannot do, when it becomes necessary to
suppress another epidemic, has yet to be dis-
covered. Curiously, the bitterest opponent
of the new health law in the Assembly was
an undertaker.
• * * *
Machinery is now entering into com-
petition with the coal-hewer. The Pelaw
Main Colliery (N.S.W.) recently put in coal-
cutting machines, and all the miners have
been discharged, with an offer of re-employ-
ment as fillers, wheelers, surface hands, &c.,
at 7s. a day. As the men were previously
earning 12s. a day, the drop is a big one.
* * * *
The new coupon system for the tele-
phones in N.S.W. (by which non-subscribers
were to be made to pay threepence for the
use of a subscriber’s machine, the subscriber
getting one-third and the Postal Dept, the
other two-thirds of the amount) hasn’t
turned out a success as yet. So far, not
one application has been made for admis-
sion to the “ coupon list,” but, if the Dept,
carries out its threat to suddenly close down
on any subscriber who allows a deadhead
to use his instrument, it will perhaps make
a difference.
* » * *
Woman’s intense admiration for the
bookmaker, the financier, and the Stock
Exchange man, has often been noted. It is
the natural development of her old love for
a predatory mate. When tails were just
going out of fashion for human wear, woman
gave her young affections to the suitor who
could hunt and rob best—that was a natural
instinct leading her to comfort and the race
to betterment; now predatory methods have
changed, but woman’s nature proves eternal.
* * * *
A Melbourne clerk wails in the Argus
that. thousands of clerks are compelled to
slave in Victoria for £1 and 15s. a week
and that they are supposed to dress well
and pay their just debts. The clerk has
mostly himself to blame. Often he is more
stupidly Tory than the man who graciously
gives him 15s. a week. Let him try trades-
unionism.
Mr. James Graham was not a particularly
brilliant success as Mayor of Sydney, but the
clean section of the public would be glad to see
him back again, if only to enforce the anti-spitting
by-law, which of late has been entirely ignored
in Sydney streets.
• • * •
Down the South Coast (N.S.W.) there is
a farm of 20 acres carried on entirely by three
orphan spinsters. The baby has just turned 75.
They dig, chop, milk, sow, reap, and slaughtei
without man’s assistance. No doctor has been
called in since a measles epidemic of 20 years ago.

“ G.R.” : The disappearance of the male
public-school teacher in Maoriland and every
other land (8., 14/1 /’O4) is due to the low salaries
paid to teachers. To be employed in a sweated
profession is a sort of disgrace. The schools need
the best intellects of both sexes, but you can’t feed
intellect on bread-and-dripping pay.

TO PHYLLIS SMOKING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Two red lips and two soft fingers
And a cigarette between,
And the curling smoke that lingers
Where the cigarette has been.
Still, though banished from her lips,
Happy on her finger-tips.
Two bright eyes with glance pathetio,
Seem appealingly to say—
“ Don’t deny that it’s Aesthetic,"
Be its morals what they may.
Ah, she knows, the sweet coquette,
How to use a cigarette.
Some fine day, I wonder whether—
Not just now, but by-and-bye—
We’ll be smoking one together,
Just as an economy.
And if all the smoke goes missing ?-
Why, then, there is always kissing 1

Troubles.
The Sydney daily papers record a secret
meeting of employers to discuss the Arbitration
Act against which there is just now an organised
campaign by capitalism—and forget to say any-
thing severe about the employers excluding the
press. . What is a sin in trie Labor party is a
virtue in the Loaf party. It is rumored that the
meeting agreed upon inaugurating a series of
little “lock-outs,” to test the efficiency of the
Act and the degree of irritation against it which
can be aroused.
Some N.S.YV. employers have, or profess to
have, curious ideas as to the Arbitration Act.
One big Sydney hotelkeeper, the other day,
trumpeted through the press that, in consequence
of the Act, he intended to shut up his dining-
rooms, and to dismiss all his boarders. The last
would be a quite supererogatory sacrifice. No
award of the Arbitration Court, so far given,
affects hotelkeepers, except where they carry on a
restaurant business. If an hotelkeeper confines his
catering to his boarders lie does not come under
the operations of the common rule promulgated
by the court on the application of the Hotel, Club,
Restaurant, and Caterers’ Employees’ Union.
Further if that hotelkeeper “dismisses his
boarders” and makes his business a purely drink-
selling one, he will run up against the Licensing
Act, with probably great damage to himself. As
a licensed victualler his contract with the State
binds him to provide food and lodging for the
travelling public. It is a cruel thing, Socialistic
legislation, and very hampering to private enter-
prise. All. hotelkeepers should unite to put it
down —beginning with the restrictive law which
prevents anyone from competing in their business
of liquor-selling.
» * « ♦
N.S.W. Arbitration Act is really in some
danger from the slanderous campaign against it
in the daily press ; and the workers should make
a special effort to rally to its help. If the law in
regard to wife-beating were attacked every day in
the newspapers; if every case of wife-beating
were cited as a proof that the law was useless, and
every hoodlum at the’same time urged to wallop
his old woman just to vindicate his independence,
there would probably be quite a lot of bruised
female in the community ; for, after all, it’s public
opinion as much as the policeman that guards the
Eeace.. The Tory newspapers, and the Sydney
>.T. in particular, are doing their utmost to
encourage the people to break the Arbitration
law, and their efforts are having some effect.

Transportation of criminals from England
to New South Wales ceased in 1840, to Queens-
land in 1849 (the contingent from Sydney Harbor),
to Van Diemen’s Land (now Tasmania) in 1853,
to Western Australia in 1808, No criminals were
ever transported to Victoria. An employer in
Van Diemen’s Land wrote in 1850 to a friend in
England: “Even in our small menage our cook
has committed murder, our footman murder, and
our housemaid bigamy.” The total number of
British convicts to Australia from 1788 until 1808
was 131,101; of these 110,842 were males and 20,319
females. It was about this time Charles Lamb
used to write to his friend Baron Field asking
affectionately “how the hemp crop was getting
on,” and if the colonists didn’t find it dull having
nothing to do but picking one another's pockets.
Cape Colony escaped the taint of convictism
(although proclaimed a penal colony in 1849) by
the struggle in 1850 against the unloading of the
Neptune convicts. The ship lay from September,
1849, to February, 1850, in Simon’s Bay, and then
proceeded to Tasmania.

up as a speaker, and read for about half-an-hour
off foolscap. Just so much dough dumped on the
crowd, and he never “got a hand.” Madden looked

Why not Deakin or Trenwith ? M'Eacharn got

could not say aught about the soul of the thing.

The low state of the Melbourne matrimonial
market had evidence enough (if any doubting
Thomas required it) in the swarm of girls
that descended on the South Wharf the
other day to see a bachelor medico weigh
anchor for the island of abrupt eruptions.
The darlings positively wept in many
cases, and when they didn’t weep they
wiped away a sigh. The sight of so
much sorrowful girl at the young man’s
departure suggested years of cruel
separation, of banishment perhaps to
some St. Helena where he would suffer
solitary existence on the Rock of Despair.
Yet he was merely passing hence for a
few weeks to go geyser-hunting in Maori-
land, and the tears were probably evoked
by the thought that one of the enemy
might capture him there. The young
man, it needn’t be mentioned, is abnormally
rich.
It takes a globe-trotter to make sure of
her man. The latest idea is to have a
betrothal photograph taken. You thus nail the
man down to the counter.

Death of Graham Berry recalls the cham-
pion mean act of any Australian Parliament.
Like all other men who devoted themselves
absolutely to politics, Berry amassed no money,
indeed made no money. He worked 16 hours a
day for his country at a living wage, and when old
age and the cold glance of the constituencies fell
on him hp found himself poor. Victorian Parlia-
ment had just enough grace to recognise the call
to do something for its old servant, to know that
he couldn’t be left to beg at the street corners, and
made him the gift of a small annuity—£soo
a year to be exact—and then smirched what
should have been an act of grace with loathly
meanness by restricting the annuity to the old
man’s life. He had a wife who had borne him
children, and his declining days must have been
embittered by the thought that with his death her
livelihood failed. Man and wife are one ; to feed
one and to starve the other is to starve both. But
practically that is what the Victorian Parliament
did —assured for Berry his loaf, but carefully
stipulated that, should she survive him, there was
to be no loaf for Mrs. Berry. Even now it is not
too late to redeem in some measure that graceless
meanness by making provision for the dead states-
man’s widow.
Next to Mr. Reid trying to be funny the
most pathetic sight in the Commonwealth is
owned by Launceston (Tas.). The local authori-
ties advertise, among the wonders they have to
show to visitors, “ a small collection of wild
animals'’ in the public gardens. There is one
large brown bear, very frayed at the edges ; one
fancy colored baboon, moping by himself ; one
cage of ring-neck doves, and one of Australian
birds, mostly white cockatoos (no longer white);
a kangaroo or so ; an occasional emu, and a few
other poor unfortunates, with here and there a
dilapidated empty cage. But the most pathetic
exhibit is hidden away in one corner, where no one
ever seems to go. The cage is a large affair; the
sides and roof are wire netting, built round two
stumps, whereon perch four of the most miser-
able, woe-begone and moulty eagles that ever
were hatched. In one corner is a small shelter
shed, while all round is the blessed air and free-
dom-separated from them by thin wire. Now
and then the wretched prisoners glance at you
vacantly, but ever their eyes wander to the top of
the cage, trying to unravel the mystery of the
almost invisible wire that lets the air to them,
but keeps them from the air.

There is virtue, it seems, even in shoddy.
The Hospital points out that modern dress fabrics
which wear out in a little while are much less
likely to spread disease than the old-fashioned
silks and laces and cloths, handed down from
generation to generation.
“Burglar Bill” (who naturally takes a
professional interest in the subject):
Administration of the system of identification by
flnff er prints should not be placed in charge of the
ordinary police and prison officials. Rather, a large-
minded, brainy expert should be put in charge of the
working of the system of identification. The man who
has spent his life in the police or prison service has
a natural longing for a conviction which makes him
totally unfitted for the work. He would insensibly work it
in the interest of convictions.

In the Transvaal now the Royal coat of

A Sydney man tells of a weird experience
on the southern mail. He had tipped the porter,
hoping to monopolise a compartment, but soon
heard a woman’s voice asking if he were the only
occupant. She wasn’t bad looking. She came in
and expressed a hope that “ they should have the
carriage to themselves as far as Albury.” Then
after a chat she said “it was time to turn in, and
before he could hurl himself out of the window
she had commenced to take her hair down.
“ When she started to unfasten her front buttons,
he goes on, “I swooned away. On recovering
consciousness I noticed she had donned a little
pink jacket and was engaged in pulling off her
boots. She asked if I was ready to have the light
out, and stood upon the seat and pulled the night-
cap over the lamp. Then she lay down, spread
her rug over her, and said ‘ Good night.’ And only
then I felt safe. In the morning she took her spare
hair and other fixings into the lavatory, and
presently emerged in full rig. When subse-
quently expostulating with the porter, he smd,
‘Didn’t you know her? Why, that was M's.
, the woman’s rights lecturer.’ ” . And that
man begins to think there is something in women’s
rights after all.

A special reporter of Reynolds’ News-
paper (London) gives a gloomy account of Cosme
settlement: —
As a Socialistic settlement Cosme is a failure ; as a
settlement under Communistic principles Cosme is a
fraud. Prom the start it has been kept going only with
the money brought in by individuals who had come from
England ‘and Australia, and who, having parted with
their money, found that the whole show is owned ana
ruled by a small clique. Cosme is not even a Democratic
settlement, let alone Socialistic. Cosme, as it is at
present, is just an estate belonging to the Lane family.
William Lane owns the land, and the colony owes him
10,585d015. ; John Lane is chairman and practically ruler
of the colony ; Ernest Lane (another brother recently
arrived at Cosme) helps to make up the members of the
family ; five or six men of the pioneer type serve as
hangers-on to the Lanes ; and the rest are nowhere.

Australia is evidently a sanatorium for
siok Governors-General. Lord Hopetoun was an
invalid returned to this country for the benefit
of his health, which had been so delicate that
when he formerly made his debut, as Governor of
Victoria, he fainted from the fatigue of his first
levee. Lord Northcote carried his arm in a sling
(rheumatism) within a week of his arrival at
Melbourne.

Canon Boyce, discussing the site of the
Rev. Johnson’s wattle and dab church, the first
erected in Sydney, says it was built out of John-
sjn’s pocket. But Johnson’s handwriting shows
he did not consider the expense his, and, on the
Governor refusing to pay the bill, he dunned the
Colonial Office until the cash was re-paid to him.
That first Sydney church was thus paid for by the
British taxpayer.
STORY-TELLERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We writers share this common lot with Fate :
Give us a book of many stainless pages
And we shall write like world-experienced sages
Of man, his love, his hopes, his hate ;
But, smilingly, Oblivion, man’s friend,
Consigns our work to darkness in the end.
And so, when Fate a new existence spies,
She straightway writes a sweet or bitter story—
No new thing in it, but a repetition hoary
Of ancient love and laughter, sobs and sighs ;
It matters not, for in the end comes Death
And wipes her story out with one fell breath.
RODERIC QUINN.
IN THE HOSPITAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
H.S.W
The narrow beds in silent row
Shone white beneath the moon ;
The night nurse moving to and fro
Hummed an old dancing tune.
The city’s din was faint and far,
Yet there were some could hear
The noise of theatre and bar,
And glasses clinking cheer.
They dimmed the light above his head,
The nurse beside him stayed,
They put the red screen round his bed—
We shuddered and we prayed.
And when down that long corridor
Dawn walked with rosy bloom,
Two wardsmen on a stretcher bore
A dead man from the room
V.J.D.
SANCTUARY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Perchance the night is but the magic closing
Of some vast flower in sleep,
Beneath whose mighty dome the earth’s reposing
Is infinitely deep.
And maybe Dawn is but the blossom waking,
With petals upward curled,
'Neath which the outer glory inward breaking
To Day transforms the world.

Miss Helena Rubenstein (says the Australasian),
who has rooms at 243 Collins-street, Melbourne, is intro-
ducing the new skin food, known as “Valaze,” prepared
by Dr. Lykushi. the famous Russian skin specialist. It is
claimed by the inventor and Miss Rubenstein from expe-
rience—that if the instruction given in her book be fol-
lowed, anyone disfigured by wrinkles, freckles, or any
skin blemish whatever, will be delighted with the results
obtained after a course of treatment. An advantage of
“ Valaze ” is that it does not remain on the surface of the
skin, where it can be seen, but is absorbed, and creates a
healthy condition, the result being that looseness is re-
moved, and the skin made firm and healthy. Miss
Rubenstein declares “ Valaze ” to be absolutely the only
genuine imported skin food. Her little work is illustrated
by a number of diagrams, showing the method of apply-
ing the food. Further particulars will be found in our
advertising columns.*
A woman’s crowning glory is no longer her
hair—it’s her figure when encased in a P.D. corset
mould. The male connoisseur doesn’t turn his head
because of a stray chestnut ringlet under a flash millinery
design, but he does turn, and, with a yearning eye,
follow the lovely figure that boasts poetic lines, and is,' in
fact, the embodiment of beauty by the grace of a P.D.
shape. If all women only knew its magic ! The stout
may be made lean, and the scraggy develop curves
through the agency of a P.D. Varieties. There are
over a hundred of them, from the trifling ribbon
and lace and whalebone of a mere waist
supporter for a naturally shapely figure to the
heavily boned and steeled work of art that is the
salvation of the bulky marm. With but just a little
judicious care and the correct measurement of the form,
the right P.I). is selected, and if the fair and inexpressive
divinity can’t look like a Venus de Medici or the modern
equivalent for the classic Grace she’s not worth the lace
she’s tied in. For, now the P.D. manufacturers have de-
termined out of pure philanthropy to help towards the
moulding of all female physical grace, and if the average
She neglects to look too sweet for anything in a straight-
fronted, spring-hip, grip-evervwhere P.D., its because of
her own foolishness. Or perhaps she is a Christian
Ehdeavorer, who would rather mankind bilious than
amorous. But the healthy-minded, moderately co-
quettish woman takes to her P.D. as a duckling takes
to water.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 2, 1904.
My Dear Arini.—
Miss Crossley, now about arriving at the Cape,
gave her Kalgoorlie concert in an iron shed with
the temperature at 130 or so. Other of her achieve-
ments were standing godmother to the child of a
former associate of hers in Dr. Strong’s Australian
choir, and attending a crabbing picnic near Perth,
and a wild ride on a Fire Brigade’s steam engine.
Minister Kingsmill and wife gave the picnic, and
Ada caught crabs all day in the raging heat of sun
and a bush fire. Party returned to Perth nigh to
midnight, the town being brilliantly illuminated
from the fire burning on Mt. Eliza.
There is sometimes another explanation than
the simple one of ‘ ‘ lie ” to the break-down,
under cross-examination of a girl’s story (the
sort of story that is best left without too close
a description), a correspondent claims in a letter,
and gives in proof her personal experience. She
(a type-writer) Was passing through a park at
night on the way home from her office—a rush of
work had kept the staff late—when she was
confronted by a blackmailer, who threatened
arrest for “indecorous behavior” if she did not
pay him. Too courageous to be frightened that
way, she called for help, and subsequently
reported the. case to the police. An officer was
detailed to investigate, and, so she states, his
conduct was not above suspicion, and finally
ended in a bullying threat that she had better
confess “ her story was all lies.” And that was
all the satisfaction she got. “ A retraction,” she
concludes, “may sometimes have just as little
value as a confession which is torn from a victim
on the rack.”
Supposing this grievance well-founded, it is
scandalous that the police should spare any
humanly possible effort to track down so dastardly
an offender as the blackmailer. Scandalous it is,
too, that municipal sloth seals the parks in so
many of the big cities against decent wayfarers
after nightfall. A few police and a good few
lamps would open up many a delightful walk.
Ihe parks should be as safe to women at night as
are the streets.
As are the streets ? Yes. For the streets, to
womenfolk who have need to pass through them
and are wise enough to go about their business
without dawdling, are “safe” any hour of the
night. Very rarely indeed will a woman who
walks briskly and alertly be addressed by a
stranger. If she is, hersilence is take*) as a sufficient
snub in all cases but one of a million. The infesting
of the streets by herds of wild men seeking whom
they may devour is, in Australia at any rate, a
figment of the imagination. Of course, there are
individual brutes, but it is only with very hard
luck that one would run across them. The parks
are different; but shouldn’t be. Now that woman
laS vote > a good, practical use to put it to
would be to agitate to open up the parks after
night-fall to decent citizens. The freedom of
the city parks these hot summer nights would be
something worth winning.
Miss Vinia de Loitte, who plays the French
girl, Fifi, in the Williamson “Belle of New York”
combination, now at the Royal, has some personal
i n terest. Some weeks ago Syd neysid ers com men ted
upon a death notice in the local press: “Adams.—
Jan. 17, at Fernlea, Balmain, Lavinia Amelie (nee
Oomtesse de Vilme-Hautemont), beloved wife and
rf ng * 1 J of the household ’ for 58 years of R.
i Adams.” The deceased lady was Miss
de Loitte s grandmother, and it was to
get news of her (Mrs. Adams’) whereabouts
that the Queen of Roumania wrote to
Mr. I honias Hughes, the Mayor of Sydney, some
months back. The Roumanian Queen and the
Oomtesse were girls together, and after the latter
married (a run-away match—Mr. Adams is related
to me Earl of Carnarvon’s family) and came to
Australia, they corresponded for some years. Lord
Rosebery was also another correspondent of the
late Mis. Adams, who seems to have been one of
the most retiring of women. The de Loitte
family must now be in its third or fourth
generation in Sydney. It is an old line
for a young country. Miss Vinia de Loitte,
by the way, plays and sings her coquettish r6le
with the sprightlmess and archness of a girl born
in Baris rather than at Snail’s Bay. I first heard
her with the Liedertafel many months ago—she
then sang something from “ Semiramade,” and as
an encore “Angels ever Bright and Fair,” and I
was impressed with the crystalline quality of her
voice, and the feeling with which she produced it.
Her range is from lower middle C to D in
f lfc - Mr. Woodhouse was her teacher. She
herself (although she does not look more than
20 years of age) has been teaching for three
years.
The boarding-house lady who made “a good,
wholesome tomato sauce,” but which her boarders
would not touch until she put a faked French
name on it, has her parallel in a girl who in a
letter to me tells of her struggles to “ get on ” and
of her achievement through a French alias. She
is the eldest of a family of eight and at the age of
16 went to look for work. She answered an
advertisement fpr milliner’s apprentice only, to
hud she would have to servesixmonthsfor nothing.
Same with dress-making. Nothing open to her
but service. She easily got a situation at Bs. a
week and stopped in it 18 months. Owing
to the family’s removal she had to take another
billet, which she left at the end of the week rather
than say “Ma’am” instead of “Mrs. Jones” to
hey mistress—left mentally vowing that she would
call nobody ma’am and be nobody’s servant. She
would be. a waitress. There were many advts.
for waitresses. But on her applying she was
always met with “ And what experience have you
had ? ”
In despair she gave up the waitress business
and took another domestic service situation at
12s. a week, ma’amed her employer, and banked
every penny possible. Onafewof her “afternoons
out” she learnt the cleaning and dyeing business
for £l, then started on her own. She had =612 in
bank, and spent it on fitting up a shop, marked
“Miss , Cleaner and Dyer,” with a bunch of
elaborately-curled feathers and some gloves, in the
window. In a fortnight she was nearly broken;
no business of any worth. Then she risked all
her capital. Took a window on the opposite side
of the street, draped it wonderfully, and had gold-
lettered on the glass “ Madame La Rallee, French
Dyer and Cleaner. ” Now this enterprising young
person is doing so well that she may presently be
able to buy a motor car.
A Sydney girl confesses to having proposed to
such a nice young man—a bank clerk—lately.
This is leap-year. She proposed by letter, under
an assumed name, and mentioned that if rejected
“ she liked diamonds.” He replied rejecting her,
for her own good, on account of his many
objectionable qualities. Some of them: He
loathes domesticity, and poultry, a wood heap and
an axe. He would prefer to lie abed dreaming
bright dreams to doing a little gardening. He
flirts and gambles, is an insomniac, but when
asleep grinds his teeth all the time. Still if she
had a little capital he would not be averse to meet-
ing her. Regrets that she likes diamonds but
asks her to send along her size of glove.
How few women are so honorable as to give a
wooer a list of their bad qualities when he says
‘‘Will you?” Naughty things ! They let him find
them out when too late, after the sacrament has
joined them for the rest of their natural lives.
A citizen of no mean country township gave,
shortly before Christmas, a good subscription to
his local church. Soon afterwards his first son
was born. On Christmas day the usual hymn,
“Unto us a son is born” was inflicted on the
congregation. The citizen, who had been present,
waited on the minister and church-wardens at the
conclusion of the service, and gravely thanked
them for the honor they had done him in instruct-
ing the choir to sing a hymn so complimentary to
himself.
There is more in the “tights” of the panto-
mime and comic opera charmer than the public
wots of : legs are to be expected, of course ; saw-
dust stuffing, too, if the cynical are to be credited.
But it was news, to me at least, to learn that there
is much art in the dyeing of these aids to theatrical
beauty. “By a long and careful process of dip-
ping in the dye, tights are shaded so that they
become paler and paler in tint towards the feet.
This gives a tapering effect to the ankles.” Pre-
sumably, too, ladies who are extra modest, or wish
to appear so, may have an embarrassed blush dyed
into their tights.
The Melbourne jewellery firm that made the
diamond lyre-bird ornament presented to Ada
Crossley by the Melbourne musical profession
sent the warbler a handsome opal as a present
from themselves, but it was courteously returned.
“You see,” said Ada to an acquaintance,
“opals are unlucky, and I sent it back with
an explanation that my superstition would
not allow me to accept the gift. I pay no
heed to most superstitious beliefs. I will walk
under a ladder, or be one of 13, or commence a
journeyjon Friday, or doany thingelse on the list of
unlucky acts, but I am really superstitious about
opals. ”
There’s a deal of child mind study done in
Sydney. Some years ago Dr. Alan Carrol founded
the Association for Child Study in Australia, and
since then numerous branches have grown from the
main stem. I have stumbled into a few of their
meetings at odd times and have always come away
with a dazed feeling that I was never a child and
that the lady or gentleman reading the paper of
the evening must have been just such another kid as
was young Macaulay. (He was a fearsome infant.)
They dug so deep down and reached so far out,
and seemed so humorless and destitute of anec-
dote to point their logic. Talking of anecdotes, a
pretty, well-bred looking, little girl of seven or
so, with shabby shoes and in shabby, neat mourn-
ing clothes, wasstopped by abold-faced, bare-footed
girl- of about nine, oarrying milk-cans, with the
greetings—“’Ullo, I ain’t seen you for an aige.
Yous useter to be smarter than you are now.
Your father’s dead, aint he?” “Yes,” replied
the other, shrinking somewhat, “God is my
father now.” The milk carrier put down her
cans, sniffed scornfully, and remarked, as she
glanced over the little mourner's outfit, “Needn’t
brag. I know what stepfathers is. They won’t
spend much on yous.” The mind of a child is
rather like the way of Solomon’s serpent on a
rock, and the C.S. Associations don’t seem to
make any reliable charts of its route of thought.
That walking-stick, which may also be a sitting-
stick, is slowly, very slowly, coming into use.
Julius Knight had one at Anniversary races, last
week. At the recent cricket matches I saw a few
in active service—the iron point stuck in the turf
and a man above taking, at least, partial ease.
Couldn’t women contrive to make their sun-shades
serve the same purpose ? I’ve seen crowds of
women and girls at the various sports festivals
who looked “ready to drop” from too much
standing exercise. If their umbrella-handles were
built a little differently and much more strongly,
they could use them, as did Julius Rossi his stick,
for, no matter how fiercely the sun shines, they
dare not unfurl the sun shades for fear of angry
remonstrance from spectators behind.
Mrs. Oswald Watt (daughter of Mr. Justice
Williams, late of Melbourne) has had to undergo
an operation for appendicitis, and for some time
was in great danger. She is convalescing at some
English watering place. Miss Constance Dickson
