- The Hotel Australia is having an internal
decorative bath. Its ceilings are taking an opal
or pearl complexion and the authorities are look-
ing wildly for a suitable wall-paper.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Last week’s rush to sign the brand-new book
of the new lodgers at Government House was
brisker than the descent of Woman on a boom
bargain sale. The stream of humanity up the
drive started at the hour of breakfast and con-
tinued till the flunkeys drew in the door-mat for
the night. Any time of the afternoon the porch
was packed. Amusing to glance along the line of
waiting ones and mark the varied expressions.
The old society hand drew her glove off lazily and
looked bored. The thing had to be done. It was
a bore in itself, but the hanging about for the pen
was a deadlier bore. Outsiders, who never get
beyond Gov House porch, grew nervous under so
many eyes and smudged the book. The average
she wore a half-ashamed half-defiant look, as of
a cat caught in the act of stealing the cream.
“A printed injunction is suspended over the
Northcote book to the effect that all callers should
write their names clearly. Needless caution !
Does any anxious groveller ever fail to dot her i’s
in that volume? All the same, the women
whispered it was * nice ’of the Northcotes. Now,
of course, they can attribute a sound reason for
not appearing at any of the Tennyson shivoos.
‘ I used to scribble my name in such a disgraceful
fashion, my dear. Of course, it was beyond
deciphering. Frightfully careless of me, you
know.’ And so the old blot is scribbled out of the
family escutcheon.
“A shoal of pasteboards was also washed into
the Government House porch by the social tide
last week, so many swelled the current by a card
for Lady Knowles and for each of the new aides
of the suite. By the way, the interesting young
Englishwoman mentioned is a widow. Her hus-
band was Mr. Alfred Millington, an officer of
the Imperial Yeomanry, who was killed in the
early part of the S’African war—not a year
after the marriage. Lady Knowles still dresses
in subdued raiment.
“ The numerous public bodies who converged in
a special kow-tow to the new G.-G. at the V.R.
residence last-Thursday were given every oppor-
tunity to have a goodly wash of wine after the
speeches were fired off. An ample buffet provided
enough solid and liquid refreshment to banquet an
army after a day’s fasting. And it was called
‘ afternoon-tea ! ’ Officialdom smacks its lips and
talks praise of the new Excellency by the yard.
Besides, the little man commended himself nobly
to the numerous addressers by the easy, fluent,
apt style of his delivery of the speeches in reply.
“ Lady Norchcote is not going to miss anything.
During the grand official crawl the other day at
Government House she regarded the scene
from a near niche, and when the welcoming
batches returned to an upright position, and looked
like reasonable male beings again, she descended
upon them and smiled acknowledgments, and
took the hand of every man in the gathering. For
a ready flow of talk and easy, good-humored
smile, the G.G.’s wife beats the most popular of
her order to date —so thought the local aldermen
who worshipped the new star that day.
“The Waxellency’s back hair took an extra
kick when the Northcote aides hinted at the
radiant impressions his daughter—the g;em of the
collection—was making at Bombay. Miss Ruby’s
complexion tints, it seems, were admired, and
the ordinary run of pallid belles there are
as limp flannel flowers beside the radiant Mel-
bournienne. Of course, the aides didn’t run
into metaphor over the matter—for their kind
never run and never metaphor—but there was
quite sufficient said to make Mr. Madden happy.
“ She-travellers, arriving from home, scout the
idea of trained street-suits. The walking-skirts
they bring back with them are short—so short
that they don’t need to be held up at all. Scribe
met one of the returned wanderers on the block,
t’other morning, in crisp, blue-muslin of well-
covered design. Her short skirt stood well out,
inviting admiration of open-work hose and natty
shoes, and permitting a glimpse of two petti-
coats. The top layer, a plain, white-muslin slip,
with frill of same material as gown, was held out
from wearer’s ankles by a starchy, blue garment,
matching gown in color and furnished with
goffered frills. The decided hint of crinoline
tendency in the lower part of the toilet was in
snappy contrast with the sloping shoulders,
slipping-down sleeve-puffs, and pointed, pelerine-
effect of the bodice.
“ But belles, back from abroad, want to flourish
something more startling in the faces of dull
stay-at-homes than mere toilette-novelties. A
fiancee, of course, is the best souvenir of a voyage,'
but failing that, foreign mannerisms and weird
slang serve to astonish the natives fairly well.
Some travelled Australiennes have brought home
with them lately a queer lingo, alleged to be the
quintessence of ‘smartness.’ When their friends
met them on the mail-boat, the wanderers ran
from one welcoming group to another, squealing
rapturously, ‘Taste my tulips,’ i.e., ‘Kiss me!’
Two extra-naice sisters, not long from ’Orae, went
to view a friend’s trousseau t’other day. At the
sight of the lingerie they duetted, 4 What divvy
undies! ’
“Yet another high-stepping voyageuse has just
rejoined us, infected with the craze for leading a
pet-dog round the Block. It’s a pretty little tyke
and behaves well—for a dog. * Still, one can’t
expect Chesterfieldian conduct from a terrier.
“ Polo sets the fashionable world agog next
Thursday (4th), when the tournament starts
with the Chirnside Cup, at the Kooyong course.
“Something does coine, after all, to her who
waits. A lady who put in several years on a pro-
bation term, and murmured not at delay, received
the news of the death of her fiance not long ago.
She thought Fate was cruel, until the lawyers
sent along word that she was the chief beneficiary
under the will.
“ Appendicitis is out of fashion ; the thing was
so frightfully overdone. Surgeons now grapple
with an outbreak of tumor among their fair
patients. Latest arrivals from the Big Smoke
confirm the popularity of tumor among the
smartest.
‘ ‘ Mr. and Mrs. Gordon Lyon have taken up
fresh domestic quarters out at Heidelberg—the
place noted for its hills and its hospital for incur-
ables. Gordon Lyon, one remembers, was the
Beaucaire of giddy Melbourne in the Brassey days.
Now he is an exemplary father and hearth-loving
Benedict.
“The death of Colonel Bingham was sudden
news to most of Melbourne, though the Madden
family had known of his long illness and his sick
leave which necessitated a change to the Cold
Country from the tropical blazes of India. The
Colonel, when he left Melbourne two or three
years since, was rather alarmingly florid and thick
in the neck. Still the post in the tropics was a
promotion, and he went cheerfully to take it, in
company with his aesthetic and decorative wife, a
younger sister of Dame Madden. The couple
entertained all the smart Melbourne people who
put in at Bombay for any time, and Miss Ruby
Madden saw the first chapters of her Indian life
under their auspices.
“Did you ever notice the demure, angelic
expression of the giddy and promiscuous girl-flirt
once she settles down in the collar of matrimony ?
There’s one of her type at present in Melbourne;
her lowered eyelids, buttoned-up mouth, and un-
inviting hat shade, as she passes through Collins-
street, upsets the seriousness of all her old flames.
She looked an unkissed Sweet-and-Twenty at the
Mayoral reception the other night, and Society
marms twitted her proud spouse on burdening so
young a thing with the cares of a house-
hold, and the fair one toyed shyly with her fan,
and invited more twitting. It is, I suppose, the
feminine equivalent to the prudishness of the con-
verted rake.
“The Melbourne woman is showing a more
pronounced disposition every day to host it out of
the house. The afternoon-tea at the kiosk of the
Botanical Gardens is a recognised form of up-to-
date hospitality. Another place of hospitality is
the cafe-spread. A private room is booked, a
cart of flowers and foliage is tipped in, the artiste
disposes them to the best effect, and, when all is
arborish and sweetly poetic, the hostess and her
flock arrive.
“Mrs. Maesmore Morris, according to recent
word from the Big Smoke, grows fat. And her
sesthetio, languishing, violet-eyed, taper-chinned
beauty will be sacrificed for ever if she allows that
tendency to continue, and her slow languor be
but accounted the heaviness of the woman of bulk.
“ An artist of some observation touring abroad
writes encouragingly to his women friends here
on a subject of vital interest. ‘ Nothing strikes
me so hard,’ he scrawls, ‘as the bloom of the
Englishwoman in the forties. Any smart fashion-
able is brighter, livelier, more amusing, more
magnetic, and better to look upon than the
debutante, no matter how her youth flies its pen-
nants in cheek and eye. The woman of 40 takes
the place of the belle of the last century. No
woman in the world owns up in hair, color, neck
lines, or face wrinkles, to a day beyond 40. It
seems to me the feminine physique as well as the
character goes on developing and maturing (yes,
maturing) till that rocky year, and there it remains
triumphant, till—well—indefinitely. The young
English Miss is sly, insipid, and a dull companion,
and her beauty isn’t in it with the strong charm
of the forties.’
“The Williamson management spread itself
in a glory of bright scarlet gladiolas and roses
for the first greeting of the new vice-royalties
in Her Majesty’s, Saturday night. An elevated
cushion should be in use for the little G.-G.
As it is, his mightiness is lost amid the
sweep of his lady’s chiffons, the surf of Lady
Knowles’ train, and the four large aides. Lady
Northcote mostly regarded “The Country
Girl” through a lorgnette. Anon, she passed
the peepers over the audience. She wasn’t there
merely on show, she wanted to inspect in turn.
She wore turquoises this time, mingled with
diamonds, and a white silk and black dress. All
society rallied to the show owing to the due
advertisement of the Northcote presence. The
L.S. Mackinnons had a rustling bevy of girl and
all the smart bachelors available. Mrs. Walter
Coldham mounted her comely fairness in a grand
array of sky blue chiffons.
“ A few chosen spirits trooped down to the
decks of the Omrah the other day to say farewell
to the Countess Darnley. The floral anchor, the
heart of forget-me-nots, and the posy of remem-
brance were handed up by the Dowager of Clive-
den, Mrs. Robert Power, and others. The
Countess travelled only with a maid. By the
way, many wonder where the functions of that
dresser come in.

accountant Sutton, and grandniece of late Mon-
signor O’Hea, who leaves by the Moravian to

friends last week with tea, music, and a view of
her trousseau. Miss Sutton reports that Mrs.
Maesmore Morris has grown tired of George
Alexander as a manager, and that she and Beryl

“The presentation bob as a habit is spreading.
Lady Silverlocks Clarke gave the younger women
an object lesson in bobbing whenever the greater
Vice-Royalty approached, and Dame Hutton
excited their envy by her dexterous genuflections
during the Tennyson tenure. But now all Toorak
bobs rather adroitly. It is rather a pity to see
Australian knees put to such a use.

“NERVES.”
Nerves, nerves, nerves, nerves ! Everybody has “The Nerves” this Summer. And Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for'Pale People (being a blood-building tonic) is the only thing that can cure you quickly and
surely. And they do this because they actually make new blood—and that new blood soothes and steadies
your nerves. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills give you strong nerves, high spirits, good appetite, sound sleep, ruddy
cheeks, self-confidence, energy and strength to withstand the heat. They did this for Miss Mary Mulhall,
1 Welford-street, Enmore, Sydney, and they can do the same for every person who feels weary, nervous,
or run-nown.
“ For a long time,” says Miss Mary Mullhall, my health
was miserable. My blood got weak and thin, and then my
nerves started to twitch and shake at the least thing. I was
too nervous to sleep properly, and every morning I woke more
tired than when I went to bed. I used to have nasty, nervous
headaches, too, and I often wonder now how I ever dragged
through the long, weary days. My appetite fell off. After
even the lightest meal a heavy weight seemed always to be
messing on my chest. If I walked a few yards my heart
thumped and jumped like mad. My legs often felt as though
thevcould not hold me ; and my hands trembled like a leaf if
I even poured out a cup of tea. Day after day I kept in this
weak, miserable state, until I heard that Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills actually made new blood. I knew that was the only way
to soothe and steady my nerves, so I got some. Soon I began
to feel they were doing me good. My nerves got much steadier,
and the weakness left my legs. At last every trace of illness
left me, and I was able to go about my work as well as ever.
My nerves are now strong and steady, and my health is perfect
in every way. People with shaky nerves or watery blood are
sure to find a perfect cure in Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People.”

Though St. John's, Parramatta, has been
a going church since 1797, it has only had four
incumbents—Marsden, Bobart, King and Gunther
—all Englishmen.
“Adam Tramp,” one of the best known
of Labor verse-makers of the Queensland bush,
who lately worked his passage to Cosine, Lane’s
S’American settlement, was disappointed with it
after a few weeks’ trial, and has moved on.
Mr. L. J. Brient, ex-editor of Sydney
D.T., has now been promoted on the staff of Lon-
don Daily Chronicle to the post of night editor.
Brient’s progress in London has been fast —due to
his keen knowledge of what constitutes “news.”
Graham Berry was twice married. The
surviving children by the first marriage are
James, Harriet, Benjamin, Emma, Charlotte,
Graham, Eliza and George; and by the second,
Katie, Alice, Arthur, Clare, Edith, Harold and
Dorothy.
Robert Et heridge, whose death in England
is announced, was one of the front men of the
British Museum. Mr. Robert Etheridge, curator
of Sydney Museum, is his son, and in geological
and ethnological researches treads in his father’s
footsteps.
Graham Berry in his time had been much
admired by many women. ’ He was welHnto the
sixties when he was presented to Queen Victoria
at Windsor Castle, and she remarked to a duchess-
in-waiting that “the gentleman from Australia
had beautiful eyes.”
Mr. J. J. Lee. formerly of the Crown
Solicitor’s Office, Sydney, and now in his 85th
year, was, ’tis said, recently appointed a Knight,
or something, by the Pope on the nomination of
Cardinal Moran, but when Rev. O’Haran told
him of the bauble, sensible Mr. Lee offered him
£5O to take it away.

bourne Herald resulted in drawing H. Junor
Browne, one-time'distiller, and father-in-law to

once strongly addicted.
Graham Berry, who died on January 25.
gave the insurance company from which the Vic-
torian Government bought an annuity of £5OO a
fair profit. The Government paid £3OlO for
Berry’s annuity just six years ago. The company
has, therefore, made the interest on the yearly-
decreasing purchase money and £lO over.
C. Byrnes, dead at Bundarra, was a
Crimean veteran, in the thick of all the great
engagements. He fought the Australian goldfields
next, and was among the first to tackle free
selection under Robertson’s Act. Used to say
life in the Crimea was bad, on the goldfields
worse, but on a backblocks selection worst.
Jas. Munro, of Armadale, andE. J. Dixon,
of Malvern (Vic.), are the only survivors of the
20 “ stonewallers ” who followed Graham Berry
to the country in 1877 and s. roped the pool.
Bryan O’Loghlen still remains of the famous
Berry Ministry of 1878, which imposed Black
Wednesday on an astonished country, and turned
Gov. Bowen’s hair grey.

ciscan order.

Died, the other day, Long Gordon—-for a
while Protector of Aborigines in the Boulia (Q.)
district. Gordon was a squatter in the early
days, and a great dare-devil. It is told £ low
one time tracked certain aboriginals, who had
killed a surveyor, to a cave where they were
hidden, entered the cave alone, and did summary
justice with a shooting iron. In his declining
years he was a true friend to the .native tribes,
until retrenchment lost him his position.
When Elijah 111., the man with the
purple wings, was last in Australia, he had a
fine gift of language, as instance this outburst in
1883 on the Argus :
All the hundred eyes of the Argus glare with hate
upon anyone whose actions might endanger any of the
gods of the Exchange. All their thousand claws are
eager to tear, and their ten thousand serpents’ teeth are
ready to bite, the monster who dares to question the
divine right of Pluto, god of darkness and of gold, and
his drunken but wealthy associate Bacchus. The Argus
is the dog Cerberus who watches over the dark interests
of the Hades of a corrupt society, where Mammon holds
undisputed sway. I count it among my honors to
number among my many adversaries this foul literary
Cerberus, whose “mouth is an open sepulchre,” despite
its veneer of culture, and I should be as much ashamed to
merit its praise* as I am thankful to have been honored
by its abuse
Whitaker Wright “played the game ”
boldly at the finish. His genius for management
was never shown to better advantage than in the
arrangement of his death. He had lost, and had
been sentenced to seven years’ imprisonment-
seven years of skilly and horrible drudgery—and he
promptly died before they could change the high
collar and frock coat of ease for the grey garb
and broad arrows of the cells. And he was right.
Better the Unknown than the skilly and plank
bed of the penitentiary after the gorgeous luxury
of Rippon Lea, with its glass and marble smoking
room under the artificial lake, and its rooms
dadoed with flowers of carved metal that waved in
the machine-made breeze and wafted out con-
centrated perfumes. Compared with the miser-
able Wilde the great wild-cat died game.
“ Galvo ” writes about his adopted king :
Have been living in M.L. and studying its politics for
18 months now, after many years spent in different parts
of Australia. Arrived with rather a prejudice against
Seddon. My stay here has convinced me that he is a
much-maligned man. Really, there are two Seddons.
There is Richard Seddon, P.C., who runs up the Royal
Standard on all possible occasions. But there is also
Dick Seddon, the strong, alert democrat, who apparently
never rests and is always fighting, scheming and striving
for the advancement of Maoriland. I otten wonder what
will happen to M.L. politics when Seddon drops the reins,
and foresee a Tory reaction. Joseph Ward, his most
likely successor, is a Tory at heart, and has not a tithe of
Seddon’s strength or determination. He depends less on
force than lubrication, and the manner ill which the
Opposition papers praised his handling of Parliament
dur'ng Seddon’s absence in England is itself sufficient to
cause suspicion. So far as the Opposition itself is con-
cerned, it is composed of propertied men whose ambition is
to reduce things to the state they are in in Victoria. The
N Z Herald, their special organ, is never tired of holding
the Iceberg Govt, up as an example. Seddon may be
(and is) a terror to talk, and to talk flapdoodle, but taking
him all round. Imperialism and Democracy blended, there
is not another man in Australasia —not even Kingston
who has done so much for the workers or who strives so
single-heartedly for the advancement of his colony.
LORD NORTHCOTE.
(By a bilious caricaturist.)
Tamahau Mahupuka, the big Wairarapa
chief, who died the other day, had two wives,
Areta (Alice) and another. The other was
seriously ill in a hospital when she heard of
his death, but got up and came straightway to the
resting-place of her lord to join the mourners.
By the way, the old order of the tangi changeth.
No longer are continuous chanting and wild
dances indulged in round the corpse. No longer
is there savage gluttony in liquids and solids,
with pig-like intervals of sleep between gorges.
The whole tangi was conducted decorously under
the control of the Maori Council.
Mr. Ben. Cowderoy, sec. of Melb. Chamber
of Commerce up to ’B6, then a Government valuer
for 16 years, now living in retirement and breed
ing sheep at Notting Hill (Vic.), recollects the
declaration of peace subsequent to Waterloo. As
a Blue-coat boy, when the school was alongside
Old Newgate, he saw Fauntleroy hanged, besides
many others of less note. Smugglers by the
dozen he has seen dangling in chains on Thames
banks—left there to the mercy of birds and
weather. Sheep and horse-stealers and arsonists
he has witnessed pay for their offence with their
lives. The Iron Duke, Coleridge, Lamb and
Dickens he remembers well, also the “ first gentle-
man in Europe,” who “ had cheeks like flitches of
bacon.” In ’53 he reached "Victoria, whose history
he has at his fingers’ ends; he now talks of
writing his memoirs.

Robert Tooth, whose
daughter was lately saved
from drowningin England,
is responsible for a window picture in St.
Andrew’s Cathedral, Sydney, “The Calming of
the Sea.” That picture was suggested to him in
his old brewery days by the rescue of a female
relative from drowning.
Whenever the late R.C. Bishop Torreggiani
came to Sydney the cabmen fought shy of him.
Not that he was a bad payer. It was on account or
his tremendous weight. Out in the Wooliant**
district, where lie used to stay with the FranJSis&an'
monks, he broke the springs of two As
the drivers and owners of the damaged cabs were
Romans, they suffered and made no sign. Ih o
Bishop’s name, however, with an estimate of his
weight, was passed round among the drivers, and
thereafter he was politely, yet firmly, boycotted.
About the quaintest old figure in Vic.
labor-union life has just dropped out. For nearly
a quarter of a century Peter Phillips had been
secretary of the Miners’ Association at Bendigo.,
For 20 years he had been to everybody .
Peter.” In the earliest infancy of the union he lost
his arm by a mining accident; and a good deal for
that reason, they made him secretary'. A rugged
old battler, who never thought once before lie
spoke twice, he had trodden on nearly everyone’s
corns sometime or other; and yet they all forgave
him for his obvious sincerity and his devotion to
the association, and because he knew every miner
on the field, and could talk to them all in private
like a father. He didn’t believe in any “new-
fangled ideas of labor going into politics,” and so
fought the Labor candidate pretty well every
time.

prising fiend went up to Lord Northcote, as he was
lunching in the pavilion on the Adelaide Oval,
and, tapping him on the shoulder, said: “Oh,
excuse me, sir, but if I don’t get a photograph of
you, I’ll get the sack.” The little roan jumped
up: “Oh, that would be too bad. Certainly, I’ll
come. Shall Igo with you now?” Whereupon
the magnanimous photographer responded : “Oh,
no, sir; finish your lunch first.” Directly the
meal was ended, Northcote, Tennyson, the Ad-
miral, and Governor Le Hunte trooped to the
back of the Oval and submitted themselves to be
“ posed.”
“ G.H.” writes :
Think mighty little- with all due respect of John
Farrell’s “ great integral principle” that nothing
matters, anyhow. When the individual starts
out clinging to the idea that nothing is of any
importance, the nation is likely to resemble an
improvident, irresponsible vagrant. A swagman
Australia may look picturesque enough in the abstract,
and provide pathos for a lot of debilitated poets ; but a
fixed, responsible country with one permanent address
would be far better. If Farrell had skewer, d himself to
the world with a more substantial pin than the nothing-
matters principle, he might have possessed a Daily
Telegraph of his own, and made out of it a national
weapon that would have kept his memory green for ever
in Australia. Instead, through not mattering, he leaves
a small parcel of rather good verses ; and the recollection
of a fellow troubled throughout life with a huge go-easy-
Which is mostly truth, but partly misconception.
John Farrell was a worker all the time ; his
“ nothing matters onnyhow ” was the motto not
of laziness, but of gentle contempt for the feverish
worry of money-making and ambitious striving,
something of the spirit of Walt Whitman :
I stand and look at the oxen long arid long,
They are so placid and self-contained,
They do not lay awake at night weeping over their sins.
A man who wanted a billet interviewed
the Minister of Works the other day. Mr.
Lawrence Eoley was in the Minister’s room at the
time. “Do you think you can find anything for
him to do, Larry?’’ said the Owe Sullivan.. “I
think I can find him a job down the yard, Ned,’
was the affable reply. And that Bill went to his
dirt-money in joy.

Aboriginalities.
In the cane-growing districts, the O.S.R.
Co. keeps its books by the decimal system, ex-
tended “to three places.” When the cookies get
their delivery receipts for each load in tons and
decimals, their mental struggles to battle out the
value of the decimals is appalling. “ I can’t make
out tliiin decimals,” said one; “what the devil
are they at all, at all ?” “I don’t rightly know,”
replied the other, “but ” (with an air of great
wisdom) “it takes a mighty lot of ’em to weigh
a ton ! ”
* * * *
Weather-seer Wragge is being threatened
with a serious rival, and should get all his isobars
ready for competition. One John Hurrell, a stout
German ex-bootmaker, has been going up and
down the Vic. Mallee of late like a meteorological
dervish, prophesying all manner of climatic
things. So far, too, lie has had his prognostica-
tions backed up by events, and the kind of
weather lie foretells has come right along.
Hurrell has a big pull in not needing any expen-
sive outfit of telescopes, barometers, and things.
He seems to manufacture his weather out of bits of
string, waste paper, and the like.
Mr. John Hurley, M.L.A. (N.S.W.),
claims that he has in his possession the skin of a
red-bellied black snake shed 17 years after the
reptile’s death ! The owner of the defunct
wriggler, an Emmaville (N.S.W.) resident,
alleges that this is the seventeenth skin that
snake has shed since it was killed 17 years
ago, and other highly truthful residents of
the town vouch for the statement as Gospel.

Some queer names of Victorian localities :
“Singer and Die,” an old lead near Avoca; “Good
Morning Bill,” a hill in the Ararat district;
“Pretty Sally,”a hill near Ivilmore; “Temperance
Township,” the continuation of Ascot Vale;
“ Whisky Hill,” at the back of Melbourne R.A.
Show Grounds.
* * * *
A new Centralian stock-route is indicated
by F. H. Hann, who, leaving Laverton, in Wes-
tralia, on September 28, got through to Oodna-
datta (S.A.), on the trans-continental line, by
December 23, and has since started back by a
neighboring but different route. The distance is
900 miles, the border being about mid-way. Good
grass and saltbush and water (at reasonable
depths) all the way.

“ Peri ” : The prettiest bit of Nature's
handicraft is the brown snake—in my eyes. It
(of either sex) is as vain as it is hand-
i some, and as harmless as a dynamite
y cap till you fool with it or take it
unawares. The brown species is
the aristocrat of snakedom, the
black the middle class, and the
tiger the plebeian. The tiger is a
lazy, loafing and bad-tempered
devil ; the black is a grafter and
knows a thing or two. When
you come to know snakes there
is nothing mean about them.
Would rather handle a pet snake
than a yellow-
crested cocka-
too any day
—they are
not nearly
so treacher-
ous. The
snake as a
lover is
brave and
gallant. Al-
though m y
creed teach-
es me to
kill every
snake I come
across, re-
gardless of
likes and dis-

only because

“ J.P.” : Some people prefer coal fires.
Give me wood. There never was or will
be a chop in Heaven or the Other Place,
cooked over coal, that comes anywhere
near the chop grilled, or fried for that
mattter, on a good wood fire. Gas is
worse than coal. Apart from cooking there is
nothing like wood—nothing half so cheerful as a
good blaze. If the back country is ahead of the
towns in any one thing, it’s in this one. In an
old-fashioned, six-foot, wattle-and-daub fire-place,
with a few logs of manuka, you can have the
finest fire that ever happened on this planet or
elsewhere.

“ Alex M.” :He koala-shooting. My own
experience is that the “bear’’can frequently be
brought down by a second shot, fired so quickly
after the first that the animal hasn’t time to put
on his “extra special” grip. If he gets time to
do that, you may (unless you manage to hit him
fair in the cerebellum) bombard him unsuccess-
fully for hours.

“ H.F.” : Re “Quidam’s” criticisms (8.,
14/l/’O4) of bush-writers. So far writers have
only touched such aspects of the bush as came
under their own notice : Lawson the highroad,
“Banjo” the cattle-station, “Steele Rudd’ the
Darling Downs. Now, it is a fair inference that
I—who, as a free selector, have lived for 15 years
in a district untapped by these and other writers
—know what I’m writing of, and I say that a
native bear can be killed with a gun, but often at
an expenditure of shot which no economical skin-
getter would contemplate. Cheapest cartridges
for guns 6s. 6d. per hundred, for “22” rifles
Is. 3d. And the small-bore rifle is now so
accurate that my boys prefer it for ’possum
shooting. I have only killed one bear myself,
and though they are thick round my house, I
would a 3 soon kill a baby as another. When
wounded, they cry and sob so much like a child
that it is heart-breaking.

Abundance of grass, plenty of water, and
a record harvest; this is a glorious season for the
teamsters, and they are going in for record loads.
One man drew up at Narrotnine (N.S.W.) with
193 bags of wheat on his waggon, weighing a
little more than 21J tons, the gross weight (includ-
ing waggon) being a few pounds under 25 tons.

“ Ento.” : How is it that the hornet (the
large, yellow and black-banded variety) is not
classed among our insect-destroying and protected
birds ? _ This insect destroys more caterpillars,
proportionately to its size, than many a protected
songster. Have lately been watching one closely,
and when she had completed the mud receptacle
for her first egg, and duly laid therein the said
she set to work in the usual way to provide
for the commissariat of the future grub. Forty-
seven times did she return to the nest, each time
laden with a plump caterpillar, in that comatose
condition to which the hornet’s sting has the strange
power of reducing other insects, to serve as food
for the hornet-grubs. The next step is to close
the opening to this first nest, and to build a similar
one beneath it. In this another egg is laid and
another supply of caterpillars is laid-in. A third
time the process is repeated, and the lady-hornet
departs, secure that her progeny’s future is pro-
vided for. Now, suppose she procured three times
47 caterpillars in all, those 141 caterpillars would
have produced a like number of butterflies, which
would have laid enough eggs to produce 40 million
caterpillars to assist in the shortage of next year’s
wheat-crop. Hornet-conservation, therefore, is
plainly an agricultural duty.
Late John Farrell was in
many things as guileless as a child.
When he went to Albury to take a
job at the local brewery he arrived
about 11.30 p.m., and was driven to
the pub. nt arest the beer-foundry.
He found the pla~e live'y, the bar
and other rooms being crowdtd.
The hotelkeeper was the next week
before the court on a charge of
making too free with the Licensing
Act. Farrell was relied upon as
the principal witness for the de-
fence. Solicitor for the defence in
court asked him : “ Now, Mr.
Farrell, will you kindly tell the
court what you know of the case? ”
With his broguish drawl Farrell
dropped into a description of the
scene. “ Well, there was dancing,
and singing, and card-playing, and
shaking for drinks ; business was
brisk, and every one seemed to he
having a good time. I don’t know
what the police wanted to interfere
for.” At this stage the majesty
of the law hid itself in a spacious
smile, the audience guffawed, and
the defendant and his attorney
looked unutterable things. When
the court recovered it asked pleas-
antly if the defendant had any
more witnesses to call. The de-
fendant hadn’t, and w;.s fined the
full penalty. And when Farrell
stepped out of the box he could
not understand why there was such
a cloud on the face of the man who
had subpoenaed him.

“ Bullainan ” tells this with
an air of great veracity:—At Mt.
Fox, 25 miles from Ewan (N.Q.), the
ironbark timber is being gradually
transformed into box. The change in many cases
commences at the ends of the topmost branches,
where small twigs are conspicuous by the differ-
ence in the leaves and the white bark. In the
case of one large tree, the change has been pro-
ceeding for years, and the tree, barring a few feet
of ironbark near its butt, is now a full-blown box
tree. Trees are to be seen in all stages of the
transformation. The blacks call this locality
‘ ‘ debil debil ” country.

“Old Tom” about Young Australia: —
At our place there is a married couple-—man,
overseer; t’other half, cook and housekeeper.
New Year’s morning overseer didn’t turn out till
a couple of hours later than usual, and the cook
remarked to her six-year old son, in pretended
fear, “My goodness, father might have died last
night. Oh, whatever would we do?” The nipper :
“I dunno what you’d do, mammy, but I’d nick
the keys of the store, quick and livelv.”

J. F. Keane, Atherton (N.Q.), writes :
I was asked to remove a beehive from the vicinity of a
garden gate, owing to a horse getting stung. It was a
2ft. 6in. x 2ft. packing case, without a top, lying on its
side on the ground, and covered with a much-twisted
and bent sheet of galvanised iron. On throwing the iron
off, the open side of the box exposed the interior, up to
six inches of one end, filled with comb and bees. 5 * From
the space unoccupied by bees bolted three half-grown
rats, which ran round the box, and then in agaiii, three
times. I tried to kill the rats, hut the bees paraded in
their full force, and covered the eventual retreat of the
rodents through a paling fence. Afterwards I found a
well-built rats’ nest in the, otherwise, vacant end of the
box. There can be no doubt that bees and rats had
domiciled amicably in the same compartment for weeks.
The rats, as they ran in an! out, brushed carelessly
against clusteis of bees on the comb without creating
any excitement.
BILL AND I.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bill and I went north o’ Bourke
Twenty years ago,
Took a job of contract work—
Fencing job in 00.
Bill and I a cheque knocked up,
Shared two hundred pound,
Then to see the Melbourne Cup
Bill and I were bound.
Bill and I arrived in Bourke,
Met some mates of old,
Spent the fruits of twelve months’ work,
All our blooming gold ;
Never saw the Melbourne Cup,
Never got past Bourke.
Three days saw our beans done-up—
Went back north to work.
Worked like tigers one more year,
Cup time came again—
Straight past Bourke we swore we’d clear
By the first down train ;
Vowed that year we’d view the Cup,
Swore ’gainst rum and Bourke ;
Yet that darned place pulled us up—
Darned dry place is Bourke.
’Leven times we’ve started down—
Down to see the Cup—
’Leven times in Bourke’s sweet town
We our cheques done-up.
Bill and I still, north o’ Bourke,
Toil from year to year ;
Bourke’s a town we fail to shirk—
Further, fail to steer.
North o’ Bourke we earn our bread,
Bourke provides our rum ;
North o’ Bourke our souls must tread
Out to Kingdom Come.
WILMOT WATSON.


The catastrophe in Herbert Spencer’s un-
published tragedy is said to be —an induction
killed by a fact.
Miss Helena Rubenstein (says the /( ustralasian),
who has rooms at 243 Collins-street, Melbourne, is intro-
ducing the new skin food, known as “Valaze,” prepared
by Dr. Lykushi, the famous Russian skin specialist. It is
claimed hy the inventor and Miss Rubenstein from expe-
rience—that if the instruction given in her book be fol-
lowed, anyone disiigured by wrinkles, freckles, or any
skin blemish whatever, will be delighted with the results
obtained after a course of treatment. An advantage of
“ Valaze ” is that it does not remain on the surface of the
skin, where it can be seen, but is absorbed, and creates a
healthy condition, the result being that looseness is re-
moved, and the skin made firm and healthy. Miss
Rubenstein declares “ Valaze ” to be ab olutely the only
genuine imported skin food. Her little work is illustrated
by a number of diagrams, showing the method of apply-
ing the food. Further particulars will be found in our
advertising columns.*

“ W.A. Bushman”: J2e ceremonies of
W. A. natives. Once a year the North Yilgarn
tribe, now nearly extinct, “go bush walk,”
returning laden with bark, of a very tender fibre,
winch forms the chief ingredient of an annual
feast, bucks only admitted. This bark is cooked
on embers, and is mixed with human blood, ob-
tained from the main artery of the fore.irm,
opened by the finger-nail only. The wound is
bound up, and soon heals. Considerable signifi-
cance is attached to this feast.

“W. W.” ; Most bushmen are aware that
manna gums, during the summer and early
autumn, supply various birds and insects with
snow-white sugar, which falls from the trees in
lumps often as large as walnuts. I’ve often seen
from 61b. to 121 b. 1 ying on a small patch of ground
beneath manna gums. But the richest treacle
mine I ever struck was pointed out to me the other
day. It was an old manna gum, and from where
the tree forked, about 40ft. from the earth, to the
roots was streaked with dry marina, in places half-
an-inch broad and quarter-inch deep. Other
streaks were in liquid state, sweet as honey, and
same color. Above the fork there were also
streams of manna. Several lbs. must have fallen
to the ground ; and there was a train-load of ants
picnieing in the vicinity. My theory is that the
tree is on the point of death and the sap is flowing
frofn it. Tree mentioned is on Tareutta Creek,
seven miles from Tumberumba.
* * * *
t “ B- Yates” writes from Ouriinbah,
N.S.W., re domestication of the lyre bird :
I have one at present which I got in August last, he
being then about a month old. He is perfectly tame and
friendly. As for diet, he will take-on pretty well any-
thing—from small frogs to porridge. His mimetic
accomplishments are, so far, limited to barking like
an excited puppy, and whistling in a melodious but ear-
splitting way. He already has the two outer curved tail
feathers, about 7in. long, and a curious circumstance is
that he seems to have no crop. No matter how much he
eats, there is no swelling as with other fowls. Also, he
drinks by sucking water up without raising and throwing
back his head. He delights in a bath, though he makes
it as thick with mud as possible.
• * * *
An old rangatira in the Bay of Plenty bad
but one daughter. She had been married many
years, but was childless, and had gone in vain to
the sacred tree in various localities. The old
chief was sad, for the extinction of his line was
threatened. But his hopes were revived the other
day when his grand-nephew announced that the
local schoolmaster had a new tree in his garden
marked “ tamariki” (children) tree. So he begged
permission, which was granted, to pay his re-
spects to the spirit of that tree, and every morn-
ing for a week the old chief, his daughter and his
son-in-law prayed to that tree for the blessing of
children. There has been so far, however, no
birth announcement.
* • * •
The “ evil eye ” is still a power among the
Maoris. At Waipatu, the other week, two young
girls were brought to the tohunga suffering from
delusions ; along with them was brought an old
woman, charged with witchcraft and with the
responsibility for the girls’ mental upset. The
old woman, while she seemed to take some pride
in her reputation, shrilly protested that she wasn’t
guilty of anything in connection with the girls,
and implored protection, as she had been
threatened with a witch’s death. The Maori
council, which knows too much to jump very
violently on old superstitions and practices, has
given the woman protection, and is digging into
the matter.

Australia is to be invaded this year by a
gang of half-caste Maoris. They are going across
• to shear sheep, and they reckon they will knock
out some records. Ten will form the gang; all
natives of Hawkes Bay. Hawkins, a half-caste
of the Wairarapa district, recently declared that
lie shore last December 230 sheep in hours, and
1276 sheep in six days.

“ E.F.” : Like your correspondent “ H.”
(B. 7/l/’O4) I have often wondered why Bulletin
artists draw such wonderful axes, shovels, &c.
In issue l/7/’O3 an old man is depicted felling a
tree with an axe like that used by the headsman
of romance. There certainly is a short-handled
axe with such a blade. It is called a squaring
axe, but it is never used for felling trees. The
artist’s stockwhip, too, as a rule, resembles six
feet of clothes line, and the pictured beast ahead
of the pictured horseman has often got a soft
thing on, for the holder of the whip must in-
evitably strangle himself before he concludes his
stroke. Another thing that puts all Australian
artists on their beam ends is the tail of the
kangaroo. As a rule it is represented as so thick
and heavy that, if the ’roo really had such a tail, he
would be considerably handicapped in making a
record jump. Again, a still stranger thing is that
in Sydney, the home of boating men, no artist
correctly draws a boat, an oar, or a scull. As a
rule there is portrayed a man in flannels propelling
an ancient British raft with a pair of clothes-
props. One well-known local artist confessed to
me that he always fought shy of depicting a boat
and a pair of oars ; and Frank Mahony, our best
animal painter, has told me often that the proper
gauge of a Ws tail was beyond him, and that a
native bear was a hopeless impossibility Leggings
were at one time an artistic stumbling block, and
though the Australian artist seems now to have
got over that difficulty, his saddles and bridles
Btill occasionally leave much to be desired.

“D.11.R.” : A Bulletin contributor once
contended that it was an advantage to be small;
but the advantage doesn’t last. Know a cocky
who is just sft. 2in. high, and goes by the
sobriquet of “ Booky.” He used to run horses in
his neighbors’ paddocks. His sheep used to get
through the wires and depasture on adjacent
holdings. A bore drain supplies water to the
cookies, and “ Booky ” would monopolise it with
the aid of two dams, and so grow twice as much
as anyone else. When complaints or protests
were made, “Booky” would revile the protestant
and all his ancestors till purple language obscured
the view, trusting to his diminutiveness to protect
him. But, the other day, something happened.
A big man, who had stood bullying for six years,
turned, and “Booky” went home a muddied oaf
and a wreck, with an intimation that in future he
must behave just like a big man or else take the
consequences.

“T.H.A.”: A certain N.Q. squatter’s
wife took a half-caste girl (whose father was a
neighboring squatter) out of the blacks’ camp, in
order to bring her up decently. _ Somebody
informed the authorities, and the kind-hearted
woman was notified that she mustn’t harbor an
aboriginal. Hearing that the police were going
to take her away, the unfortunate half-caste
cleared out, and is now —at 12 years old—a
diseased wreck Such is the “ protection ”of the
aborigines.

“ Rol.” : All the Bulletin authorities on
damper-making, so far, have omitted to mention
the need of sprinkling some cold ashes on the
bottom fire and on the top of the damper to keep
it from burning. True damper has r.o baking
powder in it. You might put a few ashes to help
it rise, but otherwise nothing but flour, water and
salt. The art is in the mixing. Damper requires
much kneading to make it a success, while the
powder loaf must be mixed as quickly as possible.
When baking in the bush with an oven, the best
way is to dig a hole the size of your oven. Put a
fire in and let it burn to ashes. Place the oven in,
so that it and the lid may get thoroughly warmed,
and then put in your powder-bread, when you
should see it rise instantly. The hole prevents
the wind from blowing cold on one side and thus
causing the loaf to be half-cooked. [Damper dis-
cussion now closed.]
* * * *
A Queensland bushman says that the
gidgee and opal go together. The same authority
declares that he never can be lost where there is
mulga, because there is always moss on the south
side of the stem.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will betaken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is tinder-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany .
San Remo: Some of your letter is worth print-
ing as showing the troubles of a newspaper : “ (1) Is your
litor a Scotchman? (2) Is it true that a Scotchman has
no sense of humor? I have contributed a few pars, to
vour pages and have been occasionally staggered by your
censorship methods. Thus in humorous pars, inserted,
you have frequently cut out the sentence containing the
joke, the backbone of the whole fabric. In another
instance you cut out nearly the best sentence (humorous)
of the par. To particularise, I draw your attention to
the censorship of my Queensland Tale of Sin
(‘ Aboriginalities,’ 21/l/’O4). I wrote : ‘ The Captain
stopped it (the drum stick, not the drama) with his eye.’
In my opinion, your alteration of the bracketed words
destroyed about the brightest sentence in the par. My
last sentence was: ‘The amount of hard praying and tough
testimony done by Charlie as a penance for his sin was
prodigious, but he was never the same old Charlie.’ Your
substitute, “Charlie repented,” was very weak compared
to the original sentence. Slips like these are rough
enough bn budding genius.” The chief tribulation of an
editor is that sawdust stuffing, that cheap mechanical
“ humor ” which the “ budding genius” reckons so fine.
The editor finds it weary, weary reading (as the public
would) and laboriously stamps it out to get the bit of
good metal of fact from the dross, and is repaid with the
contumely of the contributor. Fortunately there are
compensations—pay-day is one of them... .Tortoise : Just
in time for the soup Jo: A story, like a girl, should
not have thin arm5....0.C.0. : “Georgevitoh” too much
drawn out Exile: You stay at the wickets too long
for the runs you make... .W.T.R. : Ideaall right, but you
handle it poorly.. .G.II. : “Kidney Stew” not for our
menu T. 11.5. : Hardly up to the mark.. ..Nora :
You must do your longing in some other publication
Ku-Klux : The reason of the parson’s poor horse and the
priest’s prancing prad? Well, all Irishmen have a know-
ledge of horses, which is rare amongst the class of Saxon
that usually furnishes the parson J.L.L. : Nothing in
either. ..JohnH.: Good stuff badly handled.... Jaybell:
Painfully labored humor....Wilmot W. : Don’t make
such a fuss over trifles.. ..Will O. and T.H.A. : Select
better, and don’t send in such sheaves of stuff Quidam
and Hiphen : Rolled copy is an abomination... .Wo-wo-
Rung : Too long for its interest J.McC.: Versesflimsy,
prose sketch not well enough finished.... Beaumont S. :
Have you read “ Resurrection ” ? If not, read it, and
see your little story much better told in the first
chapters Len B. : May be a fact, but it’s not
an interesting fact Geebung and Genung: Pen-
names too like older contributor’s pseudonym, “Go-
bung.” Please alter... .W.C.S.: Incredible rubbish....
Tarboy : Might go, if you could get into half the space..

(passing over) of the crown is so commonly supposed to
mean the death of the sovereign that demise is generally-
taken to mean death D.D. : Your trouble is that you
reckon yourself a big gun and you are only a small bore.

THE COLINTON ESTATE, tlie property of W. J. H. MOORE, Esq., M.L.A., has for many years been known as the
Best Battening, the Best Watered, and the Warmest Winter Country within a radius of several hundred miles from
Brisbane. The BRISBANE RIVER flows right through the centre of the property, which has a frontage of over 18
miles to each bank, or upwards of 36 miles in all. The Balance of the Estate is watered by Seventeen Creeks with
frontages to same of over 111 miles.
The Terms are up to 20 years at per cent. Interest
10 per cent. Deposit and no other payment for 2 years,
with Non-Forfeiture Clause.
Large Areas and Liberal Options. No Residential Restrictions.
NO FEES will be charged for Surveys.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Enough of “ Reform ” parties; let us have
a “ Perform ” party !
Irvine economy about equals patching the
sleeve of the coat with the seat of the pants. It
is not economical and not decent.
“It is hard to say whether the increase
of the unemployed poor, or that of the unem-
ployed rich, is the greater social evil.”— Huxley.
Hobart Mercury :
At present the Legislative Council seems to have the
most weighty portion of public opinion on its side.
The “most weighty,” certainly : all the fat.
Victorian Opposition, hitherto misled by
Peacock, will in all probability find a new skipper
before the elections in Donald Mackinnon, member
for Prahran.
Members of N.S.W. Assembly still use
the terms “Colonial Secretary ” and “Colonial
Treasurer.” Why not “State Secretary” and
“ State Treasurer ” ?
Of Graham Berry’s colleagues in the Black
Wednesday Ministry, two remain alive and in
the public eye—Henry Cuthbert, M.L.C., and
Bryan O’Loghlen.
Said that Canadian Premier Laurier will
make Canada’s demand for independence in
making foreign treaties the chief plank in his
Government platform next elections.

Collins-street (Melb.) dailies as to the fact that
the time for the issue of electors’ rights for the
next Vic. State election is rapidly expiring. They

on the roll per the Argus Inquiry Agency, and
then voting the Labor ticket. More awful still is
the case of many who owe their franchise to the
Age efforts and who voted for Trenwith.

A cable, showing how the tariff is advan-
taging Australia:
Delegates representing the Staffordshire potteries are
proceeding to Australia to prospect the clay of the
country and inquire into the coal supply. It is proposed
to start large pottery works in Australia.
Queensland Premier Morgan can polish
off a deputation in fine style. Courteous, and
yet firm, Morgan creates a kind of majestic
impression that simply overawes most deputa-
tionists and reduces them to a sort of apologetic
attitude. Temperance cranks, religious-instruc-
tion faddists, discontented publicans, have all
besieged him of late—and all retired badly routed
by the smiling Premier.
Elated by its success at the Federal elec-
tions the Westralian Labor party is determined to
make every member of the expiring State As-
sembly fight for his seat unless he is hall-marked
by the Labor executive. Premier James and all
his Ministry will be opposed.

now to rely on the charity of the foreigner to be

A reciprocal arrangement has been entered into
between the Welsh tin-plat*, makers and the United
States Steel Trust, by which the former will buj’ the
Trust’s steel billets in return for the Trust’s subordinate
tin-plate company not exporting tin-plates to Britain.
T’other day a lorry drove up to a Mel-
bourne Government office, and the driver
laboriously rolled a heavy box into the doorway.
Thus unostentatiously arrived the Commonwealth
Seal. There will now probably be a rise in the
price of sealing-wax owing to increased consump-
tion caused by the introduction of this ponderous
instrument.
For the Ryde, N.S.W., by-election, the
Deform party ran a John Stinson and not a
Joseph Carruthers candidate: and Joseph found
himself unable to speak on his behalf: he was
either too Dusy or too ill. And from all appear-
ances, Joseph’s friends in the electorate voted for
the independent Terry rather than for the John
Stinson Deformer.
Dr. Holtze, director of the Adelaide
Botanical Gardens, who was for some years Govt.
Botanist in the Northern Territory, thinks that if
the transcontinental railway were built to Port
Darwin enough cotton could be grown in the
N.T. to supply the world, and that it would pay
the planters to carry up white cotton pickers and
employ them at decent wages for two or three
months of the year if reasonable fares were
charged on the line.
A Federal Arbitration Act extending to
railway servants does not mean—as the poisonous
daily press suggests—control by the Federal Govern-
ment of the conditions of State railway employ-
ment, but the power of reference to a court of
justice of those conditions in case of dispute. It
is not Federal control, but just control, that the
Act would give—the same just control as N.S.W.
Arbitration Court has over railway employees’
conditions in that State, and as the Federal High
Court has over disputes between a State Govern-
ment and an individual citizen.
Transvaal correspondent of London Daily
Chronicle :
As a matter of fact, nothing has actually been adduced
to show that there is a shortage of labor sufficient to
warrant the importation of 250,000 Chinese, or that any
shortage exists which could not be met by a proper
inducement in the shape of wages. Before the war there
was no cry for Chinese labor, because the mine owners
well knew the Transvaal Government would never have
permitted the Celestials to come. Now the balance of
power has shifted from the Government to the capitalists,
and the first step they take on the resumption of the
staple industry is to force this demand for cheap labor—
from anywhere you like, but at the lowest possible price.
From its beginning to its end the agitation has been not
one for labor, but for cheap labor

The Age has at least begun to show—
what well-informed persons have known all along
—that Victoria is really one of the most lightly-
taxed countries on earth, especially as regards
municipal taxation. The highest Victorian
municipal rate is that of Footscray—2s. 6d.—the
lowest that of Echuca 9d. (which latter, owing to
the dishonestly low valuation, is really about 3d.)
Against this compare Limerick’s 11s. 3d., Cork’s
9s. 9d., Wolverhampton’s Bs., Nottingham’s
7s. 9d., Bristol’s 7s. 4d., Manchester’s 6s. sfd.
S.M. Herald’s ’‘special correspondent”
at Thursday Island:
It was thought by many that the passing into law of
the Alien Immigration Restriction Bill would spell
disaster to Thursday Island. So far these gloomy
prognostications have not been enforced by experience.
The report was widely spread, when the Bill was before
Parliament, that the industry would be transferred from
Thursday Island to Dutch New Guinea. Mr. Douglas
(the Govt, resident) supplies a complete and conclusive
reply to these predictions by pointing to the fact that
during the past year not a single lugger has changed
headquarters.
Taverner, Vic. Minister for Lands, is
throwing open 150,000 acres for selection in the
Mallee before any provision for water supply has
been made. On the strength of this year’s un-
usual rainfall, young men are being deluded into
the belief that fortunes await them in a district
where drought is the normal condition. Still it
may serve to show Victoria that Irvine hasn’t
the pluck to even attempt to carry out the work
of compulsorily resuming some of the rich Wes-
tern lands, as he boasted of being about to do a
few months ago.

The Commonwealth Government has
shown the State Governments how to bring moral!
pressure to bear on those ex-members of Parlia-
ment who fain would stick to their free railway
passes. By allotting the free passes to constituen-
cies for the use of their representatives, it is made-
impossible for a new member to get the use of his
pass until the ex-member has returned the badge
to headquarters. So the new man brings the old
man up to the scratch.
The shire council of Kyneton (Vic.) is
another fine satire on Kyabram. It lately
endeavored to bluff the Government into pro-
mising £450 towards the repair of two bridges,
the shire to make up the balance (.£250) of the
required sum. Subsequently it .suggested that the
Government and the council should share and
share alike in the cost of these belated repairs,
and then the Government learned that the
Kyneton shire rate is only Is. in the £.
It was on the motion of John Quick, of
Bendigo, that the referendum was introduced —for
the establishment of the Commonwealth —into the
higher politics of Australia. But it was in muni-
cipal politics many years before. The payment
of mayors was voted on by referendum a decade
ago, the late Ninny Melville, ex-Chairman of
Committees (N.S.W.), a strong advocate of the
referendum, being one of the first deprived by
the ratepayers’ vote of his mayoral allowances.
“H.S.R.": Here is a platform for an
Australian party:—
(1) Australia always first.
(2) Preference to Australian-made goods.
(3) Abolition of State Governors and. Upper House*
(4) Australian navy and citizens’ army for defence only.
(5) Abolition of titles.
(6) No Australian to take up arms unless for the defence
of Australia. All Australians to bind themselves to-
defend a White Australia with arms.
(7) Taking the country out of pawn and keeping it out.

"Lord Tennyson to-day spoke a farewell message to
Australia into the phonograph at Government House.
The record will he preserved in the Public Library. The
directors have arranged that phonograph messages from
all the leading public men of Australia will be similarly
preserved.” —Sews Item.
The Vic. Government agricultural experts
having discovered that soft fruits —such as plums,
apricots, etc. —kept in good condition in a level
temperature of from 34° to 36° F. for at least
six weeks, the Argus is now demanding that the
Government shall establish cool stores in London.
Which is no doubt very desirable ; cool chambers-
here, properly maintained temperatures during
the voyage and cool stores at the other end, with
adequate departmental supervision and branding*
would shortly build up a great trade. But what
about the Socialism of it ?
THE “TIRED" VOTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VIC.
He tells you, when you ask his vote :
“ ’E takes no interest—
The cove’s a bloomin’, sapphire goat
’Oo don’t give votin’ best! ’’
When you advance some bitter facts,
He rubs his mouth, and grins :
“ Chuck ’istory to ’Alifax—
Wot do I care ’oo wins?—
So long’s I get my usual w’acks
Of blanky beers, and gins ! ”
When you point out, by voting, he
Might get a better wage—
He fills his pipe up languidly,
And eyes a sporting page.
“ Wot’s goin’ to win the Plate ? ” he asks,
Or “ ’Ow about the Match ? ”
His soul’s hid in the brewer’s casks—
He rubs his cranium-thatch.
Quite happy while in sport he basks,
And has a skull to scratch.
Inside the “ home” his weary wife
Sits mending Johnny’s pants.
For her, existence is a strife
With.rent-day cormorants.
The mindless toiler’s wife is worth
A thousand of himself !
The searing round of debt and birth
Makes her “ dead-keen on pelf.”
And so—with eyes long void of mirth—
She votes ; poor grey-faced elf.
Her husband smiles in dull contempt—
“'E takes no interest; ”
The mate who of some winners dreamt
Pleases his ears the best.
When you submit him bitter facts,
He rubs his mouth, and grins :
“ Chuck ’istory to ’Alifax—
Wot do I care ’oo wins ?”
He’S satisfied with usual whacks
Of lengthy beers—and gins !
G.H.

About the man who invented trams :
George Francis Train, who was shunted into the
“ mortuary station ” last week, started the horse trams
in London as well as in the United States. If he had
not plunged into politics thirty-five years ago, Train
might have become a millionaire. As a politician and
platform orator, George Francis was a sort of extrava-
gant enlargement of our own King O’Malley. He did his
best to pass himself off as a “screaming eagle,” and
enjoyed the joke when the American public classed him
as a barn-door fowl. But for his ever-present sense of
humor, Train might have been accepted seriously when
he ran for the Presidency. His “ platform ” consisted of
women’s right to vote, total abstinence, and self-goverm
ment for Ireland. Train had managed to keep going on
cold water all his life, and he was down on grog. He
visited Australia in 1854, and on his return home boasted
that he had enjoyed the pure, cold water of the Yarra
Yarra and Parramatta rivers. In 1863 Train went to
Ireland on a lecturing tour. He was arrested and gaoled
as an agent of the Fenian Brotherhood. As there was no
evidence against him he was released, but a few weeks
later a writ for debtua* served on him by an English
firm, and he was imprisoned a second time. Swindled
out of his interest in the English tramways, George
Francis went back to America.
A WOMAN’S COMPLEXION.
It is rank foolishness to attempt to remove
sallowness or greasiness of the skin by the use of
cosmetics, or “local” treatment, as advocated by
the “beauty doctors.” The only safe and sure
way that a woman can improve her complexion is
by purifying and enriching the blood, which can
only be accomplished by keeping the liver healthy
and active. The liver is the seat of disease and
blood pollution. Chamberlain’s Stomach and
Liver Tablets act directly on the liver, cleanse
and enrich the blood, and purify the complexion.
They also cure constipation, biliousness, nervous-
ness, and induce refreshing sleep. A single box of
Chamberlain’s Stomach and Liver Tablets has
been known to cure the most pronounced and
distressing case of indigestion. Sold everywhere.

SYDNEY.
Burroughs, Welcome and Co., English
manufacturing chemists, secured, in the English
Chancery Court, last December, an injunction
against another firm selling drugs as “tabloid.”
8., 'W. and Co. claimed to have invented and
copyrighted that word, and the cla.in was sus-
tained. The Bulletin remembers 8., W. and
Co.’s Australian representative claiming, some
months ago, that we must not use the word
“tabloid,” except in description of the firm’s
goods; but didn’t consent to be frightened off the
use of a rather handy and elegant word in that
way. It’s 8., W. and Co.’s word for trade pur-
poses ; for literary purposes, the firm should
donate it to the language.
“ P.T.F.” : Present system of subsidising
mail steamers for war emergencies is a reversal of
an ancient English custom. In the reign of
Henry 111., the royal ships were hired to mer-
chants for freight purposes, and the custom did
not end till the reign of Elizabeth, late in the
sixteenth century. The earlier trading voyages
to Italy and the Levant, during the last quarter
of the fifteenth century, were all made by hired
men of war. Merchants found it cheaper to hire
warships than to build vessels capable of resisting
pirates. \

“F.”: Somebody protests that the term
“boshter” doesn’t rightly apply to David Syme,
of Melb. Age, seeing that “boshter” in the
Australian slanguage means “a splendid person
or thing.” Well, splendid means famous in the
dictionary, not to say conspicuous. David Syme
has been long noted for his political power
among Victorians. In his political aspect he was
a “ baffled boshter” when Trenwith got to the top
of the Vic. poll at the election of Senators.

Debility Promptly Ended.
Bile Beans are Invaluable During the
Summer Season.
With Summer comes that weary, wornout feel-
ing. Women, more particularly, are sufferers in
this respect, owing partly to their confinement in
the house, and not getting plenty of fresh air, and
owing a great deal to the delicate constitution of
the female organs, which give way under the strain
of work and worry. To all women who are feeling
“done up” at this time of the year, a course of
Bile Beans will prove of immense benefit. They
brace up and give tone to the various organs, thus
strengthening the whole system. As a striking
illustration, Mrs. E. J. Ramus, of 1 Arundle-
street, Forest Lodge, Sydney, says: —“Your Bile
Beans have proved a valuable remedy in my
household. I am sometimes troubled with De-
bility, especially during the hot weather, and
when I feel ‘ done up ’ with a tired, listless feel-
ing, I take a Bean or two, with beneficial results.
As a gentle purgative, they are all that can be
desired, and leave no evil after-effects whatever.
I can safely say Bile Beans are unequalled for
poor appetite, sleeplessness, and that out-of-sorts
feeling so prevalent at the present season.” Bile
Beans have been proved a reliable cure for Bilious-
ness, Headache, Indigestion, Constipation, Piles,
Debility, Female Weaknesses, Nervousness, Bad
Blood, Bad Breath, Anaemia, Insomnia, Loss of
Appetite, Summer Fag, and, in fact, all ailments
ar sing from defective liver action. Bile Beans
are obtainable from all medicine vendors, price
Is. lgd., or 2s. 9d. large box.*
A UNIQUE EXPERIENCE.
“ A lady customer of mine was so pleased with
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy, which she had
used for her children when suffering from colds
and croup, that during a fortnight’s time she
obtained at my shop,” writes F. W. J. Fletcher,
chemist, Home’oush, “ nine bottles, which she
sent to her friends in different parts of the State,
telling them how much good Chamberlain’s
Cough Remedy had done, and advised them to
give it a trial.” Sold everywhere, Is. 6d.*
There would be some points in a Chow-
canning industry. Two ancient returning cab-
bage-growers died on a Chinese steamer a while
ago, some days out from Thursday Island. The
other Mongols on board waited on the ship’s
doctor, and asked the cost of embalming. He
wanted £l5 apiece. The Celestials paid up
cheerfully. Between 400 and 500 Chinese die
in Australia yearly : a canning factory that
would tin the corpses for export to China at,
say, £lO a head, would make money.

FOR THE DEAF.
Realising that there is a great public need for
better knowledge of the causes, prevention, and
cure of deafness, an English popular medical
journal devoted the whole of its last issue to a
treatise on defective hearing, the ear, nose and
throat ailments. Anatomy, physiology, hygiene,
and treatment are described in language suitable
for popular reading, the work forming, in fact, a
pamphlet which everyone should possess. Copies
of this work are ready for free distribution, and to
obtain one, the reader has only to send name and
address to—“ Review ”c/o box 241 G.P.O. Sydney.
With the “ Review ” there is also a report form by
means of which anyone can obtain free advice by
post.*

Legal Notice.
WHEREAS by an Order of the Chancery
Division of the High Court of Justice
made in the Matter of the Estate of Clara Sparrow
deceased Williams against Swallow 1902 S. 2551
the following enquiries were directed namely (1)
An enquiry who was the heir at law of the Intes-
tate the said Clara Sparrow at the time of her
death (the 22nd day of November 1899) and
whether such heir at law is living or dead and if
dead who by devise descent or otherwise is
entitled to such real Estate if any of the said
Intestate as descended to such heir at law. And
(2) An enquiry who were the persons entitled by
virtue of or according to the Statute of distribution
of Intestate Estates to the personal estate of the
said Clara Sparrow living at the time of her death
and whether any of them are since dead and if so
who are their respective legal personal representa-
tives.
Notice is hereby given that all persons claiming
under the said Enquiries or the legal personal
representatives of such of them as may be dead
are by their Solicitors on or before the 21st day of
April 1904 to come in and prove their claims at
the Chambers of Mr. Justice Far well and Mr.
Justice Swinfen Eady at the Royal Courts of
Justice Strand London England or in default
thereof they will be peremptorily excluded from
the benefit of the said Order Thursday 'the 28th
day of April 1904 at 11.30 of the clock in the
forenoon at the said Chambers is appointed for
hearing and adjudicating upon the claims.
Dated this 23rd day of December 1903
Note —The said Clara Sparrow sometimes called
Hannah Sparrow was at the time of her death the
widow of William Chambers Sparrow of Grafton
Street North Melbourne in the Colony of Victoria
Custom. House-Officer deceased, and for some time
previously she was living at Hawthorn Villa
Underdale Road Shrewsbury in the County of
Salop in England and it is alleged, that she had a
child who was known by the name of Philip
Henry Seymour who is believed to be resident at
Sydney, New South .Wales. .i
J. C. FOX

Australians in London.
“ L.M.” sends some gossip of artistic Australians
now in London:
I went to see Harold Parker yesterday. Through a
biting wind, that presently developed into a fall of snow,
I fought my way with a friend to the Stamford Bridge
studios, off the Fulham-road. Who is Harold Parker?
A sculptor hailing from Brisbane. For some time he
studied there under Godfrey Rivers. Seven years ago he
left Australia and came to London to study further. The
other day someone said to me : “ Do you know Harold
Parker? He is a sculptor, and comes from Australia.”
I thought hard. I couldn’t recall even his name.
That well-known sculptor, John Tweed, had seen
Harold Parker’s work, and had said of it—“ It is some of
the finest stuff I have seen.” John Tweed gave, and is
giving, Harold Parker what work he can give, and help-
ing him in the generous way a famous artist helps an
unknown, but clever, younger brother. Why doesn’t
Brisbane commission this poor struggling fellow to do its
Queen Victoria ?—if it must have a Queen Victoria.
Last year Harold Parker exhibited at the Academy, for
the first time. His work was a girl’s head, called
“Esther.” Simple, unconscious, it scarcely prepared
one for the piece of sculpture he is at present working at,
an “ Ariadne ”as lovely as can be imagined. It is this
Ariadne that has provoked Mr. Tweed’s admiration.
Ariadne is shown as a kneeling woman, her hands held
out before her in pleading.
Sculptors have been banding together lately, with a
view_ to guarding their interests. A meeting of the
leading London men was held at Derwent Wood’s
house last week ; George Frampton in the chair, and
nearly forty artists present. They discussed their
grievances in a large studio, opening off the front hall, a
room which is known as “ the Museum,” by Derwent
Woods, but which is, in effect, our Florence Schmidt’s
music-room, with her grand piano at one end, and her
songs and operas lying about.
Derwent Wood is passionately fond of music, and when
at home is generally to be found somewhere in the
vicinity of the piano, which his wife plays while he sings.
The Other day Florence Schmidt went over to her hus-
band’s studio at Chelsea, about half an hour’s walk from
their home. Picking up some clay, she cried, “ You sing
my songs ! I’m going to model in your clay ! ” and pro-
ceeded to mould Derwent Wood’s head. In a few minutes
the soprano had turned out a quite remarkable likeness
of her sculptor, who was delighted with her effort. “By
Jove ! you know, there’s something about that I wish to
goodness I could get into my work I It’s quite early-
Florentine 1 ”
Does anyone in Sydney remember Miss Darragh ? She
came out with Cartwright on his last and somewhat un-
lucky tour to Australia, was fair-haired, slight, sprightly,
and always exquisitely gowned. In “ The Idler ” she
played the American girl; in “Moths” she was Lady
Dolly ; but, whatever she was, she never created any en-
thusiasm except over her gowns. That was six years
ago. And now ? Well, now she stands acclaimed by
London critics as one of the few "great actresses.” The
praise poured pown on her is almost wildly extravagant.
It is rather annoying to think that no one in Australia
ever foretold this success, or even hinted at its proba-
bility. As a matter of fact, Miss Darragh was looked
upon as the least striking actress in the whole company.
Another actor who didn’t make any stir in Australia,
but is now doing big work in London, is Basil Gill, late
of Wilson Barrett’s co., now Tree’s leading man. He and
his pretty wife, Marjorie, have settled in England for some
years, and bought a house at Putney.
The Australian Ladies’ Vocal Quartette (ugly name !)
is quite an institution now in London. The present
composition is Kate Rooney, Kate Traill, Queenie Cadden
and Constance Evelyn. But there is shortly to be a
change, Miss Evelyn leaving.
Few singers have done as well as Alice Hollander in
London. She goes steadily and rapidly onward, and
figures at most of the leading concerts—her name in big
letters—among the stars.
Melb. Argus unintentionally dated one of
its pages in accordance with its opinions the other
day, thus:
THE ARGUS, FRIDAY, JANUARY 22, 1094.
Melbourne clerks have of late been weary-
ing the public with pitiful tales of sweating, but
when the Clerks’ Union called a meeting lately to
discuss grievances and propose remedies, only 45
of the white-handed sweated attended, and
several of them declined to have anything to do
with a body that was affiliated with the Trades
Hall Council.
A retired Indian officer, now visiting Aus-
tralia, told a Perth interviewer, the other day,
that there exists a great deal of unrest among the
natives of India, and it would take very little to
make them revolt. A desire for independence is,
he says, the sole cause of the trouble.
Complained again that, though the
S’African war medals were distributed two years
back, the bars to go with them have not yet
reached the heroes, who miss them sorely. It is
about the only sort of bar they can hope to breast
now.
The prickly pear, besides having saved
thousands of cattle during the drought, is now
discharging another useful function. There have
been several cases lately of people lost in the
Queensland bush who have sustained life by
eating the fruit. An old woman of 70 years, lost
for several days in the Rockhampton district,
admitted when found that she had lived on pear
all the time, and found herself little the worse
for her experience.
A sensible correspondent to the daily
papers: —
The teaching in State schools of English history instead
of Australian in the early stages is an unpardonable
anomaly. In fact, most children attending small schools
never learn a word of Australian history. I have been
repeatedly asked how such names as Lang, Wentworth,
Dailey, &c., were given to the Federal electorates. At
present the usual custom is to start the history instruc-
tion with a people called Celts, and teach a lot of more or
less fabulous stories about Saxons, Danes, Romans, Nor-
mans, &c., which neither interest nor instruct. Should
not a thoroughly detailed account of our own history be
taught to the future rulers of the country ?
“K.K.” gives impressions of a wallaby-
tour through Victoria: —
Daylesford.—Every second man you meet looks like an
undesirable immigrant, and it’s 10 to 1 his name ends
with “ otti,” “ ini,” or “ zoni.”
Ballarat.—Tie a piece of blue ribbon on your coat (if
you have one), and talk admiringly of John Wesley. Also
say something disparaging of Bendigo, and you will have
the freedom of the city presented to you, with a bottle
of lemonade.
Hamilton.—lf you want a job, and a man asks you a
question, pretend to be deaf, and say, loudly, “ Hoot!
A’m Scotch.” That will fetch the job nine cases out of
ten.
Gippsland.—When you strike a hamlet call on the
president of the local Deform League and deplore the
present trend of Socialistic legislation. Say you believe
in eight hours for farm laborers. (Eight before dinner
and eight after— with an hour or two extra during the
busy season.)
Bendigo.—Ourse Bent, praise Lansell, and scoff at
Ballarat.
Prosser v. Sheane, in the City of London
Court a few weeks ago, before Judge Rentoul,
was a lovely case. A clerk borrowed £2O from a
shark in 1886 at 25 per cent. He could not repay.
The sum swelled at compound interest. By the
year 1903 it had mounted up to £lOO, which he
bad paid in interest, and the plaintiff sued for a
a further £153! Said the Judge :“I will make
an order for one farthing a month.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Mayor Cohen, of Adelaide, was very sore about
certain badinage indulged in at his expense by cricketer
Bosanquet in a letter written to the St. James’ Gazette,
London. Matters have been set right by the cricketer,
who sent an apology in which he assures Mr. Cohen that
what was taken for ridicule was meant in sober earnest.
* . « * *
Joe Darling, ex-captain of the Australian
Eleven, is doing very well on his Tasmanian holding and
will not sacrifice his prospects by playing further cricket.
Performances in the inter-State match gave
little information as to prospective changes in the test
team. M'Leod failed as a bowler, but was a great batting
success; Hopkins failed yet again in batting, but saved
his bacon by his form with the ball. Cotter’s meteoric
flash (his career of success lasted for about three over 3)
fairly won the match for his side, but, unless followed up
in the coming match N.S.W. v. England, will not get him
in the fourth test. The Australian selectors, with Warner
one game in, can’t throw away chances, or indulge in any
specs.
r * * * *
Laver, as captain of the Victorians in the
last inter-State match, appeared to trifle with the
chances of his side in the interests of Harry Trott.
The veteran was bowled right out, and 134 runs were
scored off him. Armstrong, whose position in the test
match was assured, was not put on to bowl until 360
runs were scored ; only 29 runs were made afterwards,
and Armstrong’s figures were three for 10. In test
matches Armstrong is usually second change. The
proper wav to bowl Harry Trott would seem to be a few
overs every now and then. When he is persevered with
the batsmen get used to his slows, and help themselves
with impunity.
