“At first it was Mr. P. F. Warner’s Eleven;
then, after two or three successes, it became the Maryle-
bone Eleven ; after a few more wins, the England Eleven !
Nothing succeeds like success.”— English paper.
* * * *
“ Bulli ” : Comparing the attendance at the
Queensland v. England fixture with that at the third test
match (in Adelaide), it should be good business to play a
test-match at Brisbane instead of Adelaide. The esti-
mated number who witnessed Q. v. England struggle was
—Friday, 6000; Saturday, 14,000 ; and Monday, 1500—
practically as many as watched the test. Almost double
that number could be depended upon if a test were
played at Brisbane. The wicket and ground are quite
equal to Adelaide’s.
* * * *
Claxton, of S.A., whom many consider worthy
of test match honors, put up a good all-round perform-
ance last Saturday, scoring 127 not out, and getting three
wickets for 35 runs.
* * * *
Tasmanian team sprung something of a surprise
on Warner’s combination when, after putting only 141
together in their opening effort, there were but three out
for 350 in the next. First partnership (Savigney and
Douglas) of second innings produced 202, and Savigney,
at stumps draw, was 164 not out. Every member of the
visiting combination had a trial with the ball. British
first innings score was 353.
* * * *
Hopkins’ 30 wickets tops all other Australian
bowlers’ bags in first-class cricket this season, and the
only Britisher ahead of him is Rhodes with 42.
* ♦ * *
About the most remarkable cricket incident on
record is reported to have taken place in a Melbourne
Junior Association competition. It was the final match
of the series, and the result was practically a foregone
conclusion for the team that had to come ten miles to
play. On the road out the overworked van-horses went
on strike and refused to budge. The ground was three
miles distant and the match due to start in 20 minutes.
The captain knew’ that if he was not ready to play, the
game must be forfeited, and a certain victory lost. He
accordingly struck a bargain with a passing cyclist, and
arrived on the scene five minutes before time, won the
toss and sent the other side in, declining all assistance in
the field. Ten minutes were occupied in getting ready,
and the first ball went to the boundary for 4. The soli-
tary player strolled leisurely after it, carried it up to the
crease, and after further delay bowled again, which re-
sulted in another 4. In the meantime the others of the
team were arriving on foot, and the game commenced in
earnest, resulting in the expected win for the visitors.
* * * *
In North v. South Island match (Maoriland),
played at Wellington on January 21, 22, 23, two ex-
Australians—Graham, of Melbourne, and Callaway, of
Sydney (coaches respectively at Dunedin and Christ-
church)—battled for the South Island. Graham- was
troubled with that old internal wound of his. He made
10 runs, and was clean bow’led by Aucklander Stemson.
Callaway was in good form with the bat, but was better
with the ball. He made 17 in four hits, and secured four
wickets for 19 runs.
* * • •
A bookmakers’ strike brought the Ballarat
Miners’Turf Club meeting to “an untimely end” (vide
Age report). Hatwood, an odds-on favorite, appeared to
be winning the January Handicap in the proverbial
canter, when an outsider came along in a hurry and
appeared to beat the dead-bird decisively on the post.
The judge, however, was positive that Hatwood won by
a head, and as he refused to be scared out of his decision
by the howls and yells of the angry Hebrew, as many of
him as were there present took off his bag and
collectively discontinued betting. Needless to say,
the demonstration against the judge was all on the
part of Israel. If an odds-on favorite had appeared
to beat a rank outsider and the judge had decided
against the public fancy, and the public had hooted, the
poor man would have been warmly cheered by his admir-
ing champion, Moses aforesaid.
“L.W.”: After all, horse-racing is an utterly I
useless form of sport, and a demoralising influence
generally. The old contention that it improved the
breed of horses is the wildest joke ever put forward in
the disguise of an excuse. About the only sort of moke
which doesn’t race nowadays is the splendid upstanding
Clydesdale draught—the most useful as well as the finest
sample of horseflesh. Under the euphonious terms of
“ ponies ” and “little ’uns,” every miserable, undersized
equine beast is made, providing it has any speed qualifi-
cations, a medium for gambling, and a means to the
physical deterioration of small boys who are engaged to
ride the rubbish. It may not be able to travel faster
than a healthy schoolboy, but providing a dozen or so
others, weak and miserable as itself, can be got together,
it becomes the best of its “ class,” men bet on it, and
people pay admission to see it flounder along. The
justification of all sport is the improvement of the
species. Cricket, iootball, running, jumping, boxing,
rowing, swimming, and even billiards, are all justified on
this ground; but horse-racing can only plead that it
turns out spielers, bookmakers, and undersized jockeys.

started at 4 and 5 to 1 against, and the anxiety of the
bookmakers overnight to lay against her filled the minds
of punters wdth dark suspicions. The mare was bred at
Durham Court stud by Metal from Devotion, by Beau,
champ from Brenda, by The Tester. She won thre p
races in Northern N.S.W. during 1901-2 season. Time
(2.36) is a record for the race since its increase to 1J miles
nine years ago.

Bitter antipathy still exists in England to Aus-
tralian starting gate, where it is blamed for delays and
poor starts that ought never to occur under proper
management or unless deliberately caused. What was
possible under old conditions was shown in the English
“ Racing Calendar ” for October 21. 1859 : —Northallerton
and North Riding Meeting.—North York Nursery Hand icap
Plate of £lOO, given by the fund, for two year olds.
Thirteen starters. After more than an hour had been
fruitlessh' spent in endeavoring to start them, the
acting steward declared the race void. Jockies Snowden
and Bullock were suspended, and Withington and
Grimmer were fined £lO each.” Which explains why the
horses couldn’t be started.

“0.K.”: There is no truth in the statement
that “The V.R.C. waited on the postal authorities with
a brassy request that they should be allowed a censorship
over all messages banded into telegraph offices on race-
courses.” The V.R.C. has taken no part in the trouble,
which cropped up a while ago between the Telegraph
Department and the proprietors of sundry Melbourne
suburban racing “ clubs,” who would like to exercise
a censorship over messages despatched from the course.
Neither would the object of such censorship be “to
prevent the despatch of racing results to hotels for the
convenience of betters who didn’t go near the course.”
The matter in dispute is just this. Some of the suburban
racecourse bosses reckoned that the attendance of
punters at their precious meetings would be increased if
there were no facilities for telegraphing from the
course the names of the intended starters for
each race half an hour before the event. The
list of starters, being sent along to Melbourne
and elsewhere, is circulated among the s.p. bookmakers
in town. Their punting clients are thus enabled to pick
their fancy after the starters’ numbers are hoisted, the
same as though they were at the scene of action. To
prevent this enlightenment of the stay-at-home public,
the racecourse proprietors were at first inclined to dis-
pense with telegraph-offices altogether. Finding that
the proposed remedy was worse than the disease, they
had the gall to ask for authority to prevent the despatch
of telegrams intended to convenience s.p. bookmakers
and punters. Their claim to control in anyway the work-
ing of a public telegraph service was preposterous from
the political point of view ; nevertheless, its avowed
object, although purely selfish, has the sympathy of
everyone who knows the evils of s.p. betting in town.
Better that another twenty, fifty, or a hundred infatuated
punters should go to the racecourse to gamble than that
a thousand should develop the pernicious habit of haunt-
ing a bookmaker’s shady den for the purpose of picking a
fancy from the telegraphed list of starters.
ifc * * *
Crack Sydney jockey A. Richardson made a
big punch at Glen Innes last week. He had a mount in
every race of a two days meet, winning five, and finishing
second in five others. Recruit, handled by Richardson,
scored thrice and was second once. Richardson is
identical with the lad who won Melbourne Cup on Clean
Sweep, Caulfield Cup on Sweet Nell, and Australian Cup
on Great Soot.
* * * *
Cabled that Russian Hackenschmidt defeated
gigantic Turk Madralli in London, Saturday last, at
Graeco-Roman wrestling, and won the world’s cham-
pionship. Contest only occupied 44 seconds, through
the Turk’s shoulder being dislocated. Match was made
December 23 after much talk, during which Hacken-
schmidt stuck hard and fast for Graeco-Roman, though
he is a good catch-as-catch-can man too. Turk wanted
catch-as-catch-can because he knew little of any other,
but, being the more eager for the struggle, had to give
way. Madralli is a marvel of physical power, and
weighs 18st., while Hackenschmidt is a few stone
lighter, but, judged by the great amount of muscle he
displays, is probably almost as strong.
* * * *
At the New Year sports at Mulgrave (N.Q.)
there was a five-minutes beer drinking competition ; it
was won easily by a gentleman known as the “ Stingaree
of the North,” who swallowed 17 long sleevers. A Cairns
paper points out that this was by no means equal to the
“ Stingaree’s ” previous pay-day performance and that
something better might reasonably have been expected
of him.
* * * •
Fourth round (Saturday) of Sydney bowling
pennant matches resulted in Manly and Waverley
dropping out and Redfern, St. George, Chatswood, Peter-
sham and Victoria Park being left with but one life while
City remained undefeated. Coker’s (City) rink, which has
performed remarkably well previously, went down heavily
on latest occasion, beaten 25 to 11 by Fitzgerald’s
(Waverley) four.
* • * *
Latest London craze, performed at Crystal
Palace Circus for the first time Christmas Eve and which
has taken the place of “ Looping the Loop,” is “ Flying
the Flume.” A cycliste starts from the top of the track,
about 70 feet from the ground, and reaching the bottom
where there is a gentle upward curve, plunges through
mid-air (carried by the impetus gained in the descent) to
alight 50ft. further away. This startling ride is done by
Mademoiselle Helen Detrieu.
* * * •
Contest for N.S.W. 220 yards swimming
supremacy at Goulburn was badly managed. Cavill,
Sobraon boy Keran, and others streaked over consider-
ably more than the required distance. Cavill alleges
that deliberate interference hampered him seriously, and
Keran lays a similar charge, but Cavill’s position as the
winner ■is unassailable. Latest display by Keran
endorses the forecast, printed last week, that he’ll
be on the top plane ere long—his latest was a decided
improvement on the first effort in public, a week before.
* * * *
Billed to go against his world’s “ hundred ” at
North Sydney Swimming Carnival on Saturday, Dick
Cavill begged to be excused through not feeling “ too
well,” but all the same raced and finished in 59sec. —lsec.
ahead of the best accomplished by any swimmer in the
world bar an Australian.
* * * *
There seems to be an increase in the public
interest in matters natatorial, North Sydney District
Swimming Club having to turn money away from the
Lavender Bay Floating Baths on Saturday, when the
club held its second annual carnival. Even the roof was
■crowded, and the people thronged so thickly that the
timid ones among the audience feared “ something would
happen.” This fear was increased when they heard the
noise of hundreds of feet on the roof. The chief
item of the afternoon was the stubborn race between
Greenwood and Tartakover for the Northern Suburbs
100yds. championship. Greenwood, who was favorite,
won, after a desperate effort, by not more than 9in. or
lOin. in lmin. 7 l-ssec.

in Melbourne Saturday and Monday, were poorly at-
tended. Most noteworthy things done were G. W.
Smith’s (N.Z.) creation of a new Australasian 440 yards
hurdles record—sB£ (prior best was Holder’s 58 4-5), N.
Barker’s (N.S.W.) making of a fresh Victorian best for the
furlong (22 3-10), Ferguson’s new Vic. figures (15.30) for 3
miles, Sutton’s Australasian half-mile record—l.sl 4-5
(same man putup 1.574-5 at Bendigo recently,but that has
not been endorsed yet), and O’Reilly’s Victorian record
weight put—39ft. 2|in. Victoria, now for the first time,
owns inter-State supremacy with 6 points. N.S.W.
finished second, and M.L. third—3 points. Preceding
five meetings saw M.L. score thrice and N.S.W. twice. A
dead-heat between Barker,(N;S.W.) and Hunter (Vic.) in
the 100yds. had to be decided Wednesday, and if N.S.W.
won, that State’s points would be level with Victoria, but
latter, thiough holding majority of seconds, must remain
on top.

“ Crab ” : Again world - champion sculler
Towns’attitude is peculiar. Before Tresidder became ill
nothing definite could be got from Towns. While
Tresidder lav sick Towns expressed anxiety for a match
but didn’t want to “ force” his rival “ to fix an immediate
date ” Now Tresidder is well—2st. above his rowing
weight—and has challenged through the papers and
lodged £25 for a match for £5OO aside and has also written
a letter to Towns at his private residence. Weeks have
passed, but Towns is still dumb aiid is said to have in view
a trip to ’Murka. Surely Towns cannot thus ignore a
legitimate claimant like Tresidder and retain the cham-
pion title.

The Maoriland Cornishman, Bob Fitzsimmons,
after his fight with Gardner, told an interviewer that he
had definitely retired from “ plug.” “ I will never fight
again,” he said. “I am getting old, and I believe there
are yet good young men to develop.”
J 6 * ’ * * *
Light-weight boxing premiership of Queensland,
fought at Brisbane, Thursday night, was one-sided and
wearying. Costa led from the opening till he knocked
Moore out in the 12th round.
* * * *
At Sydney National Sporting Club last week
Fleming and that colored streak Blakesley fought to an
almost empty house. It was their second meeting and
on the former shake-up Fleming completely outclassed
the other. Occasionally there was some hard hitting,
but there was never any doubt that Fleming would win
the second fight as easily as he did the first. At the close
of the 20th round the referee yawned, stretched his arms
and declared Fleming the winner.
* * * ♦
Tasmanian light-weight, “Billy” Maher, col-
lapsed last week in Melbourne when he met “Cake-
walker” Savral. Maher rushed in to finish it with a
shower of savage blows, but the negro stood up and gave
swing for swing, and hooked and jolted with equal fero-
city. Maher’s condition was not what it ought to have
been, and in the second round his strength petered out;
then the negro, much the stronger man, rushed Maher,
and the white was too exhausted to keep him off, and fell
down utterly done for. A second battle is talked of. The
white man was so confident of winning that he insisted
the loser should receive only £5 of the gate-money. At
the start, betting was 4 to 1 on him, and during the first
round 5 to 2 was freely offered. No one guessed the
negro had so much sand in him.

At Poverty Point.
Melba’s wonderful chest is no longer weather-proof.
Maybe she weakened it for European purposes by spend-
ing a summer in Australia after she had turned 42. Any-
way, the cable reports that she is recovering from a
severe attack of bronchitis at Monte Carlo.
Theie is a craze in London just now for a kind of chant,
of American origin, called “ Hiawatha.” It started in
the music halls, but has now been dropped into “ The Girl
from Kay’s,” and entrusted to Carrie Moore. “ The song
isn’t much,” says Black and White, “ but it’s made a
good deal of by the girl who sings it; an Australian,
pretty girl too, and deuc.ed clever.”
The younger members of the Musgrove Shakspearian
co. left Melbourne by the P. and O. boat for London last
week. Nora Kerin, Dora Rignold, Margaret Cellier,
Norman Partridge, Holding, and Lathbury were on the
passenger list, likewise Alfred Bucklaw, the melancholy
Jacques of the crowd, who has ceased to be young.
“ McDuff ” : lie “ Haresfoot’s ” inquiry for Barlow, the
“ Blue-Tail Fly.” He is living in Crown Koad, Gympie
(Q.), very feeble and pretty hard up. His wife is with
him. The old couple try to make the best of it.
The English basso, Mr. Watkin Mills, w'ill be heard this
coming season throughout Australia, Maoriland and
Tasmania. Mr. W. Spencer Jones, his manager, sails
from ’Frisco for Sydney on 3rd March.
Died last week, aged 90, Wm. Beaumont, an old caterer
for public amusements. Arrived in Sydney in 1840, and
established the pleasure grounds and hotel at Botany,
known as Sir Joseph Banks. For years he had an exten-
sive menagerie of birds and beasts, long before a public
Zoo was thought of. In the late 50’s, “ Beaumont’s ”
comprised hotel, pleasure grounds, Zoo, a circus and
other attractions. Later on Frank Smith got hold of the
Sir Joseph Banks grounds and established pedestrianism
and pony races.
Latest of Nance O’Neil is that she has gone “ on at the
halls.” In recent law action against her and Bankin
plaintiff alleged—“lt is a case of Trilby and Svengali.
From the stage of my theatre I have seen M‘Kee Rankin
exert his influence over Miss O’Neil. When he looked at
her she forgot her lines, and when he snapped his fingers
at her she would act. He has an undue influence over
her, and kept her separate from other actors and
managers.” Miss O’Neil denied this yarn. And, any-
how, if Mr. Rankin “kept her separate from other
actors ” it showed his sense.
After years of continuous vaudeville in two places,
Wellington (M.L.) now has to subsist theatrically on the
Opera House. Dix’s Theatre Royal has been closed down,
by order of the City Council, as unsafe in case of fire,
and Fuller has shut down His Majesty’s (late Choral Hall)
to effect alterations that will give more accommodation—
and fire exits. Dix is fighting the shut-up order in the
law courts.
“ Only Me ” : Was in New York recently and attended
a performance of the ill-fated “ Mr. Bluebeard” at the
Knickerbocker Theatre. Eddie Foy, who behaved so
pluckily at the Chicago fire, took the part of Sister Anne.
The greatest gorgeousness of the performance is in the
second act, scene s—“ The Triumph of the Magic Fan,”
in which the Grigolati troupe of serialists perform. It
reaches its climax in the flight of the premiere, Fraulein
Else Haerting, through the auditorium, over the heads
of the audience, distributing flowers. And it was the
wires used in this “ climax ’’ that prevented the asbestos
curtain from being lowered in the Iroquois Theatre,
Chicago, when the fire broke out.
“L. 8.” : My complaint is that Rickards has been giving
us too much strong man, and strong woman, and animal
and mere cleverness, and not enough human interest.
[Human interest! Have you seen Vulcana’s back? —Ed.
B.] The last time I went to Rickards’ show, in Melbourne,
Marzella was there with an assortment of poultry that
did clever but wearisome things. Then there was a lock
and handcuff expert, and now there are Atlas and
Vulcana. All these things may be very clever, but they
are out of place in a theatre or a music hall. What we
want at a variety entertainment is good character singing
and dancing of all kinds. Give us a Peggy Pryde, or a
Marie Lloyd, or a George Roby, but preserve us from the
fiend w'ho can play seventeen instruments and none of
them well ; or the expert with his trained troupe of
dancing wallabies. In an acrobatic turn the acrobatics
should be subordinated to the comedy busitbss. For
that reason the Wiltons, Derenda and Breen, the Harveys
and some others pass. Winton’s ventriloquist turn also
passes, because the ventriloquism is subordinated to the
“business.”
“ E.F.” : Adah Isaacs Menken has been revived as a
subject for controversial reminiscences in London
Referee. E. T. Smith, Melbourne L. L. Smith’s father,
was manager of the theatre (Ashley’s) where the lady
made her famous appearance as Mazeppa in skin-tights.
At the time of her Mazeppa notoriety Adah Menken-
more beautiful in form than face, and more magnetic
than beautiful—was on friendly terms with, amongst
others, Algernon Swinburne, and the poet immortalised
her in his “ Dolores.” Menken, like Daudet’s “ Sapho,”
profligated among men of genius, for the genius of
literature and art has the Jewish strain in its blood, and
Menken was a Jewess :
Ah, thy people, thy children, thy chosen,
Marked cross from the womb and perverse !
They have found out the secret to cozen
The gods that constrain us and curse ;
They alone, they are wise and none other ;
Give me place, even me, in their train,
O my sister, my spouse, and my mother,
Our Lady of Pain.
Thus sang the red-haired bard with vague allusion to this
Lady. Heaven knows how many Names might have been
tattooed on her “heavy white "limbs.” Not all that she
got credit for captivating, but a good many. The only
photo of “ Monte Christo ” Dumas I ever saw was in a
shop window in Chancery Lane, London. Old man
Dumas, a big grey-headed fellow in his shirt and trousers,
with his braces hanging loose, sat on a chair, and Menken
sat on his knees, one arm round his neck. Incidentally
Adah had some lawful husbands—seven altogether, she
told Thomas Buchanan Read (the author of “ Sheridan’s
Ride ”) in her death-bed confidences. The first man to
formally marry her was the Yankee writer known as
Orpheus 0. Kerr, and the last was J. 0. Heenan, the
Yankee pugilist. The prize fighter seemingly was a
failure, and Adah reverted to lovers of the brainy rathei
than the brawny order before she died of consumption.
A side show at the Manly chute ground is a man
rigged as a woman in evening dress—bare chest, ditto
arms, etc.—in a cask about four feet in circumference,
and sawn down till it reaches about to his waist. Thus
placed, he invitingly faces the crowd, whilst an assistant
sells 15in. sticks to all and sundry, inviting them to hit
him from a mark 20 feet off—if they can. They try—
try their hardest—but his agility is wonderful. His
nerve is steel, and though the missiles rain at him in a
stream, he, with graceful movements to the right or the
left, with bows, or with a calm stillness to the “ wides,” lets
them all pass. The sticks with which to hit him couldn’t
be served out fast enough to would-be slayers at four for
3d.
“ Alba” : Travellin’ mit a circus vas alride ven you haf
a good boss. I like de quick shift from town to town ;
de long bush roads, der camp on de flat. A circus vas a
big svag to carry. But id vas a wholesome life. A tiger
or lion vas good to look at und compare yourself mit.
Sometimes de cages fall to bieces midout warnin’; a
beast-rot eats dem up like white ants. A panther’s cage
go shaky once und de boss take out de brute und stick in
an old man hyena because he could not rattle it so much.
At Bendigo one nide de boss back the rotten cage against
de ring-house dot hold de monkeys. De boss came to
my tent in his nide shirt. “Sven,” he says, “ der vas
droble mit Smiler und de dogs.” Smiler vas de hyena’s
name. I put on my boots und go among the cages mit a
lantern. Der monkey-house vas like bedlam und a cat-
show. We find dot Smiler haf bit his way in among dem
und killed a lemur ape. Dese lemurs vas silly beasts mit
voices like a puma ;dey make no fight mit a stronger beast.
But de big baboon whose female have young ones get h s
arms round Smiler’s trote und start to keel him. De boss
get an iron bar while I held de hyena by de scruff mit my
naked hands. But de baboon would not let go ondil de
boss pinch de baby monkey on de back und make id cry.
Den de baboon fly to help id, leavin’ Smiler in my hands.
He keep quiet enough ondil I put him in his cage, den he
turn on his back like de shark und get my leg. Und he
howled like a child as he tore de piece avay. He vas a
horrible beast. I broke his jaw mit de heel of my boot.
All dot week de boss vas very cool mit me. Ven he pay
me on de Monday he says, “ Sven, I tink you vas a bit hast \
mit der hyena—you haf a bad temper.” “Herr Boss,” I
says, “ your hyena tore de sinews from my leg.” Says be,
“ Your dam sinews vas noding to me. I haf my family
to consider. De hyena cost me £l2. I must stop de
money from your wages efery week,” he says. Und by
Got he took de hyena from my wage because I could get
no Oder yob ! Next time he go into tiger’s cage—by
cripes, I will turn out all de lights !

“0.K.”: The duties of dramatic criticism are directly
opposed to the desires of theatrical managers. Criticism
should be just, and yet as indulgent as possible to the
young and to those who are only at the foot of the
theatrical ladder. Now, the desire of a manager is to
get heaps of newspaper praise for his battered old
“ stars,” whether they deserve it or not, but he wants no
indulgence or encouragement for the poorly paid mem-
bers of the co. If he had the editing of all notices (he
practically does edit many oi them) he would never per-
mit the publication of remarks likely to prompt a “ foot-
of-the-ladder ” artist to apply for a rise in salary. On the
other hand, the “ slating” of an unsatisfactory “ star” is
an act of high treason at any time, because they' whom
the management delights to honor (especially' at the
beginning of the engagement) ought to be commended
to the public at their managerial valuation. The public
would rather have the truth, consequently the first duty
of a dramatic critic is to tell the truth, and his next duty
is to state it in a readable way. And the manager’s duty
(to himself) is to rail against any statement of truth that
is not directly calculated to advertise his show without
inciting anybody' to approach him on the delicate subject
of salary.
Reply of English Sec. of State for the
Colonies to the Australasian protest against a
Chinese invasion of S’Africa is timid and hesitant.
It shelters the Imperial Government from respon-
sibility behind the transparent fiction —
His Majesty’s Government declares that its policy
is to treat the Transvaal as though it was a self-governing
colony, unless distinct Imperial interest is concerned,
and to interfere as little as possible with local opinion
and local wishes.

“ C.A.W.” : There were three of us in the
carriage, and we asked Ah Slim to make a fourth
for Nap. He didn’t know the game until we ex-
plained it. He made a few mistakes, but soon
picked up the idea. Soon, with a full “kitty,”
Bill yelled “Nap,” but the heathen exclaimed,
“ 0 cli, me only got four clards.” Bill’s language
was awful. The game continued until I called,
“I go the lot.” “ O cli, me got slix clards,” ex-
claimed Ah Slim. After I had invoked fervent
blessings on the dealer’s head, the game again
proceeded. Presently, Ah Slim inquired, “You
all got flive clards ? ” All admitting, lie calmly
said, “ then me go Nap.” And he got it.

About an accident that nearly happened :
There wa* a big holiday crowd on board, and a band
was playing on the sunlit promenade deck above. Up in
the bows, near the bar, lounged a crowd ot men round
whom several small boys played ‘tiggy," and made
nuisances of themselves generally. Several other smal
boys leaned more or less overside and watched the
water. A small solemn boy wearing sandshoes, sat on
the bulwarks with his back to the sea and chewed lan-
guidly at a banana. His weight was nicely balanced on
the rail, but, for security, he had placed the tip of one
shoe under the edge of the vacant seat below him. The
seat was comfortable and safe ; the bulwarks were un-
comfortable and not at all safe. The boy naturally pre-
ferred the bulwarks. An old man, with fierce ej ebrows,
sat with both knotted hands clasped over the knob of his
stick staring savagely at the boy. The toy glared sulkily
in return and munched at the banana. The situation
was strained The old man was fidgetty on account- o
the boy—not because he was fond of him, but because
he didn’t want to have his trip spoiled by a fatal acci-
dent. The boy—to judge from his sullen expression—was
not getting much enjoyment out of torturing the aged
stranger, but the knowledge that he was spoiling some
one else’s pleasure and asserting his independence was
quite enough to keep him swaying there precariously.
So they sat opposite and glowered at each other and
the boy dropped the skin of the first banana into the
foam below, and commenced to peel another A man
rolled up a paper bag and tossed it lightly over the side.
When it hit the water it was ten yards behind, and in
three seconds it had flashed along the side and been
swallowed up in the spray. The boy turned to look, and,
in his hurry, withdrew his toe from under the seat.
There was a yelp of rage, a white-faced kid clawing the
air, and a thud as he fell —inboard, on top of his half-
chewed banana. Two minutes later the old man was
leaning exhaustedly on the bar beside a stiff nobbier of
brandy, and the kid had resumed his seat and was de-
vouring what was left of his squashed vegetable

“ K.” : What is the matter with the people
responsible for the get-up of the well-known
Letts diaries? My copy starts off with a full
page of “ Lessons for Sundays ” for the year of
absolutely no use to me, who am a Presbyterian.
Even if I were an Anglican I wouldn’t want them
in a business diary. Then the daily headings are
thickly sprinkled with such remarks as “Lady
Day,” “Michaelmas Law Term Begins,” “Grouse
Shooting Opens.” There are half-a-dozen “Em-
ber Days ” —whatever they are —as well as an
“ Ember Week.” The champion reference, how-
ever, is “Jan. 25th, Conversion of St. Paul.” I
don’t care whether Paul was converted or not, but
I am annoyed both at the cocky manner in which
somebody has fixed the date and at his want of
taste in sticking the fact in a diary for general
use. The point in these criticisms is that the
blessed book is called an “Australasian Rough
Diary.” I intend sending Home a few sugges-
tions for the next issue, something like this:
“ Dec. 10th, Bulletin Xmas Number about due,;
“June Ist, Close Season for ’Possums Begins”;
“Nov. sth, Cup Day (Victoria)”; “Nov. Gth,
Day of Humiliation and Regret.” The few
things Australian in the book are mostly wrong.

The Murray is taking a pecnliar revenge
for the dry times, when the big lakes just inside
its mouth were made brackish by the sea working
in. At present it is turning out muddy, fresh
water in such quantities that the coastline outside
is affected, and the big crayfish in particular have
simply cleared out from the Port Victor'district.
General Hutton is said to be trying his
best to get a recall to London. His term in
Australia has no chance of extension, and he fears,
unless he gets into the Piccadilly swim pretty
sharp, he will be put on half-pay on his return-
Hutton, by the way, cultivated Governor Clarke
very effusively when the latter got that War
Office appointment.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“H.P.”: The yellow man is in complete pos-
session of the fruit and vegetable trade of Wellington
(M.L.). The business is said to be run by a small ring of
not more than half-a-dozen wealthy Ohows, who, as
occasion demands, import a few of the slant-eyed, and
allow them to work out their redemption on 3d. a day—
a redemption never, of course, achieved. With nothing
to pay for labor and next to nil for food, much time is
given to the shop windows, which are gorgeously kept.
* * * »
Said that the men who clean out the storm-water
sewers of Sydney make a systematic prospect of the silt
ere sending it up the man-holes—and the prospect often
pans out very well.

“ Chasselas” : Be “Hix”(B. 31/12/’O3), I still
state there is no maker of malleable iron castings either
in Ballarat or Melbourne, or in the Commonwealth, or
any furnace where they are turned out. The so-called
malleable castings “ Hix ” refers to are made from
crucible steel, a very different thing from the cheap iron.
I also state there is not one malleable cast-iron part on
any harvester manufactured in Victoria, and I get my
evidence first-hand, as I have given a good many their
preliminary canter in the wheat-fields of south-west Vic.
this season. The Yanks could knock out the local
makers in the matter of a light harvester if their patents
were not a protection. The 'Murkans have command of
the cheap malleable castings, while Australian makers
must use heavy crude castings, or expensive drop forgings,
or steel castings. If the Federal Parliament does not-put
on wstiff import-duty American competition will have to
be reckoned with.
“Scallywag”: Acting once as scrutineer of the
raffling at a country town bazaar, I had my eyes
opened a bit by the roguery practised. Commonest
form of it was that girls, receiving the price of
a share in the article to be raffled, would put
their own name down—the fleeced one seldom
taking the trouble to see his name written. Caught a
girl also with an imitation ballot ticket, with which s te
had won three articles. I then hired a nipper to draw,
but he was “squared” inside half an hour to hold a
forged ticket. Out of curiosity, I examined the lists,
and found that out of 14 articles in which I had taken
a share my name was down eight times. When bazaars
are on now, I send the promoters half a sov., and “ do ”
the rest at a pony meeting. There is better chance of a
run for your money there.
* * * *
“ Marat A man came to the door of my hash-
house a few weeks back, canvassing for orders for false
teeth on time-payment. He carried the wax for im-
pressions and a small heating apparatus, and immediately
anyone signed a paper wherein he agreed to pay Is. down
and 6d. a week, he got straight to business. I laughed
when he explained the system, and he grabbed my lower
jaw before 1 could pull it up again, and wanted to replace
my missing front tooth. He showed me 17 agreements
signed that morning. The old Jezebel who ran the hash-
house signed the agreement, and paid 2s. down. He
called on Monday for the first payment, and when I
answered the door, he said, “Mrs. Mac. in? Tell her the
fang collector, please.” He’s a humorous ouss.
* * * *
Perhaps there is no greater take-down Out-back
than the peregrinating engraver. He is rarely even a third-
class workman, and always a victim to a weakness, other-
wise he would never have reason to leave the cities, where
good men with the graver are scarcer even than honest
parsons. The wandeyer ekes out a living spoiling watch-
cases. Every country town has citizens who should have
known better whocan produce a watch upon whose cover
the itinerant has sought to etch, in flowery characters, the
owner’s name or initials. One can tell at a glance at what
hour of the day the “artist” has taken his job in hand
by the different degrees of badness apparent in the
work. Before dinner, the ambition of the graver-man
runs to flowery flourishes, and letters varying, in vain
effort, from finest Italian scroll in shadow to the bold
block letters of the city housetops. A few drinks further
on, and the ambition vanishes—plain letters, tapering in
size perceptibly, greet the eye. After dinner the ruina-
tion of the local watch-covers proceeds, till at sundown
the proceedings of the “ dotter ” are disgraceful. Finally,
at the close of the day, he simply inscribes a hiccup on
the case, or else sinks gently in a heap with the watch in
his hand, and doesn’t inscribe anything.
* * * *
“Marat”: A greater rogue than even the
registry-office shark is the average fiend who sells
small businesses to “.flats.” He generally owns a table,
four chairs, the talking capabilities of Jawbone Neild.and
a hide that would make a rhinoceros, after one inspection,
go away, a crushed animal, and hide himself gloomily
behind a shrub. One day’s canvass will put a hundred
small businesses in his hands for sale or commission, com.
being sometimes as much as one-half of sale price. Then he
advertises in the Herald for a mug “ to buy for £lO the
splendid business that will return £3 profit weekly.” The
old struggling couple who want a paying greengrocery
for their hard saved £4O, and the widow who wants to
invest the £25 she received from the lodge, call in to see
him and are picked as clean as a sheep in crow-time.
From the moment they arrive he never rests until they
I are left stranded in “ the magnificent business turning in
£3 a week,” whose previous owners had to be exhorted
daily by the agent to keep the door open another day, as
“a mug might turn up.” I know one business agent
whose wife opens up confectionery businesses with a few
pounds of stale confectionery and some empty jars. Her
husband then brings along a “mug,” and, the two being
brilliant talkers, a deposit is snatched out of his pocket
before he can get away from the shop. Last year the
husband sold .14 confectionery businesses for prices
ranging from £2O to £45, every one of which the wife had
opened at a cost of £5 a-piece at the outside.
A big Sydney retail firm ordered iis typists
to start the New Year with costumes of plain black and
white'. Also, they are strictly forbidden to wear jewel-
lery. They are now trembling lest the military boss
should order them into kharki.

Japanese commerce is flying to the neutral
ships. Will not the American flag be preferred to the
English ? Germany will cut in for a slice. She stands on
velvet. Certainly there will be no need for her to fight,
but she will put in an oar for some advantage out of the
combatants. Wilhelm and Billow will do all they know
to get England entangled. The American civil war ruined
the American commercial navy, which went over to the
British Flag.

DEATH!
The oold clasp of Death will inevitably enfold each and all of us at last, but Science has given many
of us the means of postponing the dread embrace until our tale of years is spent, and it _wou e
height of folly to refrain from availing ourselves of the aid which knowledge affords. Ueatn is
hastened by ignorance. Nature has endowed us with the sense of pain, solely in order that, y
ing relief, we may avoid playing into the hands of death. When we feel pain we are out oea•
Pain long endured is a strain upon the system which nature cannot withstand. Many of us, *
go on suffering certain kinds of pain, just as if Science could not come to the help of her sister l atur .
There is, for instance, no necessity for anyone to suffer from
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Backache
Sciatica
Indigestion
Anaemia
Blood Disorders
Biliousness
Jaundice
Gravel
Stone
Bladder Troubles
General Debility
Sick Headache
The pains caused by this long list of disorders are but Nature’s means of letting us know that the
kidneys or liver are for some reason unable to perform their work efficiently, for all of these dis-
orders are produced by the retention in the system of urinary and biliary poisons, which would be
thrown off naturally if the kidneys and liver were doing their duty. Few people realise how important
in the scheme of life is the healthy action of the kidneys and the liver, therefore the following
description of the functions of those vital organs will be of the deepest interest to many.
The Kidneys filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this
quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains m weight, of uric acid,
together with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an Ounce.
When the kidneys are in health, all this solid matter is m solution and is invisible. Directly the
kidneys, through either weakness or disease, become unfit to do their duty properly, a proportion of
the solid matter remains in the blood, becomes actively poisonous, and causes us to suffer from uric
disorders such as Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and Bright’s Disease. A simple test to make as to the condition of
the kidneys is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it
stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment, or if particles
float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy, and no time must be lost in
adopting remedial measures, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more painful
illness will result.
The Liver. —In the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or three
pounds of bile are thus made from the blood every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood,
converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood
gradually, as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into
urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived their
life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion,
Biliousness, Anaemia, Sick Headache and Blood Disorders.
The health of the liver and of the kidneys is so closely connected that it is almost impossible for
the kidneys to be affected and the liver to remain healthy, or vice versa.
It is nearly thirty years since scientific research, directed specially to diseases of the Kidneys and
Liver, was rewarded by the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warner’s Safe Cure.
It was realised, at the outset of the investigation, that it was necessary to find a curative agent which
would act equally upon the kidneys and upon the liver, these organs being so immediately associated
in the work of dealing with the body’s waste material, and after many disappointments, the medicine
which possessed the required action in the fullest degree was at length discovered. Warner’s Safe
Cure cures all diseases of tlie kidneys and livei*, and, by restoring'their activity, these vital
organs are enabled to rid the body, through the natural channels, of the urinary and biliary poisons,
the presence of which, in the system, are the cause of Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Back-
ache, Sciatica, Blood Disorders, Anaemia, Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Headache, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and General Debility. Warner’s Safe Cure cures all these disorders simply
by removing the cause of the disorder. This is the reason why cures effected by Warner’s Safe Cure
are permanent cures. ng.
The Man Who Knew.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It wa3 a “ private boarding establishmc nt ”
painfully “select.” It called itself “Highflyer
House,” and the landlady—an h-less but majestic
person, who always wore black silk and a sepul-
chral voice—had, by her own account, seen such
transcendently “better days” that it was practi-
cally an honor and a favor to be allowed to become
a “paying guest” at Highflyer House.
We were therefore somewhat surprised at the
admission to such a recondite circle of a man with
the utterly undistinguished cachet of our new
boarder. Middle-aged, smallish, wiry, hard-
featured and sandy-haired ; he had a dry curtness
»f manner that was only just not incivility. His
garments also were what might be described as
sub-shabby, and the surprise at his advent vastly
increased, when it became known that he was to
occupy the landlady’s Holy of Holies —the best
bedroom in the house, the tenancy of which
involved a substantial increase in the terms.
“ That, of course, means coin,” the Superiah
Person admitted ; “but still, y’ know, the fellow
won’t do at all. He’s uttahly impossible—b’Jawve,
yas ! ”
The Literate agreed. “Why, the man was
positively so ignorant that he had written his
surname in the hall-book without a capital
initial! ”
This was in the smoking-room ; and, as I passed
through the hall afterwards, I had the curiosity
to look at the book. “John W. ffolliott,” I read.
Now, here was no illiteracy. The West-of-
Ireland ffolliotts have for centuries spelt their name
with a small initial; that which was doubtless
originally a blunder having become a cherished
family custom. And, if John W. ffolliott—l told
myself—was really one of the stnall-f people, and
possessed his fair share of the family brains and
spirit, he was certainly not the man to be sat
upon by the four sickening snobs and pretenders
who pretty well monopolised the table-talk at
Highflyer House. Of the other and less preten-
tious boarders, I was intimate with only one—
Jedders by name—a quiet, grey man, who, like
myself, used to do a lot of inward chuckling over
the conversational caracolings and prancings of
the Superiah Person, the Spiky Spinster, the
Literate, and the Warrior Bold.
At dinner, for the first few evenings after the
new guest’s arrival, the Fearsome Four, as
Jedders called them, made a special spread of
themselves, obviously for the edification, and im-
pressment, and becrushment generally, of the dry
little man who, with an otherwise impassive coun-
tenance, kept cocking his sharp grey eye at one
and the other, as they in turn banged and
whanged and trumpeted to their own honor and
glory.
One evening, however, he began to put in a
word. First, the Spiky Spinster had got the
drop on the company, and had discoursed
divergently about the sayings and doings of her
titled friends “ at Home ” ; until, when she paused
Co recall some distinctly precious utterance of
“ the dear Duchess of Ditchwater,” the Superiah
Person seized the opportunity to dash in with
his little recollection of the Marquis of Mudflat’s
duel at Ostend with Sir Gaudy Gaydog ; and so
got to an alleged “affair” of his own on the
sands of Boulogne, “ with one of the best saw’ds-
men in Frawnce, b’Jawve! —in which I had the
misfortune to run my man—but bettaw not go
into—haw—details. Might be awkward for a
fellow, even at this distance of time —b’Jawve,
yas !
“ Good with the sword, then ?” put in the little
man, speaking for the first time.
The Superiah Person surveyed him for a
moment, much as one might look at a cockroach.
“In my p’sition in life,” said the S.P. then,
“ we are, as a mattaw of cawse, instructed in all
—haw—manly accomplishments; and, with my
speshawl physical advantages, it was easy faw me
to—haw—excel in them all. B’Jawve, yas ! ”
“Punch a bit, too, then, I suppose?” said the
little man.
The S.P.—he was a strongly-built six-footer —
depreciatingly waved a large white paw. “Pro-
ficiency with the saw’d,” he said, loftily, “may be
discussed in any company. It is the accomplish-
ment of a—haw—gentleman. But the othaw
branch of the awt of self-defence, to which you so
caw’sely refer, is hawdly a subject faw the society
of—haw—ladies. Still, as a gentleman may at
any time be called upon to chawstise the insolence
of a—haw—vulgah fellow, it is desirable to be
able to do so. Consequently, I see no hawrn in
acknowledging that I am—to use the beastly
local jawgon—‘no mug at that game—b’Jawve,
no ! ”
Here the Warrior saw his chance, and struck
in—“Whin I was a lift’nant in the Sixth, we had
a corporal that was a raal daisy with his fishts.
An’ wan day, whin— ”
“’Souse me, sir,” put in the little man, “do
you refer to the Sixth Foot—now the Royal
Warwickshire Regiment ?”
■ “ The Sixth Fat / ” returned the warrior, with
-huge scorn.. “Not a bit of it !—no beggarly
marchin’ ridgmint for Dermot de Lacy O’Toole.
The Sixth Dragoon Gyards, sir, I referred to—
the feamous Inniskillings.”
The little man pursed-up his lips like one who
has hit upon something he had expected to find,
and the warrior went prosing on about a regi-
mental scrimmage, until the Literate saw an
opening and hooked-on with—
‘ ‘ Reminds me of a situation in one of the
tragedies of ASschylus ”
"’Souse me, sir,” cut in the little man again—
" fond of the classic drama ? ”
The Literate’s face said plainly, "Now, what
can this fellow know about the classic drama ? ”
but with his tongue he said only, “Without it,
life to me would be indeed a blank.” Then,
looking loftily away from the little man, he was
proceeding: “In the ‘lphigenia in Tauris,’”
when the Spiky Spinster burst in: “Oh, that
sweet, sweet name, Iphigenia ! It ever calls up
before me the graceful form and lovely features of
my one-time dearest friend, Lady Iphigenia de
Crespigny. I remember once, when I was stay-
ing at the Bradbury-Grahams’ ”
“ ’Sense me, madam,” from the little man ; “ do
they stick a hyphen between the names, these
Bradbury-Grahams ? ”
The Spiky Spinster condescended to look for
the first time in the little man’s direction. This
common person evidently took some interest in
things upper-class and elegant. “Yes,” she
answered with condescension ; “there is a hyphen
between the words.”
“Then they’re snobs!” said the little man,
roundly.
“Sir?”
“ What I mean is that, as there isn’t really any
such hyphenated name, these people must have
assumed # ...
“Sir,” said the spinster with a pitying 9mile,
' 4 you do but expose your lamentable ignorance.
Perhaps you’ll say there is no such name as my
own.”
“And that is?”
“My name, sir, is Robinson-Briggs”—and
don’t you forget it, the lady’s manner seemed to
convey.
“There isn't any such name,” the little man
said, as bluntly as before. “Robinson there is,
and Briggs there is; but there’s no Robinson-
Briggs amongst the real hyphenated names. I
know them all, you see.”
“ What utter aonsense is this —not to say
insolence ! ” The Spiky Spinster’s nose pointed
scornfully upwards.
“Neither nonsense nor insolence, madam,”
returned the little man, “just plain matter-of-
fact. What I have called the real hyphenated
names are combinations originally territorial, due
to a stipulation in connection with the transfer of
landed property through marriage or otherwise.
The whole thing, of course, is mere frill and
flummery ; but such people as you have an ambi-
tion for the shoddy distinction ; so when you
happen to have a second surname you stick a
hyphen between that and the other, and thus
render yourselves ridiculous in the eyes of the
very people you are striving to ape.”
Here, before the Spinster could launch the bolts
of her indignation, the Literate cut in. “Speak-
ing of compound names; when I was at Cambridge
we had a young fellow called Temple-Nugent-
Brydges-Chandos-Grenville. Perhaps,” turning
to the little man, “you’ll say there’s no such
name as that ? ”
“I won’t say so, because I know that’s the
name of the Buckingham people—and a bright
lot they are ! But I’ll tell you what Iwill say. I
don’t believe you were ever at Cambridge, or any
other university. Why, man, I could scarcely
keep my countenance just now when you not only
attributed to vEschylus the ‘lphigenia’ of Euri-
pides, but miscalled the heroine ‘ Iphi/eenia ’ —a
blunder in which your lady-friend there promptly
imitated you, further, also pronouncing Cres-
pigny in a way that showed she didn’t even know
that the people with whom she says she was so
intimate pronounce their name ‘Crepny.’ What
howling humbugs the pair of you are!—and
boarding-houses swarm with your like.”
The little man pushed back his chair and got
up; but, before he could leave the room, the
Warrior also arose.
“Look here, me frind,” he said, “as an officer
and a gintleman, I feel that it devolves upon me
to demand from ye, before ye lave this room, an
ample apology for the insult ye’ve put upon this
lady. If ye refuse I will foind meself undher the
peenful necessity of inflictin’”
Here the little man broke in with a laugh of
hearty amusement. “Oh, by George, it’s rich I
It oniy wanted this ! The ‘officer and gintleman’
feels bound to make some attempt to play-up to
the character. Why, man, you’re as big a fraud
yourself as any of ’em. A pretty cavalryman,
truly—to confound the 6th Inniskilling Dragoons
with the 6th Dragoon Guards. If you’d ever
been even as much as a mess-waiter you’d have
known that you count seven regiments of
Dragoon Guards before you begin afresh with the
Ist Dragoons.”
Here the warrior, with fists fiercely clenched,
made a melodramatic stride forward; but the
little man, instead of retreating, made a stride
forward himself, and the warrior, despite a very
audible snigger from the Spinster, drew hastily
and ignominiously back.
At this point, Jedders, trying for a diversion,
put in: “Possibly, Mr.—Mr. ffolliott, you may
have done this gentleman—indicating the
Literate —an injustice. It is surely quite pos-
sible for him to have been a university-man and
yet to mix up the Greek dramatic authors, or
even to mispronounce classic names. , I have
known graduates to make much worse blunders.”
“If I have misjudged the gentleman I’ll be
quite ready to offer him ample apologies. And,
to settle the question,”—the little man turned to
the Literate—“ will you kindly tell me which of
the Cambridge colleges you were at ? ”
The Literate knew he had to answer promptly.
“ Magdalen,” he said, with a gulp.
The little man smiled a grim smile. “ Magdalen
happens to be an Oxford college,” he said. “And,
in any case, if you’d ever been near the place,
you’d have known that the word is pronounced
‘Maudlin.’ I think we may consider your case
closed, too.” . ... . ,
He walked to the door, and had his hand on it
when he was tapped on the arm by the Superiah
Person, who thought, I suppose, that, under the
eyes of the Spiky Spinster and the landlady, it
behoved him to make an attempt to assert him-
self.
“ Haw !—a word with you, my fine fellow,” he
said. “ You cawnt go on like this, y’ know, with-
out giving satisfaction.' B’Jawve, no ! ”
The little man grinned. “No,” he said —pur-
posely misunderstanding ; “I don’t suppose I’m
giving satisfaction. People seldom do get much
satisfaction out of being shown up as absurd pre-
*‘ D you daah to insinuate that lam haw a
pretendah ? ” ,
“ I don’t insinuate it—l say straight-out that
I wasn’t long in sizing you up as being just as big
a fraud as the others. ’
“Enough, sir! You must give me a meeting.
Can you do anything with the—haw—saw’d ?”
Again the little man laughed with genuine
enjoyment. “Oh, Lord!” he said; “this is
quite too funny for anything. This man will be the
death of me—but not with the ‘ saw’d.’ Go away,
you ridiculous large person, and- don t talk
drivelling tommyrot. Or, here—stay a moment. ”
The little man suddenly slipped out into the
hall, and quickly returned with two walking canes.
“ Here, my bold niaster-of-fence,” he said;
“ take-this stick, and engage me in quarte—this
way. Don’t be alarmed, madam” (to the expostu-
lating landlady)— ‘‘ there’ll be no thrusting. Now,
sir; here we are in quarte ” —the big man was
half-stupidly holding the cane in position—“ and
I disengage—thus—to hit you in sixte. The
counter-parry of quarte is your best move—make
it, sir. Go on, man—make it! Not you—you
don’t know how ! Oh, Lord !—here’s the man
that spitted ‘one of the best saw’dsmen in
Frawnce, b’Jawve,’ and he positively doesn’t
know septime from seconde ! ”
The little man walked out, and was replacing
his cane in the hall-rack, when the Superiah
Person, who had been standing like a monument
to the Supremely Ridiculous, suddenly rushed at
him and landed him a blundering blow on the side
of the head that knocked him to the foot of the
stairs.
Jeddera, myself, and a couple of others hastened
out to preserve the small man from annihilation;
but we weren’t needed, for he was up again in an
instant, and, darting swiftly in, planted three
lightning-like blows on the “mark before the
big man had time to cover that susceptible locality.
Very hard punches they must have been, too, for
the big chap doubled-up like a wet rag, and fell,
with a grunt, upon his hands and knees.
“Get up, old man,” jeered the small battering-
ram. “Get up, and go on ‘chawstising the inso-
lence of the—haw—vulgah fellow.’ Very sorry to
have had to do it, ma’am—to the protesting land-
lady—but he’d make four of me, you see ; and I
don’t take any chances with bullocks. And, any-
how, I don’t think he wants any more.”
He didn’t; and it was a very Inferiah Superiah
Person that presently slunk up the stairs to his
room.
“Always hit a big-un in the dinner, if you can,’ -
the small man said to us, with a wink, as he began
to fill his pipe. “Even if you don’t drop him, it’ll
probably get you a good second opening.”
The Superiah Person left next morning, and the
Spiky Spinster a few days later. The Literate
and the Warrior, having thicker hides, stayed on ;
but never a word concerning Humane Letters or
the Art-Military did either of them afterwards let
fall in the hearing of the Man who Knew.

The Colinton Estate, Queensland.
40,000 ACRES OF DAIRY FARMS.
The Colinton Estate is situated on the ever-running
fresh waters of the Upper Brisbane River, about 5J hours’
train from the city, and is the property of W. J. H. •
Moore, M.L.A., and not of any syndicate, who buy whole-
sale to sweat the agriculturist and make a profit by
retailing.
Colinton is essentially a dairying property, and con-
sists of a succession of sloping ridges, with a large propor-
tion of lucerne flats, with frontages to the Upper Brisbane
River, and to numerous creeks which intersect the pro-
perty. It is sheltered on the west by a high range of
mountains, which form the main watei shed of the Bris-
bane River, and its fattening properties are borne out by
the certificate of the local Tick Inspector, whose returns
show an average of 5494 fat bullocks per annum, turned
off during the past five years, and during this time it was
only partly stocked.
The rainfall works out at nearly 40 inches per annum,
and the property during 1892 proved itself practically
drought-proof.
There is upwards of 18 miles double frontage to the
Upper Brisbane River, and, in addition to eight large
dams, there are 55£ miles of permanent running creeks,
so that every farm has permanent water on it. The
station is free from ticks, and has been kept free from
pests ; and there is no nut-grass on any of the farms.
The auction sale will take place at the head station on
Easter Monday and the following days of Easter week,
and the terms are as follows :—One-tenth cash, then no
payment for two years, and the balance in annual instal-
ments for twenty years, with interest at 4J per cent.
There will also be a “ non-forfeiture ” clause in the sale
contract to the effect that, if through drought or loss of
crops, a purchaser is unable to meet his instalments, the
vendor will not enforce the forfeiture clauses of the agree-
ment under certain reasonable circumstances.
A very large number of newspapers in Southern Queens-
land, and all through New' South Wales and Victoria, as
well as in New Zealand and South Australia, are publish-
ing the advertisements in connection with the Estate,
and a large, illustrated pamphlet has been prepared, of
which 150,000 have been printed and circulated as supple-
ments by these newspapers. These supplements, which
are obtainable at this office, contain copious information
about the Estate, as well as twenty large, half-tone illus-
trations. The greatest facilities will be afforded to per-
sons going to inspect, and the date of the sale is well
ehosen, as people attending will be enabled to take
advantage of the Easter railway excursions.*

Shareholders in the Lloyd copper mine (N.S.W.)
are petting impatient, and little wonder. Capital and
borrowed money to the amount of £lOO,OOO has been put
into the mine, and since the co. was formed it has pro-
duced nearly £300,000 worth of copper, yet there has
been no profit and no tangible asset, but, on the contrary,
a debenture liability of £50,000, which will soon mature.
At a recent meeting in London a proposal was made to ap-
point a committee of investigation; this was carried on the
voices by 13 to 3, but negatived by ballot by an equally
big majority. In May last the directors issued a circular
which stated that £17,000 profit had been made in four
months, yet the balance sheet at the end of June showed
a loss of over £ll,OOO. There was an actual profit of
£6OOO on working account (the mine idle for five
months owing to the drought), but interest on
debentures and £12,000 depreciation made up the loss.
Directors were challenged as to the discrepancy between
May circular and June balance-sheet, and excused them-
selves on the miserable pretext that the former was based
on manager’s estimates. It is all moonshine for directors
to attribute the unsatisfactory position to a decline in
value of ore from 6 and 8 per cent, to 3 and 4 per cent.
It is only lately that work has been carried on in the
poorer quality, and the marvel is that the supply of good
ore held out so long. Burraga, like every other mine,
has lenses of good and inferior ore. In the late Mr.
Lloyd’s time it was supposed to be worked out scores
of times, but it is not exhausted yet, nor will it
be in the lifetime of anyone now associated with
it. Operations are now being conducted in the
right way for success. Past failure is to be accounted
for by the grossest bungling —the absurd practice
of changing managers every year, which has meant
constant doing and undoing; and not a small factor
has been the directors’ penchant for seeking, at great
expense, the advice of purely theoretical experts. At
last the co. has started to explore the main lode, running
north and south, and the last report stated that at No. 1
level a body of ore 10ft. wide, worth 6 per cent., had been
found. The old Cousin-Jacks who worked in the mine in
the early days are unanimous in the opinion that the east
and west formation was merely an off-shoot of the north
and south lode. The latter was abandoned many years
ago, because the east and west bunch was richer and
more easily worked ; it is unquestionably the backbone
of the mine, and from the working of it shareholders will
yet get a return on their capital—that is, if they don’t
allow themselves to be squeezed out by debenture-holders.
* • * *
Crowl Creek, Slmttleton (N.S.W.), is one of the
good shows about which very little is heard ; but it will
soon assert itself in a way that will surprise both the
Sydney croakers who assume that it is a mine which
exists only in boomsters’ minds and the Melbourne
speculators who have fought shy of it for personal
reasons. Four reverb, furnaces are at work, producing
weekly on an average 15 tons of copper that realises “ best
selected ” prices. There is enough ore at grass and in
sight to justify the immediate duplication of the smelt-
ing plant. It has been practically decided that this is to
be done, but the style of plant will not be determined
until J. O. Armstrong (who recently took charge) has had
time to prepare estimates to show whether it will be the
more advantageous to erect four new reverbs., or to
instal a blast furnace plant, using the existing
reverbs, to treat matte. At the 330 ft. level com-
pact sulphide ore assaying 22J per cent, copper
has been struck. The cross cut intersected the lode
at the southern end of the chute where it is from
12ft. to 13ft. wide, sft. of this on the hanging-wall being
free from silica, assaying as stated above, and the
remaining width a mixture of ore and slate averaging 8 to
10 per cent. As the lode is driven on north, it is expected
that the solid sulphide will increase in width cor-
responding with the ore-bodies in the higher levels.
A winze from the 270 ft. level was sunk in the dredgy foot-
wall material, the clean, hard sulphide being regarded at
the time as a hanging-wall of “country." It was only
recently that this mistake was revealed. It all depends
upon Mr. Armstrong’s recommendations whether or not
the co, will appear in the div. list within the next few
weeks. Profits are said to be accumulating rapidly, but
the manager may propose extensive and costly per-
manent works, to which funds in hand may be applied.
A div. from a N.S.W. company-owned copper-mine will
be a novelty.
4c # * *
The Shuttleton Mines Co., adjoining Crowl
Greek, has donealarge amount of encouraging prospecting.
The trial shaft on the south lease has been sunk to 280 feet
on the footwall. For the last 60 feet the shaft has been in
payable ore ; it is sulphide, getting more compact every
foot sunk. A section of the bottom of the shaft ought
to show about 12 per cent, copper ; samples of the clean
mineral assay 20 per cent. The width of the body is
not known, and no attempt will be made to determine
this until a further 50 feet has been reached. These
operations are being carried on over half a mile south of
the Growl Greek workings, in the same lode channel, so
it is evident that Shuttleton is to be a mining camp of
some importance. The southern blocks are held by a
Sydney syndicate on a working option which has been
extended to February next year.

A London journalist writes: “ I hear a whisper
that Oobar Gold Mines may not impossibly have a look-in
(for a rise) before long." So far, shares have had no
market value, so there is not much required to constitute
a rise. November return was £8672, showing a net profit
of £3OOO, and October output was £9471.
* * • •
The Lake George mines case is likely to come
on for hearing in London this month. It will be a mining
cause celebre. The amounts claimed from the promoters
and vendors of the co. total over £250,000, the principal
defendants being Anthony Gibbs and Sons and Gibbs,
Bright and Co. The case will determine the legal
liability or non-liability of promoters who do not publish
a formal prospectus, but who, as is alleged to have been
done in this case, adopt the process of making a market
on the Stock Exchange.
4 * * ♦
The Broken Hill Prop, has taken up the land
necessary for the laying of a direct line to connect the
mine and the S. Australian railway system, so as to
avoid having to use the Silverton Tramway Co.'s service.
The general manager says that the co. means business,
and that the matter has gone beyond the conference
sfage. But the Broken Hill co.’s have taken up land
again and again to carry out water conservation and
other works, which they never have carried out; so that,
in spite of all protestations, this probably means just
Bluff —a line of business in which the B.H. Prop, is
unexcelled. Still, as the proposal is quite feasible,
the Tramway co. will almost certainly have to cut its
rates. And they can afford a good deal of cutting. What-
ever happens, the Tramway Go. will suffer.
* • • »
North Duke Co. (Maryborough, Vic.), which
has been in existence since 1890, has just closed its
unprofitable career. It has been a great gambling stock
from time to time, and in this respect has been a source
of great gain to the market wire-pullers. Mine produced
£239,038 worth of gold, absorbed £55,208 of capital, and
paid only £33,750 in divs. Wages amounted to £123,580,
and fuel and timber cost £56,000. A shocking item in
the balance-sheet is £11,551 royalty. W hat a gross wrong
it is that enterprising mining speculators should have to
pay greedy land-owners £11,551 for the privilege of losing
their money in mining, under the surface of not more
than 20 acres of land, most of which probably would not
be damaged in the least! It is generally supposed that
Victoria has a liberal Mining on Private Property Act,
but the only liberality in a law which permits of this
blood-sucking is in favor of the man who least deserves it.
* * * *
The old sludge difficulty in Victoria has now
got as far south as Melbourne. The Met. Board of Works
and the Harbor Trust are Yarra Purity Societies in senti-
ment ; but the Board has no established jurisdiction
over any part of the river, and the Trust cannot prevent
any fouling work that happens further inland than
Queen’s Bridge. Nobody who knew the dirty Yarra a
year or two ago can be blind to the fact that it is now
browner than ever ; and, except for an occasional news-
paper protest, the flow of liquid mud passes unchallenged.
Sooner or later Victoria will probably pay more gold to
get the mud out of the stream than the up-stream miners
ever won.

Two belated W.A. divs. for last year, which
were not cabled out, have been announced by mail—the
Ida H., 2s. per share, or £5400, payable Dec. 23; and
Peak Hill, Is. per share, or £15,000, payable Dee. 31.
These increase the total for the year to £2,026,655,
against a gross gold yield of £8,770,710. This shows that
23-per cent, of the gold won was paid in divs., chiefly to
John Bull. To do this, 20,476 men and £3,500,000 of
machinery were employed.

* * * *
Hannan’s Prop., Kalgoorlie, reports having cut
the northern continuation of the Eclipse lode in its
Orotava lease. This area is situated two blocks north of the
Eclipse and adjoining the Kalgoorlie Amalgamated. Where
cut the lode is very wide, but is not payable. Driving
and crosscutting at the 130 ft. level are now proceeding
with the view of locating any pay chutes that may exist.
As Hannan’s Prop, holds a very large area in the vicinity,
the finding of pay ore would mean a lot to it. The same
co. is sinking a shaft on its Queen of the West lease to
try and get the Underlay of the Eclipse lease at depth.
As shareholders have been badly dealt with so far, it is
to be hoped that present prospecting will lead to payable
results.
* * • *
Ivanhoe (Kalgoorlie) will shortly be in a posi-.
tion to push on work much faster. During last year
£44,660 was expended in additions to surface equipment,
mainly with the view of reducing costs. Owing to the
heavy inflow of water the sump was stopped too near the
1010 ft. level, and since the erection of the new 30-drill
compressor to provide the air required, the sinking of
the shaft has had to proceed very cautiously. In another
six weeks the danger from shdts will be past, and the
shaft is then expected to go down 60ft. monthly. In
future, levels will be 200 ft. apart, so that each level will
open Up very large reserves of ore. Many labor-saving
devices have been installed, and during the current year
costs should be materially reduced, and be as low as those
of any mine oh the fields. This is one of the mines on
which everything is done free and above-board, and all
visitors and reporters are welcomed at any reasonable
time.
• * * *
Hannan’s Reward, Kalgoorlie, has already vin-
dicated late manager Oorbould’s policy of letting the
mine on tribute. Corbould was over-ruled by the
attorney, but Sinclair, who succeeded Corbould, success-
fully fought the attorney, and offered the mine to tribu-
tors. Some 100 men immediately got to work, with the
result that the first month’s returns run to 418 oz. from
189 tons. The co.’s share is about £2OO, which is a deal
better than losing a similar amount monthly, as was done
formerly. Naturally, however, some of the tributors are
not satisfied, as the ground is very hard and the leaders
very narrow.
» • * *
Lake Yiew Consols, Kalgoorlie, since taken in
hand by Bewick, Moreing and Co., has been very
steadily and systematically developed right down to
1600 ft. The ore reserves now in sight are estimated at
about 170,000 tons, worth about lOdwt. The mine will
shortly be equipped with a 75-head up-to-date mill,
capable of treating at least 8000 tons monthly, at a very
much cheaper cost than rules at present. The old mill
was quite out of date when bought, and was badly
erected and badly looked after. The co. is still pushing
on with developments, and, should it be lucky enough to
strike another bonanza, shareholders may yet have a good
run for their money.
* • • •
Ivanhoe Junction, Kalgoorlie, is still getting
occasional rich patches, but the management seems to
leave much to be desired. In order to get “ quick and
lively” to the pay ore, which Dwyer had developed on his
lease, the Junction made a bee-line for the spot—and left
the lode to the east. This drive has consequently opened
up nothing, and has only proved a good sink for the
shareholders’ money. Possibly the manager was not to
blame, as he knew a call was pending and was little
likely to be met unless he eould dangle some rich speci-
mens before the eyes of shareholders.
• •
Menzies United (W.A.), a wild cat of the early
days, is still being kept alive, Heaven and Bewick,
Moreing and Co. alone know why. After its Menzies
property had been flattened out, the co. was advised to
speculate £lO,OOO in Cosmopolitan shares, which deal
ended in a pro'it of £14,000. This profit, on B.M. and
Co.’s recommendation, was sunk in Golden Age Consols,
but the spec, has not turned up trumps. The co. has
now got into the Lancefield, in the expectation that the
lode would .be worth lldwt. for 21ft. wide. The first
return from the Lancefield since B.M. and Co. took it over
went 6'4d\vt. This average would not pay costs, and
would certainly leave nothing with which to pay divs.
• » * * 1
After dropping many thousands of pounds
Zebina Lane has given briquette-making in Westralia
best. The Collie coal has been found unsuitable—too
crude and friable, and even when briquettes of fair
quality were occasionally turned out, mining managers
looked askance at them. . “ It seems to me that what I
made on gold I will drop in coal if I go on with this much
longer,” sighed Zeb., so, greatly to the chagrin of Bun-
bury, where the plant was erected, the works have been
closed down. The coal is right enough for household
use, and under certain conditions it will generate steam,
but it has several defects, and engine-drivers all over the
country know them. It is only when Newcastle coal is
unobtainable that the Collie article has a possible show.
* * * * .
Miller’s Karri and Jarrah Combine (W.A.) is
making big gaps in the hardwood forests of W.A. On an
average there are a dozen sailing vessels constantly
loading sawn jarrah—mostly for railway sleepers and
wood pavements in all parts of the world. British India
and Cape steamers are also calling at frequent intervals
for loads of the same timber, and if the present denudation
of the forest areas continues for a few years a valuable
asset will disappear. Beyond the wages of hewers
and mill hands and the payment of a peppercorn rental
for the land, the State derives no benefit from the
operations of the Combine, as it is questionable if the
Railway Department makes a shilling a year out of
carrying the timber from the mills to the port of ship-
ment. Among other valuable concessions blindly given
to the timber companies in the early days was the grant
of the harbor at Rockingham, about 15 miles from Fre-
mantle. It is seriously competing with the latter port,
as vessels loading at Rockingham escape wharfage and
other dues. It is more than likely that the new Assembly
will consider the expediency of imposing an export duty
on timber as well as gold—the State is being drained of
its natural wea th for the benefit mostly of foreigners.

If (as is not unlikely) there should
be a combine of the leading shipping com-
panies to force black seamen down Australia s
throat by refusing to tender for the mail contract,
why shouldn’t Australia buy a fleet of its own, or
hire one ? In the first place, the mail subsidy of
£75,000 would be placed to the scheme’s credit.
Cargo, such as frozen goods, could be carried for
Government departments. Such ships could, in
the event of war, be converted into armed
cruisers; and when the fleet for which we pay
£200,000 per annum is up North helping some
yellow ally, we would feel a little more secure
with an auxiliary fleet of our own.
The Victorian Kyabram Government’s
“economy” still runs its course over the blood
and bones of the poor. T’other day, a poor old
woman, whose only crime was poverty, and of
whom Sergeant Eason, of Melb. City Court, said:
“She is almost done; make it 12 weeks instead
of three months, so she won’t be jolted out to
Pentridge,” was put away in the big city prison to
die of old age. This sort of iniquity was left to the
honorary beak once ; now it has become so common
that even P.M. Pan ton has ceased to protest
against it. As long ago as April, ’9B, George
Turner issued regulations to the police ordering
that such persons were to be maintained outside a
gaol—either in a charitable institution or else-
where—at a cost of not more than 10s. a week,
which the Treasurer would pay. Now, the old
woman’s maintenance in gaol will cost 15s. lOd.
a week. The Irvine policy is not only cruel
but wasteful.

SUFFERING MEN
You daily stand before the
Mirror of Life. Yearly you see
yourselves grow older, stronger, or weaker. You
know whether you are degenerating or other-
wise. If at twenty you are feeble in health and
puny in stature, you can guess what you will be
like at fifty. Look at the faces in these Mirrors!
WHAT WILL YOU BE LIKE AT FIFTY?
This is the Question. Do you mean to get the
best of the world, or will
you let the world get the best of you ? Sooner or later you must understand
yourselves, and decide whether you will climb up hill to success, or slide down
hill to failure. No one can stand still. Now is the time for you to choose your
future. Do not dodge the question, procrastinate, or allow yourself to aimlessly
drift towards premature decay and Death. DECIDE TO LI VE—to rise to the
top of your chosen profession.

AFTER HANY YEARS.
Mr. JAMES LANE, farmer, of Rose Hill, Orange, was
a martyr for over THIRTY YEARS to Sciatica. No
doctor could cure him. They told him he was “IN-
CURABLE.” His wife says of his recovery:—
“Mr. Lane was in bed during last winter, could
only walk with the aid of a stick. After adopting
your treatment he could ride round his farm for
his cows. I think everyone should know ot his
cure, for he had tried many doctors, and had not
obtained relief. He is now well and strong.”
CHRONIC DYSPEPSIA CURED.
Mr. CHARLES BROWN, Newcomen-street, New-
castle, who suffered from Indigestion and Dyspepsia for
many years, and who scarcely knew what it was to eat
a hearty meal, writes
“ I cannot praise your treatment too much. At
one time I could not eat a meal with good
appetite. Your systems of treating nerve
diseases has done me good, for I now eat and
sleep well. That is more than I have done for
many yea,rs. I am quite healthy to-day."
