Under the Irvine brand of economy Vio-
tona seems to be quickly relapsing into absolute
barbarism. Age report of the Melbourne watch-
house :
The cells are small, dark unventilated pens, with air
space hardly more than sufficient for one person ; when
three or four are crowded in, as frequently happens, the
prisoners are in danger of asphyxiation.' When “ old
hands ” are locked up they at once extend themselves
upon the floor, so that they may inhale the less tainted
atmosphere that is drawn in under the doors. The cells
are not connected with the sewerage system, and the
conveniences within them are of the most primitive
description. It is a common thing on a hot night to
hear prisoners begging imploringly to be allowed out in
the corridor for five minutes for abreath of Z That an
epidemic of disease has not broken out is remarkable It
is <l uite likel y> however, that disease acquired -there
develops elsewhere. The watch-house and court-houses
adjoining are built of wood throughout. The walls har-
bor vermin. They are among the oldest ol Melbourne’s
public buildings, and through the “ process of the suns”
have been converte i into so much touchwood, and are
just waiting for th< flame that may any day come into
accidental contact with them. What might happen in
such circumstances when the cells are crowded is too
dreadful to contemplate. . . . Medical men have fre-
quently said that to expose a prisoner suffering from
cuts and lacerations to the tainted atmosphere of such
kennels as the cells of the City Watch House is to seri-
ously imperil his life.
An extraordinary book, which describes
itself as “Smith of the Shamrock Guards’
with the sub-title “ The ‘ Ragged ’ Lieuten-
ant ” —a “drama in a prologue and four
acts”—is to hand by a recent mail. It is by
“ Officer,” and is dedicated to those officers
“ who like myself abhor the disgraceful system of
ragging.” Author prints correspondence with the
Lord Chamberlain, who four times refused to
sanction representation of the play. The L.C.
might have saved three lots of stationery, for nary
a manager would have accepted the awful thing.
Smith, the “ ragged ” officer, is second lieutenant
of the Shamrock Guards, and the son of a merchant.
You are to understand that, as far as behavior,
education and courage are concerned, he is
the equal or superior of his fellow officers.
Colonel Lord Binkerton and Adjutant Viscount
LTmfurly of the S. Guards are most anxious- to
shift Smith out of their company. “The beggar
is too keen,” they say, and is “as poor as the
devil” ; so they incite the junior officers to “rag”
him. He is “ragged” every night but doesn'6
shift. One of the “raggings” makes scene two
of the play. Some six young officers force their
way into Smith’s sitting-room, hold a mock
court-martial; then each gives him five blows
with the fire-shovel. They force down his
throat a glass of vinegar, mustard and
pepper, then chase him round and round
the room with hot pokers. All the officers
had brought their own pokers and the irons
were heating whilst the smacking was going
on. These outrages are done to a running fire
of the feeblest witticisms, and a constant
ringing for brandies and sodas—at their victim’s
expense. Does Smith break at least one jaw or
one shin ? No, he goes meekly off to bed after
they let him up; and after the yahoos have
wrecked his sitting-room they go into his bedroom
and turn his bed over on him.
At Charlotte Plains station, near Cunna-
mulla, Q., this season’s lambing gave 16,422
lambs from 15,983 merino ewes, an average of
102’7 per cent.
An extraordinary thing has just happened.
A lady who was compelled to seek a divorce from
her giddy spouse, and was said by the romantic to
have broken her heart by the act, died the other
day and left her cash and personal estate to the
offender, who had been hitched up to the co-
respondent as soon as the decree nisi was recorded.
Perhaps she intended it as a sarcastic sort of
solatium, to alleviate an unhappiness which she
presumed.

“W.H.1.” writes promiscuously :
Some years ago I caught a huge variety of South
American locust measuring about Bin. long, with magni-
ficent red-striped underwings. Wishing to preserve the
specimen, after careful chloroforming I opened the body,
removed the fleshy contents, dusted the cavity with
corrosive sublimate, stuffed with antiseptic cotton wool,
impaled specimen, and properly mounted it. When all was
complete, the beast turned its head and gibbered at me.
Asalad, always too thoughtless to collect reliable data by
careful observation, I often caught adders in the South
of England. These certainly wriggle sonie time after
apparent death, or after what ought to be utter annihila-
tion. Popular natural history requires them to wriggle
till sundown. Does this mean till, owing to the want of
light, no further observations are usually made, or is it
the chilling effect. What happens if a snake is killed at
night?
On the Venezuelan coast is a kind of jelly-fish, the
sting of which is said to be felt till the turn of the tide ;
I understand that a similar stinging fish frequents the
East Australian shores. Is there any foundation for the
belief in this peculiarity ? Seems that with luck one may
be condemned to suffer for a few moments, but if stung
just after the turn of tide, for hours.

The Inky Way.
“ Alice H.”: There can be no doubt as to the identity
of the Times writer on “ the Australian attitude.” Un-
doubtedly the hand which penned “ The New Nation ” is
responsible also for the frank common-sense and, as far as
his facts go, accurate presentation of the Australian atti-
tude. A Conservative who is not so hide-bound but that
he can acknowledge other interests than his own and other
ways of thinking ; an Imperialist without being a Jingo,
Percy Rowland has acted the part of the candid friend to
the English public. A thankless task. His performance
will be fiercely resented in some quarters, ana its lessons
as soon as possible forgotten. The article is an epitome
of the political chapters of the book brought up-to-date,
stronger, with more vim and cynicism, but the opinions
are the same. The lilt of the words is reminiscent of an
earlier writer, whom Mr. Rowland too frankly admires,
Francis Adams. Percy Rowland is the nephew of Mrs.
S. A. Barnett, of Toynbee Hall, the East-End worker, who
was, by the way, an ardent “ pro-Boer.”
* * * •
It is questionable whether many Australian journalists
fed to life a newspaper under such difficulties as Q. Labor
Senator Tom Givens, when, in ’97, he started the Cairns
Advocate. The semi-black Argus was there, and old
“ Hoppy” Draper, long-since dead, was running the
established nigger Post. Democrats were few up there,
but Chows were in thousands. “Tom,” who came fresh
from navvying on the Mossman tram-line, went to work
with an old plant, and the Advocate’s maiden issue came
out with an anti-Jap bad-house leader that struck multi-
colored Cairns with the force of a cyclone.
* • • *
" Djin Djln ": Staggered into the office of a Dunedin
(M.L.) daily, recently, a beery individual of about 45 or
thereabouts, wearing lurid whiskers and carrying bluey.
Met him on the Inky Way, heading for the editor’s door.
“’Shay, mister," he said, “ thosh (hie) Go’dam clerks
downstairs don’t know (hie) brainsh when they see
them.” (He had called in at the commercial department
and inquired for the editor, and the collar-and-cuff bri-
gade had consigned him to Gehenna.) “Now, you’re a
likely-looking (hie) 3’oung ’un I’’ I smiled. “You don’t
know who I am, I shposh?” I confessed I did not.
“ Well, y’ mightn’t b'lieve it t’ look ’t me, but I onsh
(hie) edited Shydney Morningsh Heralsh.” Seeing my
surprise he came and breathed on me heavily. “ Thash
maksh ye shtart. Fact, I wash. Wrote leadersh. Lef’
becaush enervating p’lltical (hie) atmoshphere too (hie)
. N’ look here, young’un, where’sh bosh ? ’M hard
up’n want a job. I’ll write y’article on ‘ F’d’rashun.”’
I looked doubtful. He was very dirty and very beery,
but intelligence was writ large on a fine forehead.
Thinking he might one time have trod the primrose (?)
path of the Inky Way, I gave him a bob and told him to
tome back sober with his article on “ Federation.” He
never returned. Passing along Princess-street, a few
days later, I saw a bare red-headed individual wildly
haranguing a casual crowd. Closer inspection revealed
the late editor of the Sydney Morning Herald.
» * * »
Journalist Robert Price, who died in Sydney lately
from throat-affection, edited the Tory Napier Daily
Telegraph (M.L.) for 22 years, but retired ten years
back to take up sheep-farming. A bachelor, club man,
and good fellow. When the radical Evening News was
set going in Napier, the Conservative crowd, including
Price and Knowles, proprietor of the elongated Tele-
graph (irreverently dubbed “ Knowles’ Nightshirt”) came
in for sultry criticism, and Price eventually got £lOO
damages against the News, which had commented on his
unfitness to sit on the licensing bench. The News
generally had two or three libel actions on its hands
those days. Price incurred the wrath of Lottie Wilmot
when she travelled M.L., and a famous street scene
followed.
* * • *
Away back in the ’Forties, when the present Brisbane
Courier was run by a convict, one William Wilks, a
number of prominent squatters started a rival, the
Moreton Bay Free Press, but two issues or so proved
enough, and the first rival of the weary old wobbler died.
* * * »
E F. Allen, formerly leader-writer for the Post (Wel-
lington, M.L.), who recently went to the editorial staff of
Melbourne Argus, replacing “Oriel” Sandes, is a man
who waves a facile pen on most subjects that have been
or are to be. He was for some years in the British diplo-
matic service in China, and has as much knowledge of
the mysteries of the Northern question as any European
can get, for the Chinaman’s face is impassive in front and
his pigtail is secretive behind, and even diplomatists
know little of the real mind of the nation that carries the
two baskets of cabbages on the bamboo. Allen speaks
several languages, is an interesting conversationalist, and
is quite English. Old Gresley Lukin (chief of Post), he
of the heavy humor and the three chins, has been winter-
ing at Rotorua, where the crust is hardly thick enough
to hold up a man of his tonnage. Jack Barr, whose
verses are known to Bulletin readers, has changed over
from the Post to the Times, and Bishop, from the Christ-
church Press, has gone to the Post. Every puff of wind
blows reporters from one paper to another in Wellington,
and there is a gale there every other day,
• • • *
“ Waif ”: An Inky-wailer who reports that an Austra-
lian provincial daily is run with a staff of five reporters,
and the literary wage-sheet amounts to £l6 65., must
have struck a particularly rosy patch. Three years ago I
knew a provincial city, where there were three dailies,
two morning and one evening. On the best of the three
the salary list may have amounted to £l5 6s.—probably
£l4 would be nearer. The seven men and youths on the
other two did not get £l4 among them Nearly
all were steady, and fairly competent. Several
were able men, and two or three were smart,
promising young reporters. Two were married.
One died of work, want and worry, leaving a widow and
orphans hungry. The wealthy proprietor, out of his
bounty, paid a portion of the funeral expenses. When
another left his £2 sub-editorship, his place was taken by
a married man at 30s. None of these seven journalists
in his wildest dreams pictured a possibility of getting £3
a week in that employ. They never had a holiday and
often were on duty from 9 a.m. till 2 a.m. next morning.
It is true that one of these papers was struggling and
could not pay fair screws, but the worst offender was the
richest, and the other never was reckoned an offender at
all. The pay of journalists, by-the-way, is mostly better
on the way-back bi-weeklies (condemned by one “ Jones”)
than on the provincial dailies, and the work, of course, is
lighter. .
Cheeky answer by the D.T.’s snips weekly: “W. W.
Higgins (Warren): Joke* good, but we never accept
them unless they are better than the best of the week’s
crop from England and America." In short, the editor
says he won’t pay for anything unless it is better than
what he can steal.

The Funeral.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the 70 a we wus workin’ far back on a big North
run— -
Bill and me and Scotty Wallace, when dam
sinkin’ waa the fun ;
And a whiter clump of mates in all your life ye’d
never sight—
Scotty was my facial image, and our hearts all
three were light.
Some may relish this dam sinkin’, for the pay is
fairly high,
But when no rain falls to fill ’em the amusement
is dam dry;
And we’d bin for six months graftin’, clear, with-
out a drop o’ rain,
With a roastin’ sun above us, underfoot a red-hot
plain!
Well, our water store had dwindled, and what
only saved our souls
From a skippin’ from our bodies wuz some
wretched water-holes,
Where the liquid was a beauty—from a pictur’
point o* view:
Blue and gold, cerise and crimson, just a splash
of every hue.
But for common bushmen’s bodies this ’ere stuff
was poisoned brew,
And it very soon upset us, but the whisky pulled
us through,
Bill and me, not Scotty Wallace—that old person
never drank—
So he died through drinkin’ that pictorial fluid
from the tank
But before he drew his final, our old mate made
out his will:
And he left his dog to me, and bequeathed his
watch to Bill,
With the solit’ry injunction that we’d take his
body down
And repose it next his mother’s in the tomb at
Dusty Town.
Forty miles it wuz to Dusty, and I almost funked
the work,
But as heir to Scotty’s mongrel thought it would
be base to shirk ;
So we hung aloft a notice to our callers—mostly
crows—
“Gon to burry Skottee Wallus. Back agen—
God only knows."
With Scotty carefully planted in our flour and
tucker chest,
We started on our journey to perform his last
request ;
And the first place that we camped at, a storm
upon us fell
With a hissin’ and a roarin’ like a break-away
from Hell.
How the thunder rolled around us and in torrents
fell the rain,
While like snakes o’ fire the lightnin’ skimmed
about the flooded plain !
My mate Bill was fairly cosy, for his togs wuz
waterproof,
But the rain it drenched me badly and sleep held
from me aloof.
Then an idee straightway struck me—Scotty in
the box wuz dry,
And a corpse, as you might notice, hadn’t feelin’s
same as I,
So I quietly arises, lifts im out, and lays ’im down,
And inside, myself I settles, murmurin’, “Scotty,
yer done brown.”
As I woke the morn was breakin’, I sat up and
looked around,
When a wild yell came from Willum as he started
from the ground,
With his eyes a-glarin' at me, and his face a
deadly hue ;
“ Scotty’s ghost, by G —d !” he hollered, and
away he madly flew.
I called him back ; ’twuz useless —Bill seemed
fairly off his chump ;
To each call he only answered with an extree
powerful jump.
When I found ’im he wuz lyin' on some stones a
mile away,
With a hole knocked in his forehead where he
wears the scar to-day.
Back to camp I humped Bill sadly ; Bill, ’owever,
soon came round,
But another trouble faced me, for, to my disgust,
I found
That our funeral steed ’ad bolted and ’ad gone
clean out o’ sight—
’Ad bin frightened very likely by the thunder
through the night.
I wus in a difficulty with a corpse and damaged
mate,
And still twenty miles from Dusty, so I ’greed
without debatb
To convey the dead no further, and with many a
bloomin’ sigh
I reposed him ’neath some boulders in a mulga
scrub close by.
Now ye know the simple story, but I’d hev ye
understand
We’ve both sworn with what vocabulary we had
at command — ft
That some day we’ll go up North-ways, get is
bones and bring ’em down,
And we’ll plant them next ’is mother’s in the
tomb at Dusty Town!

“ 5.0’8.” on an affliction of the back-
blocks :
The back-country people of the far North are
being continually taken-town by gangs of alleged
mummers. They arrive with a small portmanteau-ful of
scenery, an orchestra of one indifferent piano-thumper
and a day-bill, giving the names of about 25 performers
(often parodies on well-known artists’ stage-names). '1 he
five acts are described, and a synopsis of the 18 scenes
given. Well, you go, and find that the “ scenery f con-
sists mostly of the stock rags of the local hall or theatre.
The orchestra reveals its oneness and badness, the 25
stars dwindle to about half-a-dozen tallow-dips, and
parts are doubled and trebled. The hero denounces the
villain, and then rushes off and returns as a policeman
to arrest the v. Meanwhile the heroine is off, and
returns as the “bad lot” and shoots the police-
man, who staggers off, but returns in - time tci be
denounced as the real murderer of the heroines father,
said father having been the villain afoies.nfh
Still worse is the variety show. The galaxy of talent
(vide bills) consists mostly of respectable old men.and
women whom I heard as middle-aged persons 20 years
ago, the balance being made up of semi and complete
failures of from 60 to 16 years of age. A second-hand
flicker-graph and some ancient wheeze of a farce com-
plete the show. Decent shows do make money in the
{jack country, but “ take-downs” only disgust playgoers,
and spoil the chances of decent performers.

Santa Cruzoes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thb tidal wave of Christianity and civilisation
that has swept over the other islands of the
Pacific, and washed away so much of the old
picturesque life of the natives, has hardly touched
the Santa Cruz group. The inhabitants of these
sequestered isles have learnt to smoke, and to set
some value on butcher-knives and calico, but
otherwise they are the same naked, unashamed
heathens their forefathers were over three cen-
turies ago when Don Alvaro Mendana de Meyra
brought his colony of sailor Spaniards to
settle amongst them. In 1567 this gallant adven-
turer, who was then hunting for King Solomon’s
mines, believed that he had discovered their situa-
tion in the group he triumphantly named Islas
de Salomon. He returned to Peru and spent the
rest of his life in getting together an expedition to
form a colony in his imaginary El Dorado. With
four ships containing 400 souls he sailed away,
and at length sighted Santa Cruz, which he took
to be one of the Solomons. He cast anchor in a
harbor which he called Graciosa Bay (what a
genius for giving musical and pious and sonorous
names to places the Spaniards had !), and left his
bones there. Most of his company left their
bones there also, and the broken-spirited survi-
vors made their way back to Peru in two of the
vessels, the other two having been lost.
* * * * *
Graciosa Bay deserves its name. It is spacious,
and reasonably deep, and has softly-curving
shores. Here and there you see a strip of beach,
but otherwise there is nothing visible but wet,
steaming, green forest. The trees grow together
in such a twisted, tangled mass that one imagines
that if some passing giant of Polynesian fable
were to take the tops of them and pull, he would
drag the island up by the roots and leave nothing
but a muddy hole in the ocean to mark where it
had been. These islands of the Western Pacific
look like enormous green sponges. Santa Cruz—l
am speaking, now of the principal island in the
group of that name—is even more sponge-like
than others I have seen. Unless you land on one
of the strips of beach referred to and search care-
fully beneath the jungle of vegetation you will
not see enough clear ground upon which to play a
game of pitch-and-toss. And if you wander many
yards into this soggy jungle you will probably
step upon the fallen trunk of a tree, and sink to
your waist in black rottenness. The very villages
seem more like gigantic fungoid growths than
habitations of even the meauest of mankind.
Imagine even the indomitable Spaniards of that
sanguine period (when this great Australian con-
tinent, now overrun by poets and eye-glassed
politicians, was resting in primeval peace) coming
to such a place in the hope of resuming work in
gold m nes left untouched since the days of Solo-
mon ! Gazing back through the mist of years, I
seemed to behold once more the stout-hearted old
hidalgo and his valiant companions with draggled
plumes, and rusty helmets, and what were once
fine steel waistcoats looking like samples from a
mediaeval marine store, tramping wearily around
the shores of this accursed island, and pausing
occasionally to shootdown eocoanuts with weapons
intended for nobler uses. There is a fine subject
here for a writer of romance with some gift of
pathos.
* « • •
Not many vessels call at Santa Cruz. There is
little there to attract them. Even in the blaek-
birding days it was not, after a few trials, con-
sidered of any account as a recruiting ground.
The natives who were taken away to Queensland
invariably died of home-sickness. They missed
their sweet, spongy jungles. Some of them may,
however, be seen in the other islands. They are
a weak and unwholesome-looking race with light
skins, scaly with scrofula. But there is nothing
shy about them. We were not anchored an hour
before they were swarming all over the steamer.
They had never seen a crowd of excursionists
before, but they knew, just as well as if they had
been born in Switzerland, what to make of the
opportunity. They had nothing worth speaking
of for sale but redwood bows and poisoned arrows.
These arrows are elegant weapons to look at.
They are long slender reeds, unfeathered and
tipped at one end with a human bone, ground at
the point to the sharpness of a needle. Just
below the bone the shaft is carved in chaste, con-
ventional patterns. A man shot with one of these
has time to settle his affairs. The poison takes
from seven to ten days to do its work. Then
tetanus ensues. Commodore Goodenough was
wounded by an arrow of this sort on the 12th of
August, 1875, and died eight days afterwards.
The points of the arrows are supposed to be
dipped in animal putrefaction to make sure of
their deadliness. I have heard of no antidote.
Sheaves of these dreadful weapons were bought
by the excursionists. Those who bought most
Were mild-mannered men who looked as if they
Would not hurt a fly, even if it were on the butter.
But you never can tell.
o • • •
There was an exceptionally scaly person on
board who carried in lus hand a lavalava, made of
liative cloth, with fringes and tassels hanging
from it at intervals. Fabulous prices were offered
for. it. One reckless excursionist bid as high as
two butcher-knives and ten sticks of tobacco, but
the Santa Cruzoe shook his lime-bleached head.
He took the coveted article of raiment away with
him. He had evidently brought it merely as an
Exhibit of what could be produced in Santa Cruz
yan artist to whom time was no object. It cer-
tainly was the most artistic thing I saw there,
outside of,,the arrows. Breastplates and nose-
tings, and earrings could be purchased, but the
wearers were not anxious to sell them. I spoke
to one native about an article I had in my own
private eve.
“Cah-ii-co.” he sighed, rather than said.
I offered him many sticks of tobacco.
He smiled a sweet sanguinary smile—his mouth
was red with betel-nut—and gently raised his eye-
brows. , .
“Cah-li-co,” he sighed again.
I could get no other word out of him.
* • • •>
There was one merchant amongst them who
made a strange impression upon me. I could
have sworn that I had seen him, clothed and with
complexion a trifle lighter, standing outside a
second-hand-shop in Sydney. His name was then
Isaacs, or Aarons, or Cohen—probably Cohen.
The likeness was grotesquely real. This Cohen of
Santa Cruz had a bald head, and a thick nose of
the coarser Jewish type. I observed that his
nose-ring was finer and larger than the rings worn
by any of the others. He was also the fattest
man in the company. He sat on the main-hatch
and chewed betel-nut, while three or four younger
men, who appeared to be in his employment,
went around the deck offering curios for sale.
Every now and then they would come to him,
apparently to report some offer that had been
made to them. Sometimes he seemed to be dis-
pleased, and I would hardly have been surprised
to hea,r him say, “Ikey, ma tear, do ye vant
to ruin me ? ” But he merely dropped his
eyelids and kept on chewing in scornful silence.
Afterwards, when I was in the Solomons, I saw
quite a number of natives with this Jewish cast of
countenance. Probably Mendana made a similar
observation. He had evidently more than mere
visionary reasons for the idea to which he sacri-
ficed his life. These Hebraic-looking natives may
oonceivably be descendants of miners sent out by
Solomon. There may have been more than one
Ophir. Anyhow, there is one striking fact in
favor of Mendana’s conviction —the natives in
question are all plump and prosperous in appear-
ance, and seem to work only by proxy. If they
are not long-lost Jews, what are they?
* * • «
I met at Santa Cruz a trader with pale face,
long black hair, dark eyes, and careless-growing
black beard. He wore a slouch hat, and was
barefoot, and somehow made me think that
Tennyson would have cut just such a figure if he
had gone to the “ summer isles of Eden lying in
dark purple spheres of sea,” instead of singing
about them. The trader had taken a savage
woman to rear his dusky brood. She was dressed
in an electric blue wrapper, and would have been
almost pretty if she had not worn a piece of wood
like a large safety-match inserted in a hole bored
in the top of her nose. Tennyson told me that
when the natives are at war they always aim their
arrows at the legs of their enemies. If the dead
bone on the point of the arrow sticks into the live
bone in the man he will die, because the arrow
cannot be extracted without being broken. If it
sticks into a fleshy part of the body it can be cut
out, and the man will recover. Fortunately the
Santa Cruzoes are not William Tells in the way
of archery.
* * * *
They have some rude ideas of fortification.
Their villages are surrounded by stone walls
about 4ft. high. They discharge their arrows
from behind these when they are not strong
enough to meet their enemies in the open. They
have also a curious chivalry of their own in con-
nection with the conduct of war. Tennyson told
me about it. A young man belonging to a certain
coastal village stole away a woman belonging to
another coastal village. She appeared to be a
willing captive, but that did not count. The in-
habitants of her village sent two heralds in a
canoe to demand that the young man should be
handed over to them to be formally killed accord-
ing to custom. His friends replied that he was
away with the trader in the cutter, which was
true. The heralds then requested that Helen
should be given to them for the same fate, as
Paris was not procurable. The Trojans refused
to grant the request; not because they did not
rocognise the justice of it, but because the absent
Paris had left no instructions. The heralds then
politely declared war. The official enemy there-
upon took them to the village Club House and
feasted them, and afterwards sent them away
with many expressions of esteem. Next day, or
the day afterwards, a fleet of outriggers with
platforms heaped with arrows came from the hos-
tile village. One arrow in each canoe was kept
apart, from the others. It was unpoisoned. The
etiquette was to shoot off these arrows first —as a
sort of graceful parade before the serious business
of the duel began. The party attacked re-
turned the courtesy. Then the battle joined
in earnest. In this particular fight one
of the defenders wa3 hit and fell. His
party at once acknowledged themselves defeated.
When a man is struck in one of these fights, and
falls, it is the rule to assume that he is dead.
That ends the conflict. It is only in very san-
guinary battles that more than one is slain on
either side. The vanquished took the victors to the
Club House and entertained them at a banquet.
Afterwards they paid them a crushing indemnity
in shell-money—the viotors scornfully looking on
and hurling insulting epithets at their fallen
enemies. All this is done in strict accordance
with immemorial usage. After the payment was
made the conquerors, made rich beyond the
dreams of avarice, started for home, and the war
was over—for the time. The defeated party
would, of course, seize the first opportunity of
revenge.
• * • •
Most of the wars are over women. You
would not think it to look at them, but the Santa
Cruzoes are a gallant and amorous race. They
load their ears with ornaments in the shape of
blocks of wood, clam-shell rings, bones, or any-
thing handy and heavy, to such an extent that the
said ears hang down their shoulders in loops of
gristle. This is done with the idea of fascinating
the females of their kind. I saw a few of the
latter. They were less ugly, but, I should think,
a little more scaly than the men.
* * * •
There is only one trader in the Santa Cruz
group, and he is not rapidly amassing affluence.
The Santa Cruzoes gather just enough copra to
buy them pipes and tobacco and knives and a few
fathoms of calico. All island natives are lazy,
but these of the Holy Cross are laziest. Even the
missionaries have practically given them up. So
that there still remains a little dark corner of the
earth, set apart for the amusement of excursionists
and the edification of ethnologists.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
“Tot”: Recently drove with a friend
through a sparsely inhabited portion of South
Gippsland (Vic.). Struck the saddest and loneliest
pub I ever saw. The landlord received us with the
almost tearful delight that might have been looked
for if we had dropped in on Robinson Crusoe at
Juan Fernandez. Probably he had not seen a
human creature from outside for a fortnight. In
addition to the whiskies we ordered he brought
US; unasked, cups of tea, and fairly chortled in his
joy during the half-hour we remained. His brim-
ming happiness seemed to me so pathetic that,
with a few more whiskies on board, I should have
wept. He was a fine-looking man of 40 years
■ —clever, and had travelled. What scurvy trick
of fortune could have brought this individual,
obviously capable of doing anything, to such a
pass ? He was clearly not a liquor-loving person.
Another man on the ordeal by expectora-
tion :
Re school children of the backbloeks. I can tell of
another habit which seems to be a sort of survival of the
old-time “ trial by ordeal.” Challenged as to truthful-
ness, the child makes a ring- of thu”'b and first finger and
spits through. If he misses his guilt is held proved. If
he succeeds his claim is admitted. This is accepted as
quite final by children of my acquaintance. One of less
importance, the common one, is striking the breast and
saying, “True, true, holy water.” Yet another method
is to wet the finger and say quite solemnly, “ That’s wet,
and that is dry,” wiping finger on sleeve, “God cut
my throat if I should tell a lie.” Striking the breast
seems indispensable to them all. Why ? Presumably we
used to do these things ourselves, but that doesn’t help
in the pursuit of the whyness of them.
Eighty-odd persons who went to Maori-
land from Australia as members of the Austra-
lasian Association for the Advancement of Science,
by paying J6l each, obtained a month’s free
travelling on the M.L. railways. The congress
has been proceeding in Dunedin, while those
persons have been admiring the belles of Rotorua
and elsewhere. Railway Minister Ward later on
vnred to all stationmasters to block the trippers
and make them pay full fare.

The Two Ways.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I dreamed the hazy, flower-strewn track
I careless trod in boyhood’s days,
Had reached a point where, at noon’s birth,
The road took on two different ways;
And one path led towards the stars,
O’er stony ways with thorns bestrewn ;
The other down through pleasing glades
And mellow aisles of afternoon.
Before the uphill brambled path
There stood a figure coldly fair,
Whose stirring tones had power to make
The doubter sure, the coward dare.
“My ways are rough ; no easy fruits
Are mine to win, oft bitter lees,
But soaring dreams and lofty hopes
Are ne’er fulfilled in careless ease :
'* Who craves the rose must brave the thorn,
The sweat drops dew the wreath of Fame ;
But sweeter far the hard-won bays
Than easy fruits that pall and tame.”
I turned to tread Ambition’s path
With firmer step, in gladsome choice,
When from the path that sought the shades
There welled a softer, sweeter voice ;
Where stood a maid with hot desire
Enthroned in eyes, whose fevered light
Birthed grosser dreams that hid the goal
Forever from my glamored sight.
“My path,” she oried, “slopes d >wn cool dales
Alight with love, bedecked with smiles,
Where easy joys and yielding lips
Are sharpest thorns and hardest trials.
“ Leave grief and disappointed hopes
To those sad days the years beget.
When drained or spilt, the wine of life
Has sunk to lees of vain regret;
When rounded arms no longer cling,
And lips have lost their old-time drouth . . .”
I turned me from the path of dreams,
And kissed her hotly on the mouth.
The kiss has lost its sweetness long,
The easy grades grow sheer and steep;
Yet, all paths lead where unsung song
With dreams, and hopes, and right and wrong
Are stilled for aye in dreamless sleep.
VIC.
J. McCLELLAND.
The popular belief, during the recent
droughty years, was that the abnormal nature of
the season was the cause of the typhoid epidemics
in country towns. Just now, however, many of
the country hospitals cannot hold the patients.
Dr. Horsfall told the Hamilton (N.S.W.) council
that this summer, although one of the mildest on
record, was the worst for illness he had ever
known. The diarrhoea, dysentery, and typhoid
cases were, he declared, due to the decomposition
of vegetable matter.
A Prisoner of the Bush.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Poets rave about the “rose-leaf flush” and the
“glowing dawn but to me these things are but
the signal to get up and struggle through another
long, hot day.
Sleep has been an impossibility, and we wearily
crawl in from the verandah to the mosquito-
infested rooms, and wash in brown, tepid water
that leaves a thick scum in the basin. I call to
the boy to get up and milk, and then my husband
yells to me to bring in the nets and hang wet bags
over the door and window while he turns over for
another drowse. I drag myself out to light the
fire and get breakfast, but the flies have got into
the meat-bag and the meat is only fit to throw to
the dogs. As I fish for the salt mutton in the
brine-cask a man rides up and says, “ Mornin’;
boss at ’ome ?”
“ If you will wait I will go and tell him,” I say.
“ He is busy just now.”
“What the blazes did you wake me for?” he
growls. “ I’d just got to sleep.”
“A man outside wants you.”
“ Well, let him wait. What does he want?”
“I didn’t ask.”
“ Why the devil didn’t you, then, before you
woke me up ? Go and ask what the blazes he does
want.”
I return to the man, who is reclining on his
horse, catching flies in his eyes and gazing at them
thoughtfully. Then I go back to my husband.
“ He says he wants to see you about buying some
ration-sheep. ”
“Why didn’t you say so straight away?” he
snaps, searching for his trousers.
When breakfast is over, and “the boss,” after
growling at everything, rides away, I heave a
sigh of relief as I struggle out with the full pig-
bucket, wondering, the while, how it is that,
though my husband considers it beneath his
dignity to saddle his own horse, he allows his wife
to do work a cook would turn over to his slushy.
Already the thermometer stands at 103°, and
down the dusty road a whirlwind slowly moves,
turning statelily like a figure in a minuet. Far
away against the throbbing horizon a black speck
appears. It is the mail-coach, whose weekly
arrival is the only excitement in my brown life.
As it draws nearer I run down to the slip-rails to
say “Yes” to the driver’s greeting, “Bit’ot, ain’t
it ?”
Later on my husband returns, bawls out to
know if dinner will ever be ready, and tells me to
mix him a whisky—three fingers—and to be sure
and use water-bag water in it. Then he subsides
on a lounge to read the mail, and I trail back to
the smoky kitchen.
At dinner my husband growls again about the
mutton being boiled to rags, and the squash not
boiled enough ; and while I am washing-up I
hear him ordering—“ Jack, get up the horses,
and wash the buggy wheels ; I’m goin’ into town
this evening.” Then he comes out to the kitchen
and asks peevishly : “ What are you stuck in this
roasting shop for ? It’s cooler inside. Just pack
my brown bag, will you ; I have to go down to
Sydney for a few days to see the bank.”
Of course I know the bank he is going to
see is on the Cricket Ground ; and as I scrub
the table, my mind’s eye sees the crowds of gaily
dressed, smiling women and the crowded tea-
rooms; and I hear the band playing and the
tram-bells shrilling in the distance. I think ot
the flower-vendors in Pitt-street with their sweet-
scented wares; of the harbor with the brilliantly-
lighted boats sliding over its surface, and a score
of other tilings that are cool, and lightsome, and
pleasant, and
“Are you ever going to come and pack my
things?”
I hurry in ; remembering, as I look sadly over
the brown plains, that these things are not for me.
I must live and die a Prisoner of the Bush !
WENONA.
A Question of Damages.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A hairy, active man rushed into the office of the
man of law, followed by a clean-faced and more
sedate young gentleman of the city.
“Are you the boss?’’ said the leader of the
expedition.
“I am the principal of this establishment,”
replied the stout person in the chair.
“ Well, I suppose you know your business, don’t
you ? ”
“Well, yes, I think I am acquainted with the
rudiments of the law of this ”■——
“That’s all right. I want damages. This
cove’s my witness.”
“ Yes—quite right,” said the lawyer, brighten-
ing; “ who has done you wrong ? ”
“This is the case of me versus the Melbourne
Tramway and Omnibus Company Limited.’’
“I see, I see,” said the lawyer, getting very
bright—“ breach of contract.”
“No! C’lission. ”
“Dear, dear! A collision. You interest me.
Let me take a note of the circumstances.”
“ There wasn't no circumstances. I came here
a stranger, from out Bonrke way. Only lit here
yesterday, and a darned sight too soon at that. A
visitor I am.”
“Most inhospitable,” said the lawyer. “Just
tell me how it happened.”
“ Right ! I was drivin’ along that road out
there ”
“Flinders-street,” said the witness.
“Yes. And one of them there tram-cars come
at the back o’ me.”
“ Aha ! Took you unawares. Gross negligence
on the part of the company’s officers. ’
“Unawares! I don’t think. I saw it cornin’
right enough, an’ I put on the whip and tried to
get out of the way.”
“Good! That negatives contributory negli-
gence. Proceed.”
“Proceed? Yes, that’s the word. I did pro-
ceed ; but the mare got away badly. She wasn’t
used to them there flooring-boards and shied.
And the tram kep’ straight after me, wobblin’ its
bloomin’ bullick bell, tryin’ to frighten me like.”
“More than a suggestion of malice,” said the
solicitor, and added, “You had no time to get off
the track.”
“That’s just where they thought they had me.
Reckoned I was a greenhorn. You don’t under-
stand drivin’. If I’d a turned it would have been
on me in a minnit. No, I didn’t lose my head ;
kep’ straight on, stan’s to reason; it wasn’t a time
for dodgin’.”

BOLEG.
The Argus concludes a discussion of the
sweated-clerk question:
If there are clerks with dormant consciences and un-
developed energies, so also there are employers who
glory in getting their work cheaply done, regardless of
the damnation, temporal or eternal, they bring on the
miserable creatures who plod on from year to 3’ear doing
mechanical duty for them. For much of the evil in the
world there seems to be no cure possible but a religious
and social awakening. When it comes, it will have to be
soul-stirring, for some of the greatest “sweaters” of to-
day are so-called “ religious firms.”
Which is fine logic. Some of the worst sweaters
are religious firms : therefore, the cure for sweat-
ing is religion.

_ ALL COMJtfUNTCATIONS CONFIDENTIAL. (Please mention this paper when writing.)
“D.”: Australian cities are placarded with
signs of dressmakers, tailors, &c., whose plain name of
Brown or Jones is propped up with the announcement,
“ Late with Kobinson & Co.” Robinson & Co. having a
good reputation, people think the announcement is a
sufficient guarantee of Brown’s or Jones’s ability and act
accordingly. In many cases the suit or skirt is lament-
ably botched, and if the person who gave the order makes
diligent inquiry, he may find that Brown or Jones was
in Robinson’s employ for a week or fortnight shifting
packing-cases. The detriment to Robinson’s business,
does not concern me, but X hate to see my fellovv-crea*
tures in ill-fitting clothes. Not that I care a darn for my
fellow-creatures, but I don’t want their clothes to blot
the landscape while I ain using the landscape. Moreover,
I don’t want to be disturbed by rows. A Brown, who
was with Poole and Co.. London, has a shop near me,
and I heard one of his customers remonstrating with
Brown a few nights back, and his remarks about Poole
and Co. for turning out such a tradesman were terrible.
I don’t mind if the customer's clothes were a misfit, and
I don’t care if Brown and Poole and Co. are denounced
till the devil eats grass, but when Brown’s conduct
causes Jones or Smith to curse Poole just when I want to
be quiet, the thing is an outrage.
“ Deeby ” : Very few people have any idea how
much cursing often goes to the making of a coffin. I
once boarded with the undertaker of a Western town.
We had just sat down to tea, and the planter was
narrating how he once had to use a long-handled shovel
to get a “ Government job ” into its case, when an urgent
order arrived for a cheap, ordinary coffin. The pious-
looking undertaker received the intimation with a
beautiful expression of chastened grief. Tea over I
went, at the undertaker’s invitation, to see him make
a “ eorfin.” He soon got to work cutting up the
necessary planks (he was out of “slops” he told me),
but the saw was dull, or wanted setting or something,
a fact which raised the “planter’s” bile, for he broke
into dreadful language. Then in attempting to nail the
brittle pine hoards together, without previous boring,
they split, which gave rise to more blasphemy. But the
most terrific thing occurred when the artist decided that
the side pieces were not properly bent. Boiling water,
from a wash-tub close at hand, was applied to assist the
bending process. It was proceeding satisfactorily, and
the undertaker had just sat on a board to weigh it down,
when it broke with a loud report, and a frantic under-
taker was scrambling out of a tub of hot water. The
language that followed was something shameful ; even
the departed one was included in the curse. He decided
that certain hoards he had on hand would “ dashed well
have to do.” About 101 b. of melted pitch was used
to botch,up the large cracks and holes in the “box.”
Then Mrs. Undertaker, assisted by her daughter, started
the upholstering of the interior, while her husband tacked
some gorgeous tinsel on the black covered lid. “Ain’t
that pwetty ?” the undertaker presently exclaimed with
pride. I agreed that it was, for the coffin was now
finished, and with its gilt ferns, angels, and handles was
a work of art fit for an alderman. Then the casket was
gently lifted from the trestles for removal to an adjoining
room. And right here occurred the disaster of the
evening, for the undertaker, colliding with a box pro-
jecting from a dark corner, caused his wife to let go her
end of the coffin, which fell to the ground with a hollow
bang. When raised it wag found that the screws and
nails holding the sides to the head piece had given away.
Then the undertaker started to yell “ Well, strike me
”; but I made off.
